Causality and Created Things
intermediate
5 min
The Objection of Universal Will and the Role of Imagination Habit
Al-Jurjani addresses an objection and defends the necessity of partial imaginations through the concept of habitude.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the objection that the will is related to the whole motion and the rebuttal that particular imaginations are needed, becoming a habit.

Core Idea
Particular imaginations become a habit, making us unaware of their role.

After establishing that particular conceptions and wills are necessary, Al-Jurjani presents an objection: the will seems to be related to the whole motion at once. He responds by explaining that we do have particular imaginations, but they become a habit (malaka), so we don't notice them. Evidence is that cutting off imagination stops motion.

Guided Path
1
Read the objection
Read the objection: if the will is related to the entire motion, why would we need partial imaginations at each step?
2
Understand the answer
Al-Jurjani says that the imagination of the particular does not require external existence; it can be internal. He then argues that these imaginations become a habit, so we are not conscious of them.
This is a key psychological point.
3
Examine the evidence
The proof is that if imagination and will are cut off mid-motion, the motion stops. This shows they are necessary.
4
Reflect on the habitude (malaka)
Consider how skills become automatic: we don't think of each step when walking. Similarly, the particular imaginations become ingrained.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
المرصد الرابع - المقصد الرابع : الحركة الطبيعية ليست المكان لا إرادياً موقوفاً على الشعور والإرادة وكذا نقول في الكيفيات والكميات وملائمة بعضها لبعض الأجسام، ( ويعلم من ذلك) الذي مر ذكره في الحركة الطبيعية ( أن العلة للحركة الإرادية ليست هي النفس لثباتها وعدم اختلافها) يعني أن النفس ثابتة مستمرة فلا تكون وحدها علة للحركة التي هي متجددة غير ثابتة، والنفس غير مختلفة في نوع واحد من الأجسام ذوات الأنفس مع اختلاف الحركة الإرادية في ذلك النوع بل في فرد منه ) ولا أيضاً هي التصور الكلي ) الحاصل للنفس ) لأن نسبته إلى الحركات الجزئية سواء) وكذا الإرادة الناشئة من التصور الكلي لا تكون إلا كلية متساوية النسبة إلى جزئيات الحركة فلا يصدر شيء من تلك الجزئيات عن النفس مع تصورها وإرادتها الكليين بل إنما هي) أي علة الحركة الإرادية تصورات جزئية يترتب عليها إرادة جزئية ) فالماشي نحو بغداد له في كل خطوة إرادة جزئية تابعة لتصور جزئي ( قالوا : إن الحركة الاختيارية إلى مكان تتبع الإرادة متعلقة بمجموع تلك الحركة ثم إن المسافة التي لتلك الحركة يمكن أن يفرض فيها حدود جزئية تتجزأ بها المسافة إلى أجزائها الجزئية فالمتحرك يحتاج بعد قوله : ( تصورات جزئية وحصول تلك التصورات في الخيال لا يتوقف على وجود تلك الحركات في الخارج حتى يلزم الدور، فإنا نتخيل صوراً لا وجود لها في الخارج على ما تقرر عندهم من أن الصور الخيالية قد تجيء من خارج وقد تجيء من داخل كما في المقامات . قوله : ( فالمتحرك إلخ) وما أورد عليه من أن هذا خلاف الوجدان فإذا حاولنا أن نتحرك في مسافة معينة تصورنا تلك المسافة والحركة على الاحتمال وأما تصور كل جزء من أجزاء المسافة والحركة الواقعة فيه فكلا وجوابه : إنا لا ننكر تصور الحركة المطلقة لكن دل البرهان على أن ذلك التصور والإرادة المعينة عنه لا يكفي في صدور حركة معينة واقعة في جزء من المسافة، بل لا بد من تخيل جزئي لذلك وعدم وجداننا لذلك بواسطة أنه صار تلك التخيل والإرادة الجزئية ملكة للنفس، يدل على ذلك أنه لو انقطع التخيل والإرادة في أثناء الحركة تنقطع الحركة، وتفصيل هذا المطلب يطلب من شرح الإشارات والمحاكمات . قوله : (بل) إنما هي تصويرات جزئية فيه بحث لأن إدراك الجزئي من حيث الجزئية والتشخص لا يمكن إلا بالحواس الظاهر وإدراك الحواس موقوف على وجود المحسوس، فإن المعدوم لا يحس فتصور العقل الجزئي من حيث هو جزئي موقوف على وجوده فلو توقف وجوده على العلم به من هذه الحيثية كان دوراً فالحق أن تصور أفراد الكلي والقصد إليها على الإجمال كافيان في صدورها عن المختار . قوله : ( فالمتحرك يحتاج إلخ) قيل : هذا مما يكذبه الوجدان عند الإنصاف فإنا إذا أردنا أن نتحرك في مسافة معينة تصورنا تلك المسافة والحركة فيها على الإجمال، وأما تصور كل جزء من أجزاء المسافة بخصوصه والحركة الواقعة فكلا، فالحق أن الحركة من المبدأ إلى المنتهى أمر واحد بسيط لا انقسام فيها أصلاً، فيكفي في صدورها تخيل المسافة بأسرها إجمالاً وإرادة الحركة عليها ولا حاجة إلى تخيل الحدود المفروضة عليها وتوجه القصد إليها بخصوصها، إذ ليس هناك حركات متعددة بل حركة واحدة جزئية وإن وقع في أثناء الحركة تخيلات وإرادات لبعض الأجزاء، فتلك الأسباب أجزاء ثابتة واقعة في تلك الأوقات لا لاحتياج الحركة إليها . الإرادة المتعلقة بمجموع الحركة إلى أن يتخيل حداً معيناً وتنبعث عنه إرادة جزئية متعلقة بقطع ذلك الجزء من المسافة الذي انفصل بذلك الحد، وهكذا تتوالى التخيلات المستتبعة للإرادة والحركة فتتصل الإرادات في النفس والحركات في المسافة، ولو فرض انقطاع التخيل انقطعت الإرادة والحركة وأما علة الحركة القسرية فهي القوة التي أحدثها القاسر في المتحرك .
The fourth observatory – the fourth objective: Natural motion is not involuntary place [change] dependent on sensation and will; similarly we say regarding qualities, quantities, and the suitability of bodies for each other. And it is known from that which was mentioned concerning natural motion that the cause of voluntary motion is not the soul due to its constancy and lack of change – meaning that the soul is constant and continuous, so it alone cannot be the cause of motion which is renewing and non-constant, and the soul does not differ in one type of ensouled bodies while voluntary motion differs in that type, even in an individual of it. Nor is it the universal conception (al-taṣawwur al-kullī) obtained by the soul, because its relation to particular motions is uniform; likewise the will arising from the universal conception is only universal, equally related to the particulars of motion, so none of those particulars issue from the soul with its universal conception and will. Rather it [i.e., the cause of voluntary motion] is particular conceptions (taṣawwurāt juz'iyya) upon which follow particular wills (irādāt juz'iyya). So the one walking towards Baghdad has at every step a particular will dependent on a particular conception. They said: 'Voluntary motion towards a place follows the will related to the whole of that motion; then the distance of that motion can be supposed to have particular limits by which the distance is divided into its particular parts, so the mover needs after...' His saying: 'Particular conceptions' – and the obtaining of those conceptions in the imagination does not depend on the existence of those motions in the external world such that circularity would follow; for we imagine forms that have no existence in the external world, as established among them that imaginary forms may come from outside and may come from inside, as in the stations. His saying: 'So the mover, etc.' – and the objection raised against it that this contradicts intuition (al-wijdān), for if we try to move in a given distance we conceive that distance and the motion in summary, but as for conceiving every part of the distance and the motion occurring in it – no. Its answer: We do not deny the conception of absolute motion, but demonstration indicates that that conception and the determinate will from it are insufficient for the issuance of a determinate motion occurring in a part of the distance; rather a particular imagination is necessary for that. And our not finding that is because that imagination and particular will have become a habitude (malaka) of the soul. Evidence for that is that if the imagination and will are cut off during motion, the motion ceases. The detail of this topic is sought from Sharḥ al-Ishārāt and al-Muḥākamāt. His saying: 'Rather they are particular images' – there is discussion in it because perception of the particular in terms of particularity and individuation is only possible through the external senses, and perception of the senses depends on the existence of the sensed, for the nonexistent is not sensed. So the intellect's conception of the particular as particular depends on its existence; if its existence depended on knowledge of it from this aspect, circularity would follow. So the truth is that conceiving the individuals of the universal and intending them summarily are sufficient for their issuance from the free agent. His saying: 'So the mover needs, etc.' – It is said: This is contradicted by intuition when being fair, for when we want to move in a given distance we conceive that distance and the motion in it summarily; but as for conceiving every part of the distance specifically and the motion occurring – no. So the truth is that motion from the beginning to the end is one simple thing, having no division at all. So for its issuance, it suffices to imagine the whole distance summarily and to will motion upon it, and there is no need to imagine the supposed limits upon it and to direct the intention towards them specifically, for there are not multiple motions but one particular motion; and if during the motion there occur imaginations and wills for some parts, those are constant parts occurring at those times, not because motion needs them. The will related to the whole of motion until he imagines a particular limit and from it emanates a particular will related to traversing that part of the distance separated by that limit, and so on successive imaginations follow bringing will and motion, so wills in the soul and motions in the distance are connected; and if the cessation of imagination is supposed, will and motion cease. And as for the cause of forced motion, it is the force which the coercer produces in the moved body.
Source pages p. 237

Commentary
This passage is a multi-part argument about the true cause of voluntary motion. It begins by stating that the cause is not the soul, because the soul is constant while motion is constantly renewing; a constant cause cannot produce a non-constant effect. Nor is it the universal conception, since a universal is equally related to all particulars and cannot discriminate among them. The cause must be particular conceptions and particular wills, as illustrated by the example of a walker to Baghdad who has a new will at each step. An objection follows: if motion is willed as a whole, why is partial imagination needed? The answer is that imagination of the partial does not require external existence (it can be internal). Another objection from intuition: we seem to conceive motion summarily, not in parts. The answer explains that the particular imagination becomes a habit (malaka), and proof shows it is necessary; otherwise motion would cease if interrupted. A further discussion argues that the intellect's grasp of the particular as particular depends on sense perception, which requires existence, leading to a circularity concern. The resolution is that conceiving universal individuals summarily suffices. A final objection states that intuition suggests motion is a simple whole, not requiring partial imaginations. The answer affirms that motion from start to end is indeed one simple thing, and the partial imaginations that occur are incidental, not required. The passage concludes with a concise summary of the process of successive imaginations and wills and a note on forced motion caused by an external coercer.

Key Terms
إرادة كلية
ملكة
تخيل جزئي
حركة إرادية

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: If the will is for the whole motion, then no partial decisions are needed.
Correction: The passage shows that partial imaginations are necessary but become habitual, and cutting them stops motion.

Checkpoint
According to the passage, why do we not notice the particular imaginations during motion?
A) They are too fast
B) They become a habit (malaka)
C) They are unconscious
D) They are not real

Key Takeaway
Even though we are unaware, particular imaginations are necessary for motion; they become a habit.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
This passage reveals that the particular imaginations driving your actions have become a habit you do not feel. What daily decisions (e.g., words you speak, paths you walk) might be powered by hidden imaginations you never examine? Will you intentionally pause one action today to uncover the mental images behind it?
?
The objection relied on intuition (wijdān) to deny the need for partial imaginations, but demonstration (burhān) proved otherwise. Where in your life do you trust your immediate feelings over rational evidence about your own soul? Are you ready to reject that comfort and accept what proof demands of you?
?
Al-Jurjani says that if imagination cuts off, motion stops. Consider a time you froze or failed to act. Was it because your habitual imagination failed? What does that reveal about your dependence on these hidden habits? How will you guard your thoughts to ensure the right habits are formed?

Causality and Created Things
intermediate
5 min
Resolving the Circularity of Particular Perception
Al-Jurjani addresses and resolves a circularity problem regarding perception of particulars.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the potential circularity in perceiving particulars and the resolution that conceiving universal individuals summarily suffices.

Core Idea
The intellect's grasp of particulars doesn't require external existence; summary conception suffices.

A further discussion arises: if perception of the particular as particular requires sense perception, which in turn requires the object's existence, then how can we perceive a particular before it exists? Al-Jurjani presents this objection and resolves it by stating that conceiving the individuals of the universal summarily is enough for voluntary action.

Guided Path
1
Read the circularity objection
Read the objection: perception of a particular as particular requires sense perception, which needs the object's existence. But if the object's existence depends on our intention, there is a circularity.
2
Understand the resolution
Al-Jurjani says the truth is that conceiving the individuals of the universal and intending them in summary is sufficient.
This avoids the need for detailed perception of each particular.
3
Consider the implication
This means the agent does not need to fully perceive every detail of the motion before acting; a general conception of the type suffices.

Source Text

The fourth observatory – the fourth objective: Natural motion is not involuntary place [change] dependent on sensation and will; similarly we say regarding qualities, quantities, and the suitability of bodies for each other. And it is known from that which was mentioned concerning natural motion that the cause of voluntary motion is not the soul due to its constancy and lack of change – meaning that the soul is constant and continuous, so it alone cannot be the cause of motion which is renewing and non-constant, and the soul does not differ in one type of ensouled bodies while voluntary motion differs in that type, even in an individual of it. Nor is it the universal conception (al-taṣawwur al-kullī) obtained by the soul, because its relation to particular motions is uniform; likewise the will arising from the universal conception is only universal, equally related to the particulars of motion, so none of those particulars issue from the soul with its universal conception and will. Rather it [i.e., the cause of voluntary motion] is particular conceptions (taṣawwurāt juz'iyya) upon which follow particular wills (irādāt juz'iyya). So the one walking towards Baghdad has at every step a particular will dependent on a particular conception. They said: 'Voluntary motion towards a place follows the will related to the whole of that motion; then the distance of that motion can be supposed to have particular limits by which the distance is divided into its particular parts, so the mover needs after...' His saying: 'Particular conceptions' – and the obtaining of those conceptions in the imagination does not depend on the existence of those motions in the external world such that circularity would follow; for we imagine forms that have no existence in the external world, as established among them that imaginary forms may come from outside and may come from inside, as in the stations. His saying: 'So the mover, etc.' – and the objection raised against it that this contradicts intuition (al-wijdān), for if we try to move in a given distance we conceive that distance and the motion in summary, but as for conceiving every part of the distance and the motion occurring in it – no. Its answer: We do not deny the conception of absolute motion, but demonstration indicates that that conception and the determinate will from it are insufficient for the issuance of a determinate motion occurring in a part of the distance; rather a particular imagination is necessary for that. And our not finding that is because that imagination and particular will have become a habitude (malaka) of the soul. Evidence for that is that if the imagination and will are cut off during motion, the motion ceases. The detail of this topic is sought from Sharḥ al-Ishārāt and al-Muḥākamāt. His saying: 'Rather they are particular images' – there is discussion in it because perception of the particular in terms of particularity and individuation is only possible through the external senses, and perception of the senses depends on the existence of the sensed, for the nonexistent is not sensed. So the intellect's conception of the particular as particular depends on its existence; if its existence depended on knowledge of it from this aspect, circularity would follow. So the truth is that conceiving the individuals of the universal and intending them summarily are sufficient for their issuance from the free agent. His saying: 'So the mover needs, etc.' – It is said: This is contradicted by intuition when being fair, for when we want to move in a given distance we conceive that distance and the motion in it summarily; but as for conceiving every part of the distance specifically and the motion occurring – no. So the truth is that motion from the beginning to the end is one simple thing, having no division at all. So for its issuance, it suffices to imagine the whole distance summarily and to will motion upon it, and there is no need to imagine the supposed limits upon it and to direct the intention towards them specifically, for there are not multiple motions but one particular motion; and if during the motion there occur imaginations and wills for some parts, those are constant parts occurring at those times, not because motion needs them. Commentary
This passage is a multi-part argument about the true cause of voluntary motion. It begins by stating that the cause is not the soul, because the soul is constant while motion is constantly renewing; a constant cause cannot produce a non-constant effect. Nor is it the universal conception, since a universal is equally related to all particulars and cannot discriminate among them. The cause must be particular conceptions and particular wills, as illustrated by the example of a walker to Baghdad who has a new will at each step. An objection follows: if motion is willed as a whole, why is partial imagination needed? The answer is that imagination of the partial does not require external existence (it can be internal). Another objection from intuition: we seem to conceive motion summarily, not in parts. The answer explains that the particular imagination becomes a habit (malaka), and proof shows it is necessary; otherwise motion would cease if interrupted. A further discussion argues that the intellect's grasp of the particular as particular depends on sense perception, which requires existence, leading to a circularity concern. The resolution is that conceiving universal individuals summarily suffices. A final objection states that intuition suggests motion is a simple whole, not requiring partial imaginations. The answer affirms that motion from start to end is indeed one simple thing, and the partial imaginations that occur are incidental, not required. The passage concludes with a concise summary of the process of successive imaginations and wills and a note on forced motion caused by an external coercer.

Motion Is One Simple Whole: Refutation of Partial Imagination Necessity
Al-Jurjani defends his view against the intuition of motion's simplicity and summarizes the process.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the objection that motion is one simple thing and the reply that partial imaginations are incidental, not essential.

Core Idea
Motion is a single simple reality; partial imaginations are just incidental, not required.

Another objection from intuition: when we move, we seem to conceive the whole distance summarily, not in parts. Al-Jurjani responds that motion is indeed one simple thing, but the partial imaginations that occur during motion are not necessary; they are constant parts arising incidentally. He also provides a summary of the process where successive imaginations and wills connect.

Guided Path
1
Read the objection from intuition
The objection states that when we want to move, we conceive the whole distance summarily, not each part. So why claim that partial imaginations are needed?
2
Understand Al-Jurjani's response
He agrees that motion from start to end is one simple thing. The partial imaginations that occur are not necessary for the motion; they are constant parts that happen during the motion but are not needed for its issuance.
This clarifies that the earlier argument about habit was not saying that partial imaginations are always needed, but that they become habitual.
3
Examine the summary of the process
The passage describes how the will related to the whole motion connects with partial imaginations and wills, and if imagination is cut, motion stops.
4
Note the conclusion on forced motion
The passage ends with a brief note on forced motion, caused by an external coercer.
This is a separate topic.

المرصد الرابع - المقصد الرابع : الحركة الطبيعية ليست المكان لا إرادياً موقوفاً على الشعور والإرادة وكذا نقول في الكيفيات والكميات وملائمة بعضها لبعض الأجسام، ( ويعلم من ذلك) الذي مر ذكره في الحركة الطبيعية ( أن العلة للحركة الإرادية ليست هي النفس لثباتها وعدم اختلافها) يعني أن النفس ثابتة مستمرة فلا تكون وحدها علة للحركة التي هي متجددة غير ثابتة، والنفس غير مختلفة في نوع واحد من الأجسام ذوات الأنفس مع اختلاف الحركة الإرادية في ذلك النوع بل في فرد منه ) ولا أيضاً هي التصور الكلي ) الحاصل للنفس ) لأن نسبته إلى الحركات الجزئية سواء) وكذا الإرادة الناشئة من التصور الكلي لا تكون إلا كلية خطوة إرادة جزئية تابعة لتصور جزئي ( قالوا : إن الحركة الاختيارية إلى مكان تتبع الإرادة متعلقة بمجموع تلك الحركة ثم إن المسافة التي لتلك الحركة يمكن أن يفرض فيها حدود جزئية تتجزأ بها المسافة إلى أجزائها الجزئية فالمتحرك يحتاج بعد قوله : ( تصورات جزئية وحصول تلك التصورات في الخيال لا يتوقف على وجود تلك الحركات في الخارج حتى يلزم الدور، فإنا نتخيل صوراً لا وجود لها في الخارج على ما تقرر عندهم من أن الصور الخيالية قد تجيء من خارج وقد تجيء من داخل كما في المقامات . قوله : ( فالمتحرك إلخ) وما أورد عليه من أن هذا خلاف الوجدان فإذا حاولنا أن نتحرك في مسافة معينة تصورنا تلك المسافة والحركة على الاحتمال وأما تصور كل جزء من أجزاء المسافة والحركة الواقعة فيه فكلا وجوابه : إنا لا ننكر تصور الحركة المطلقة لكن دل البرهان على أن ذلك التصور والإرادة المعينة عنه لا يكفي في صدور حركة معينة واقعة في جزء من المسافة، بل لا بد من تخيل جزئي لذلك وعدم وجداننا لذلك بواسطة أنه صار تلك التخيل والإرادة الجزئية ملكة للنفس، يدل على ذلك أنه لو انقطع التخيل والإرادة في أثناء الحركة تنقطع الحركة، وتفصيل هذا المطلب يطلب من شرح الإشارات والمحاكمات . قوله : (بل) إنما هي تصويرات جزئية فيه بحث لأن إدراك الجزئي من حيث الجزئية والتشخص لا يمكن إلا بالحواس الظاهر وإدراك الحواس موقوف على وجود المحسوس، فإن المعدوم لا يحس فتصور العقل الجزئي من حيث هو جزئي موقوف على وجوده فلو توقف وجوده على العلم به من هذه الحيثية كان دوراً فالحق أن تصور أفراد الكلي والقصد إليها على الإجمال كافيان في صدورها عن المختار . قوله : ( فالمتحرك يحتاج إلخ) قيل : هذا مما يكذبه الوجدان عند الإنصاف فإنا إذا أردنا أن نتحرك في مسافة معينة تصورنا تلك المسافة والحركة فيها على الإجمال، وأما تصور كل جزء من أجزاء المسافة بخصوصه والحركة الواقعة فكلا، فالحق أن الحركة من المبدأ إلى المنتهى أمر واحد بسيط لا انقسام فيها أصلاً، فيكفي في صدورها تخيل المسافة بأسرها إجمالاً وإرادة الحركة عليها ولا حاجة إلى تخيل الحدود المفروضة عليها وتوجه القصد إليها بخصوصها، إذ ليس هناك حركات متعددة بل حركة واحدة جزئية وإن وقع في أثناء الحركة تخيلات وإرادات لبعض الأجزاء، فتلك الأسباب أجزاء ثابتة واقعة في تلك الأوقات لا لاحتياج الحركة إليها . الإرادة المتعلقة بمجموع الحركة إلى أن يتخيل حداً معيناً وتنبعث عنه إرادة جزئية متعلقة بقطع ذلك الجزء من المسافة الذي انفصل بذلك الحد، وهكذا تتوالى التخيلات المستتبعة للإرادة والحركة فتتصل الإرادات في النفس والحركات في المسافة، ولو فرض انقطاع التخيل انقطعت الإرادة والحركة وأما علة الحركة القسرية فهي القوة التي أحدثها القاسر في المتحرك .
The fourth observatory – the fourth objective: Natural motion is not involuntary place [change] dependent on sensation and will; similarly we say regarding qualities, quantities, and the suitability of bodies for each other. And it is known from that which was mentioned concerning natural motion that the cause of voluntary motion is not the soul due to its constancy and lack of change – meaning that the soul is constant and continuous, so it alone cannot be the cause of motion which is renewing and non-constant, and the soul does not differ in one type of ensouled bodies while voluntary motion differs in that type, even in an individual of it. Nor is it the universal conception (al-taṣawwur al-kullī) obtained by the soul, because its relation to particular motions is uniform; likewise the will arising from the universal conception is only universal, equally related to the particulars of motion, so none of those particulars issue from the soul with its universal conception and will. Rather it [i.e., the cause of voluntary motion] is particular conceptions (taṣawwurāt juz'iyya) upon which follow particular wills (irādāt juz'iyya). So the one walking towards Baghdad has at every step a particular will dependent on a particular conception. They said: 'Voluntary motion towards a place follows the will related to the whole of that motion; then the distance of that motion can be supposed to have particular limits by which the distance is divided into its particular parts, so the mover needs after...' His saying: 'Particular conceptions' – and the obtaining of those conceptions in the imagination does not depend on the existence of those motions in the external world such that circularity would follow; for we imagine forms that have no existence in the external world, as established among them that imaginary forms may come from outside and may come from inside, as in the stations. His saying: 'So the mover, etc.' – and the objection raised against it that this contradicts intuition (al-wijdān), for if we try to move in a given distance we conceive that distance and the motion in summary, but as for conceiving every part of the distance and the motion occurring in it – no. Its answer: We do not deny the conception of absolute motion, but demonstration indicates that that conception and the determinate will from it are insufficient for the issuance of a determinate motion occurring in a part of the distance; rather a particular imagination is necessary for that. And our not finding that is because that imagination and particular will have become a habitude (malaka) of the soul. Evidence for that is that if the imagination and will are cut off during motion, the motion ceases. The detail of this topic is sought from Sharḥ al-Ishārāt and al-Muḥākamāt. His saying: 'Rather they are particular images' – there is discussion in it because perception of the particular in terms of particularity and individuation is only possible through the external senses, and perception of the senses depends on the existence of the sensed, for the nonexistent is not sensed. So the intellect's conception of the particular as particular depends on its existence; if its existence depended on knowledge of it from this aspect, circularity would follow. So the truth is that conceiving the individuals of the universal and intending them summarily are sufficient for their issuance from the free agent. His saying: 'So the mover needs, etc.' – It is said: This is contradicted by intuition when being fair, for when we want to move in a given distance we conceive that distance and the motion in it summarily; but as for conceiving every part of the distance specifically and the motion occurring – no. So the truth is that motion from the beginning to the end is one simple thing, having no division at all. So for its issuance, it suffices to imagine the whole distance summarily and to will motion upon it, and there is no need to imagine the supposed limits upon it and to direct the intention towards them specifically, for there are not multiple motions but one particular motion; and if during the motion there occur imaginations and wills for some parts, those are constant parts occurring at those times, not because motion needs them. The will related to the whole of motion until he imagines a particular limit and from it emanates a particular will related to traversing that part of the distance separated by that limit, and so on successive imaginations follow bringing will and motion, so wills in the soul and motions in the distance are connected; and if the cessation of imagination is supposed, will and motion cease. And as for the cause of forced motion, it is the force which the coercer produces in the moved body.
Source pages p. 237

Commentary
This passage is a multi-part argument about the true cause of voluntary motion. It begins by stating that the cause is not the soul, because the soul is constant while motion is constantly renewing; a constant cause cannot produce a non-constant effect. Nor is it the universal conception, since a universal is equally related to all particulars and cannot discriminate among them. The cause must be particular conceptions and particular wills, as illustrated by the example of a walker to Baghdad who has a new will at each step. An objection follows: if motion is willed as a whole, why is partial imagination needed? The answer is that imagination of the partial does not require external existence (it can be internal). Another objection from intuition: we seem to conceive motion summarily, not in parts. The answer explains that the particular imagination becomes a habit (malaka), and proof shows it is necessary; otherwise motion would cease if interrupted. A further discussion argues that the intellect's grasp of the particular as particular depends on sense perception, which requires existence, leading to a circularity concern. The resolution is that conceiving universal individuals summarily suffices. A final objection states that intuition suggests motion is a simple whole, not requiring partial imaginations. The answer affirms that motion from start to end is indeed one simple thing, and the partial imaginations that occur are incidental, not required. The passage concludes with a concise summary of the process of successive imaginations and wills and a note on forced motion caused by an external coercer.

Causality and Created Things
intermediate

The Mover's Unity Does Not Affect the Personal Unity of Motion
Argues that the mover's unity is irrelevant to motion's personal unity.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand why the personal unity of a motion is independent of the unity of its mover, and how this principle supports the permissibility of two causes producing a single effect.

Core Idea
The identity of an effect is not determined by its cause.

This lesson examines a subtle but crucial point in causal theory: the personal unity of a motion remains intact regardless of whether one or multiple movers act upon it. The source passage argues that attributing part of a motion to one mover and part to another is an illusion; the motion itself is a continuous whole. Understanding this principle is key to grasping how multiple causes can converge on a single effect.

Guided Path
1
Read the Arabic passage and translation
Begin by reading the Arabic passage once, then the literal translation. Notice the claim that the mover's unity is not considered for the motion being one in person.
The passage includes a quote from al-Shifa and later commentary.
2
Identify the key example
Find the example of the stone thrown by Zayd or 'Amr. Observe that the motion does not change based on the thrower.
This example illustrates the independence of motion from its mover.
3
Understand the condition for motion's unity
Notice the condition from al-Shifa: the condition for motion's unity is that its time and distance are not actually divided. This is about actual division, not potential.
The motion can be one in person even if it is divided in supposition.
4
Grasp the underlying principle
The passage states that 'attribution to the effect has no role in the individuation of the effect.' Understand why this is the secret behind the example.
This principle allows two independent causes to produce one effect.
5
Reflect on the conclusion
The passage concludes that the unity of a particular specified mover is not considered in the unity of motion. Think about the implications for causal theory.
Be careful not to confuse the point: it is not that any mover's unity is irrelevant, but specifically that the unity of the mover does not individuate the motion.

Source Text

وأما وحدة المحرك فلا عبرة به في كون الحركة واحدة شخصية فإن المتحرك بمحرك ما قد يحركه محرك آخر قبل انقطاع حركته والحركة الصادرة عنهما واحدة شخصية متصلة اتصال المسافة ولا تميز في تلك الحركة بوجب الاثنينية فيها غير ما يتوهم من استناد بعضها إلى محرك والبعض إلى. قوله : ( واحدة شخصية إلخ) في الشفاء أن الشرط في وحدة الحركة هو أن لا يكون زمانها ومسافتها منقسمين بالفعل لا أن يكون بحيث لا ينقسمان بالفعل ولا بالقوة، فالحركة الصادرة عن المحركين واحدة بالشخص وإن كانت منقسمة بالفرض بالنسبة إلى المحركين، كقسمة الحركة الفلكية بالشروق والغروب كما في الشرح الجديد للتجريد من أن مجموع الحركة الذي بعضها مستند إلى محرك وبعضها إلى محرك آخر، لا تعدد في محركها لأن محركها مجموع المحركين لا كل واحد منهما، ومجموع المحركين واحد بالشخص وهم ناشئ من اعتبار الحركة الواحدة الشخصية المتبعضة في الوهم باعتبار النسبة إلى المحركين مجموعاً مركباً من بعضين أحدهما إلى محرك والآخر إلى محرك آخر قال : والصواب في تعليل هذا المطلب أن يقال : إن حجراً واحداً بالشخص إذا تحرك بالقسر في مسافة معينة من مبدأ معين إلى منتهى معين في زمان معين لا تختلف حركة هذه بأن راميه زيد أو عمرو أو غير ذلك وهو معلوم بالضرورة والسر في ذلك أن الاستناد إلى المؤثر لا دخل له في تشخص الأثر، ولذا اتفقوا على جواز توارد علتين مستقلتين على واحد بالشخص ابتداء وعلى سبيل البدل انتهى . إن اللازم مما ذكر أن لا يكون وحدة المحرك لمعين بخصوصه معتبراً في وحدة الحركة لا أن يكون وحدة محرك معين كان معتبرا في وحدتها فتدبر. قوله : ( والحركة الصادرة منهما واحدة شخصية) فيه بحث لأنه يدل على أن المراد بالحركة الواحدة بالشخص مجموع الحركة التي بعضها مستند إلى محرك والبعض الآخر مستند إلى محرك آخر وهو الظاهر من كلامهم، فلا شك في أنه لا تعدد في محركها لأن محركها مجموع المحركين لا كل واحد منهما ليكون المحرك متعددا، ومجموع المحركين واحد بالشخص وكل واحد من المحركين جزء من هذا المحرك الذي هو واحد بالشخص بلا اشتباه، فالصواب في تعليل هذا المطلوب أن يقال : إن حجراً واحداً بالشخص إذا تحرك بالقسر في مسافة بعينها من مبدأ إلى منتهى معين في زمان معين لا يختلف حركته هذه بأن راميه زيد أو عمرو أو غيرهما وذلك معلوم بالضرورة، والسر في ذلك أن الاستناد إلى المؤثر لا دخل له في تشخص الأثر، ولذلك اتفقوا على جواز توارد علتين مستقلتين على معلول واحد بالشخص ابتداء على سبيل البدل.
As for the unity of the mover, it is not considered for the motion being one in person. For a thing moved by one mover may be moved by another mover before its motion ceases, and the motion emanating from both is one in person, continuous with the continuity of the distance. And there is no distinction in that motion necessitating duality in it except what is imagined from attributing part of it to one mover and part to another. His saying: '(one in person etc.)' In al-Shifa: the condition for the unity of motion is that its time and distance are not actually divided, not that they are not divided either actually or potentially. So the motion emanating from two movers is one in person, even if it is divided by supposition with respect to the two movers, like the division of celestial motion by sunrise and sunset, as in the new commentary on al-Tajrid: that the total motion, part of which is attributed to one mover and part to another mover, has no multiplicity in its mover because its mover is the sum of the two movers, not each one of them, and the sum of the two movers is one in person. And the illusion arises from considering the one personal motion as divided in the imagination by considering the relation to the two movers as a whole composed of two parts, one to one mover and the other to another mover. He said: The correct explanation of this point is to say: A single stone in person, if it moves by force in a specific distance from a specific beginning to a specific end in a specific time, its motion does not differ whether its thrower is Zayd or 'Amr or someone else. This is known necessarily. And the secret of that is that the attribution to the effect (al-athar) has no role in the individuation of the effect (tashakhkhus al-athar). Therefore they agreed on the permissibility of two independent causes occurring on one effect in person initially and by way of substitution. End. The necessary consequence of what was mentioned is that the unity of a particular specified mover is not considered in the unity of motion, not that the unity of a specified mover was considered in its unity, so reflect. His saying: 'And the motion emanating from both is one in person' – there is discussion in it, because it indicates that what is meant by the one motion in person is the total motion, part of which is attributed to one mover and the other part to another mover, which is apparent from their statements. There is no doubt that there is no multiplicity in its mover, because its mover is the sum of the two movers, not each one of them such that the mover would be multiple. And the sum of the two movers is one in person, and each of the two movers is part of this mover which is one in person without confusion. So the correct explanation of this point is to say: A single stone in person, if it moves by force in a specific distance from a beginning to a specific end in a specific time, its motion does not differ whether its thrower is Zayd or 'Amr or others. That is known necessarily. And the secret is that attribution to the effect has no role in the individuation of the effect, and therefore they agreed on the permissibility of two independent causes occurring on one effect in person initially by way of substitution.
Source pages p. 240

Commentary
This passage argues that the unity of the mover (al-muḥarrik) is irrelevant to the personal unity of motion. It first states the principle: a body moved by one mover can be taken over by another mover before its motion stops, and the motion from both is one person. The only supposed duality comes from imagining that part of the motion is attributed to one mover and part to another, but this is illusory. The text then quotes from al-Shifa (by Ibn Sina) that the condition for motion's unity is that its time and distance are not actually divided, not that they are indivisible potentially. The motion from two movers is one in person, just like celestial motion divided by sunrise and sunset is still one motion. The commentary explains that the total motion does not have multiple movers because its mover is the sum of both movers, which is one in person. The illusion of division comes from considering the relation to each mover. The correct explanation is exemplified by a stone thrown by different persons: the motion itself does not change based on who threw it. The reason is that the attribution to the effect (istinād ilā al-mu'aththir) has no role in individuating the effect (tashakhkhuṣ al-athar). Therefore, it is agreed that two independent causes can produce the same effect in person, either simultaneously or sequentially. The passage concludes by emphasizing that the point is not that any specific mover's unity is considered, but rather that the unity of a particular specified mover is not considered. A further note discusses a potential confusion: the phrase 'motion from both is one in person' might imply that the total motion is a composite of parts from each mover, but the correct view is that the mover of the total motion is the sum of the two, which is one, and each mover is a part of that one mover. The key principle remains that the effect's individuation is independent of its cause.

Key Terms
وحدة المحرك
تشخص الأثر
الاستناد إلى المؤثر
توارد علتين

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: If two different movers act on the same motion, the motion must be two distinct motions.
Correction: The motion remains one in person because the effect's identity does not depend on the cause's identity, as shown by the example of a stone thrown by different persons.

Checkpoint
Why is the motion of a stone thrown by Zayd and then by 'Amr considered one personal motion?
A) Because the stone is the same
B) Because the time and distance are the same

D) Because both Zayd and 'Amr are human

Key Takeaway
The personal identity of a motion does not derive from its mover but from its own spatiotemporal continuity.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
You just learned that the identity of an effect does not come from its cause. Think of a situation where you have been praised or blamed for an outcome. How much of that outcome was truly 'yours'? Are you willing to let go of the need to claim ownership of results, knowing that the effect's identity is not tied to you as its cause?
?
This passage says two different movers can produce one continuous motion. In your spiritual life, how many different 'movers' (people, circumstances, desires) have contributed to your current state? Can you accept that your present self—your faith, your struggles—is one single person despite being shaped by many causes, and that you are not divided between them?
?
The secret revealed here is that attribution to a cause does not individuate the effect. Consider how much of your self-worth is tied to the 'causes' you credit for your achievements—your intellect, your effort, your good fortune. If that attribution is irrelevant to who you truly are, what remains of your identity that is genuinely your own before God?

Personal Unity of Motion and Irrelevance of Mover Unity
Al-Jurjani explains conditions for personal unity of motion and argues that mover unity is irrelevant.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand why the personal unity of motion does not depend on the unity of the mover, and what conditions actually govern the individuation of motion.

Core Idea
Motion's individuality hinges on time, place, and endpoints, not on having a single mover.

In this passage, al-Jurjani addresses a key question: what makes a motion one personal motion? He argues that while certain factors like time and place must be unified, the unity of the mover is not required. He illustrates this with the case of a stone thrown by different people, showing the motion remains one. This lesson unpacks his reasoning.

Guided Path
1
Identify conditions for personal unity
Read the opening sentence where al-Jurjani states that for motion to be one personal motion, there must be unity of several things such as the place, time, and endpoints. Note that he considers three things more appropriate than four.
The six things mentioned earlier include both the beginning and end points.
2
Note the exclusion of mover unity
Find the sentence that explicitly says the unity of the mover is not considered for personal unity. Understand that motion from two different movers can still be one personal motion.
3
Understand the proof with the stone example
Read the commentary and the stone example. A stone thrown by Zayd then by Amr in the same path and time has one motion. The ascription to different movers does not divide the motion.
This shows that the effect's individuation is independent of the cause's identity.
4
Reflect on the conclusion
Al-Jurjani concludes that the necessary implication is that unity of a particular mover is not considered for motion's unity. Ponder the distinction he makes.

Commentary
This passage is a definition and proof concerning the personal unity of motion (الوحدة الشخصية للحركة) and the irrelevance of the mover's unity. The author begins by stating that for motion to be one personal motion, certain conditions must be unified: he mentions that considering the unity of the two ends together with the unity of place and time would suffice, but considering three (what it is in, what it is from, what it is to) is more appropriate. He then argues that the unity of the mover is not a condition for personal unity. He supports this with an example: an object moved by one mover can be moved by another mover before its motion ends, and the motion from both is still one personal motion, continuous in distance, with no actual division due to different ascriptions. He compares this to astronomical motion which, though continuous, is imaginarily divided by rising and setting, yet remains one. The commentary from al-Shifa clarifies the condition: the time and distance must not be actually divided, not that they are undividable even potentially. The commentary also provides a proof using the example of a stone thrown by different individuals; the motion does not change, showing that ascription to the effect does not affect individuation. The author concludes that the necessary implication is that the unity of a particular mover is not considered for the unity of motion, not that a specific mover's unity was considered.

Correction: The passage explicitly states that the unity of the mover is not considered, and a motion can be one even if it proceeds from two different movers, as shown by the stone example.

Objection and Answer: Multiple Movers on One Motion
Sanusi presents an objection and gives the answer.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the objection that a second mover must have an effect either identical or different, and the answer that different effects do not break the personal continuous unity of motion.

Core Idea
Two movers can act on one motion without breaking its unity.

Sanusi presents a common objection: if a second mover has an effect, it must either be the same as the first's effect (causing repetition) or different (producing two motions). The answer is that the effects are different, yet the motion remains one continuous whole. This lesson examines the objection and its resolution.

Guided Path
1
Read the objection
The objection presents a dilemma: either the second mover has no effect (so it's not a mover) or it has an effect identical to or different from the first.
Note the two horns of the dilemma.
2
Read the answer
The answer chooses that the effects are different, but that does not invalidate the personal continuous unity of motion.
3
Ask why unity is preserved
Reflect on how different effects can belong to one motion. The commentary will clarify that the difference is only in relation to the movers, not in the external reality.
The unity is personal (الشخصية) and continuous (الاتصالية).

For if it is said: the second mover, if it has no effect, then it is not a mover; and if it has an effect, then if its effect is identical to the effect of the first mover, then the acquisition of the acquired results and the concurrence of two effectors on a single personal effect follows; and if it is other than it, then the two effects, namely the two motions, become multiple. We say: we choose that the two effects are different, and that does not invalidate the personal continuous unity. His saying: (and no separation due to difference of ascription) meaning no external separation due to this ascription such that it would invalidate the personal unity of motion, for this fragmentation is purely imaginary, occurring to the motion in relation to the two movers. His saying: (that the two effects are different, etc.) by itself in motion in the sense of cutting (قطعة) and considering the relation to the distance in the sense of mediate (توسط), and that imaginary difference does not necessitate multiplicity of the imagination and the motion externally, because its personal unity is not invalidated without actual division with respect to the distance or time, so ponder, for it has been hidden from some observers. His saying: (We say: we choose that the two effects are different, etc.) The context of his speech here indicates that what he means by the motion which he judged to have personal unity despite the multiplicity of the mover is motion in the sense of cutting (قطعة), since there is no fragmentation for motion in the sense of mediate (توسط). Thus the effect multiplies by virtue of the ascription of some of the extension to one mover and the other part to another mover. And you are aware that what is meant by mover here is the efficient cause of motion, because one of the six things related to its unity [of motion] is the agent, as explicitly stated in the previous inquiry; and that motion in the sense of cutting is something impossible to occur in external entities, as stated in the second inquiry of this chapter, and it does not need an agent, as shown by his reasoning in the previous inquiry regarding this need by his saying: 'For motion is a possible existence, so it must have an efficient cause.' Unless his meaning here is motion in the sense of mediate (توسط), and it has been established and stated by the commentator in the margins of Ma'arif Sharh al-Matale' that the effect of the agent is in the very existence and has no role in the necessary concomitants of existence. I say: even if it is granted that in its necessary concomitants there is absolute difference of relations, not the specific difference, then there remains an objection we indicated in the second inquiry of the chapter, so let one refer to it. However, the discussion is about the multiplicity of the agent of motion, and there is no multiplicity for the agent of the very motion in this assumption. The truth is that the context is a place of discussion.

Commentary
This passage is an objection-and-answer (مناقشة) regarding the claim that a single motion can have multiple movers. The objection states: if the second mover has no effect, it is not a mover; if it has an effect, either it is identical to the first mover's effect (leading to repetition and two effectors on one effect) or different (leading to two motions). The answer chooses that the two effects are different, yet this does not break personal unity because the motion remains continuous and undivided externally; any division is only imaginary. The subsequent commentaries explain that the difference is only with respect to the sense of motion as 'cutting' (قطعة) versus 'mediate' (توسط), and that the effect of the agent is on existence itself, not its accidents. The passage also discusses the nature of motion and the efficient cause, cautioning that motion in the sense of cutting is not real, and thus the multiplicity of movers is not genuine.

الأثر
تحصيل الحاصل
اجتماع المؤثرين
الوحدة الشخصية

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: That if two movers produce different effects, the motion must be two separate motions.
Correction: The answer shows that the effects can be different while the motion remains one continuous whole in external reality; the difference is only in relation to the movers.

B) The second mover has no effect at all.

D) The motion becomes two different motions.

Key Takeaway
Two movers can have different effects on the same motion without destroying its personal continuous unity.

Commentary: The Imaginary Fragmentation and the Two Senses of Motion
The commentator elaborates on the answer, clarifying the nature of the difference and the two senses of motion.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand how the commentator explains that the difference in effects is only imaginary, and how the distinction between motion as cutting (قطعة) and motion as mediate (توسط) resolves the apparent multiplicity.

Core Idea
The fragmentation of motion is purely imaginary; unity is preserved by considering motion as a continuous whole.

The previous answer that the two effects are different without breaking unity requires clarification. The commentator explains that the difference is only in relation to the movers, not in external reality. He then introduces the two senses of motion—cutting and mediate—to show how a single motion can be ascribed to multiple movers without actual division.

Guided Path
1
Read the first commentary
The first comment explains that no external separation occurs; any fragmentation is imaginary, arising only by considering motion in relation to the two movers.
Key phrase: 'هذا التبعض وهمي محض'.
2
Read the second commentary
The second comment relates motion to the sense of cutting (قطعة) and mediate (توسط). The difference is only imaginary and does not create real multiplicity.
Note the two senses.
3
Read the third commentary
The third comment clarifies that the intended motion is cutting, but cutting is not real; thus the multiplicity of movers is not genuine. It also discusses the efficient cause.
The passage becomes more technical; focus on the main conclusion.
4
Synthesize the argument
Combine the comments: the differences are in ascription, not in existence; motion as a continuous entity remains one.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
فإن قيل : المحرك الثاني إن لم يكن له أثر لم يكن محركاً ، وإن كان له أثر فإن كان أثره عين أثر المحرك الأول لزم تحصيل الحاصل واجتماع مؤثرين على أثر واحد شخصي وإن كان غيره فقد تعدد الأثران أعني الحركتين قلنا نختار أن الأثرين متغايران وذلك لا يبطل الوحدة الشخصية الاتصالية )  قوله : ( ولا فصل بسبب اختلاف الاستناد أي لا فصل في الخارج بسبب هذا الاستناد حتى تبطل الوحدة الشخصية للحركة فإن هذا التبعض وهمي محض يعرض للحركة بالقياس إلى المحركين .  قوله : (أن الأثرين متغايران إلخ بنفسها في الحركة بمعنى القطع وباعتبار النسبة إلى المسافة بمعنى التوسط ، ولا يستلزم ذلك التغاير الوهمي تعدد الوهم والحركة في الخارج، لأن وحدتها الشخصية لا تبطل بدون الفعل بالفعل باعتبار المسافة أو باعتبار الزمان فتدبر، فإنه قد خفي على بعض الناظرين.  قوله: (قلنا نختار أن الأثرين متغايرين إلخ سياق كلامه هاهنا يدل على أن مراده بالحركة التي حكم بوحدتها الشخصية مع تعدد المحرك هو الحركة بمعنى القطع، إذ لا تبعض للحركة بمعنى التوسط فيتعدد الأثر باعتبار أن اتصاف بعض الامتداد مستند إلى محرك والبعض الآخر إلى محرك آخر ، وأنت خبير بأن المراد بالمحرك هاهنا هو السبب الفاعل للحركة، لأن أحد الأشياء الستة يتعلق بوحدتها الحركة هو الفاعل كما دل عليه صريح كلامه في المقصد السابق وأن الحركة بمعنى القطع أمر مستحيل التحقق في الأعيان كما صرح به في المقصد الثاني من هذا الفصل، ولا يحتاج إلى الفاعل كما دل عليه تعليله في المقصد السابق هذا الاحتياج بقوله : فإن الحركة أمر ممكن الوجود فلا بد لها من علة فاعلية اللهم إلا أن يكون مراده هاهنا الحركة بمعنى التوسط وقد تقرر عندهم وصرح به الشارح في حواشي معرفات شرح المطالع أن تأثير الفاعل في نفس الوجود ولا دخل له في لوازم الوجود قلت لو سلم في لوازم وجودها مطلق اختلاف النسب لا الاختلاف المخصوص بقي هاهنا إشكال أشرنا إليه في المقصد الثاني من الفصل، فليرجع إليه على أن الكلام في تعدد فاعل الحركة ولا تعدد لفاعل نفس الحركة على هذا الفرض والحق أن المقام محل الكلام.
For if it is said: the second mover, if it has no effect, then it is not a mover; and if it has an effect, then if its effect is identical to the effect of the first mover, then the acquisition of the acquired results and the concurrence of two effectors on a single personal effect follows; and if it is other than it, then the two effects, namely the two motions, become multiple. We say: we choose that the two effects are different, and that does not invalidate the personal continuous unity. His saying: (and no separation due to difference of ascription) meaning no external separation due to this ascription such that it would invalidate the personal unity of motion, for this fragmentation is purely imaginary, occurring to the motion in relation to the two movers. His saying: (that the two effects are different, etc.) by itself in motion in the sense of cutting (قطعة) and considering the relation to the distance in the sense of mediate (توسط), and that imaginary difference does not necessitate multiplicity of the imagination and the motion externally, because its personal unity is not invalidated without actual division with respect to the distance or time, so ponder, for it has been hidden from some observers. His saying: (We say: we choose that the two effects are different, etc.) The context of his speech here indicates that what he means by the motion which he judged to have personal unity despite the multiplicity of the mover is motion in the sense of cutting (قطعة), since there is no fragmentation for motion in the sense of mediate (توسط). Thus the effect multiplies by virtue of the ascription of some of the extension to one mover and the other part to another mover. And you are aware that what is meant by mover here is the efficient cause of motion, because one of the six things related to its unity [of motion] is the agent, as explicitly stated in the previous inquiry; and that motion in the sense of cutting is something impossible to occur in external entities, as stated in the second inquiry of this chapter, and it does not need an agent, as shown by his reasoning in the previous inquiry regarding this need by his saying: 'For motion is a possible existence, so it must have an efficient cause.' Unless his meaning here is motion in the sense of mediate (توسط), and it has been established and stated by the commentator in the margins of Ma'arif Sharh al-Matale' that the effect of the agent is in the very existence and has no role in the necessary concomitants of existence. I say: even if it is granted that in its necessary concomitants there is absolute difference of relations, not the specific difference, then there remains an objection we indicated in the second inquiry of the chapter, so let one refer to it. However, the discussion is about the multiplicity of the agent of motion, and there is no multiplicity for the agent of the very motion in this assumption. The truth is that the context is a place of discussion.
Source pages p. 240

Commentary
This passage is an objection-and-answer (مناقشة) regarding the claim that a single motion can have multiple movers. The objection states: if the second mover has no effect, it is not a mover; if it has an effect, either it is identical to the first mover's effect (leading to repetition and two effectors on one effect) or different (leading to two motions). The answer chooses that the two effects are different, yet this does not break personal unity because the motion remains continuous and undivided externally; any division is only imaginary. The subsequent commentaries explain that the difference is only with respect to the sense of motion as 'cutting' (قطعة) versus 'mediate' (توسط), and that the effect of the agent is on existence itself, not its accidents. The passage also discusses the nature of motion and the efficient cause, cautioning that motion in the sense of cutting is not real, and thus the multiplicity of movers is not genuine.

Correction: The commentary emphasizes that the difference is only in ascription, not in external reality; motion remains one continuous entity.

C) Because the motion is divided by distance.

The unity of motion is preserved because any fragmentation is only in relation to the movers, not in external reality.

Defending Personal Unity of Motion Against Multiple Movers
Objection and answer on personal unity of motion

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand how the author refutes the objection that two movers necessarily produce two separate motions, thus preserving the personal unity of motion.

Core Idea
Multiple movers do not break the personal unity of a single continuous motion.

This passage tackles a sophisticated objection: if a motion has two movers, how can it remain one? The author uses celestial motion as an example to show that imaginary divisions based on attribution do not destroy real continuity. The discussion sharpens our understanding of causality and unity in Islamic theology.

Guided Path
1
Read the Passage
Read the Arabic passage and its translation carefully. Pay attention to the flow of the objection and the author's response.
Notice that the passage begins with an assertion about celestial motion, then presents a objection, and ends with an answer.
2
Identify the Objection
The objection says that if there is a second mover, either it has no effect (contradicting its being a mover) or it has an effect. If the effect is identical to the first mover's, that's impossible (acquisition of the already acquired). If different, there are two motions.
This is a classic dilemma: two options, both seemingly problematic.
3
Understand the Answer
The author chooses the second option: the effects are distinct. But he argues that this distinctness does not invalidate the personal unity of the motion itself. The unity is in the continuous actual motion, while the multiplicity is in attribution to different movers.
The author does not claim that the effects are identical; rather, they are distinct in attribution but the motion remains one.
4
Reflect on the Broader Point
This argument shows that personal unity can coexist with multiple causal agents, as long as the motion in itself is continuous. The divisions are only mental or imaginary.
Connect this to the concept of personal unity (al-waḥdah al-shakhṣiyyah) and how it is defended against objections.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
محرك ( آخر ولا تجزو فيها بالفعل ولا فصل بسبب اختلاف الاستناد ألا ترى أن الحركة الفلكية مع اتصالها في نفسها يعرض لها انقسامات وهمية بحسب الشروق والغروب والمسامتات، وذلك لا يبطل وحدتها الشخصية فإن قيل : المحرك الثاني إن لم يكن له أثر لم يكن محركاً ، وإن كان له أثر فإن كان أثره عين أثر المحرك الأول لزم تحصيل الحاصل واجتماع مؤثرين على أثر واحد شخصي وإن كان غيره فقد تعدد الأثران أعني الحركتين قلنا نختار أن الأثرين متغايران وذلك لا يبطل الوحدة الشخصية الاتصالية )
Another mover, and it is not separated in actuality nor is there a separation due to difference in attribution. Do you not see that celestial motion, despite its continuity in itself, is subject to imaginary divisions according to sunrise, sunset, and culminations? That does not invalidate its personal unity. If it is said: The second mover, if it has no effect, is not a mover; and if it has an effect, then if its effect is identical to the effect of the first mover, this necessitates the acquisition of the already acquired and the concurrence of two effective agents on one individual effect; and if it is other, then the two effects—namely the two motions—are multiple. We say: We choose that the two effects are distinct, and that does not invalidate the connected personal unity.
Source pages p. 241

Commentary
This passage addresses an objection to the personal unity of motion. The author begins by affirming that celestial motion, despite appearing continuous, can be conceptually divided by reference to astronomical events (sunrise, sunset, culminations), but these imaginary divisions do not break its actual personal unity. Then an objection is raised: if there is a second mover, it must either have no effect (contradicting its being a mover) or have an effect. If its effect is identical to the first mover's, that would involve a logical problem (obtaining what is already obtained and two agents producing one individual effect). If the effect is different, there would be two motions. The author replies by choosing the second option: the two effects are distinct, but this does not destroy the connected personal unity because the motion is still one continuous entity; the distinctness is only in attribution to different movers, not in the actual motion itself. The commentary then clarifies that this discussion pertains to motion in the sense of 'cut' (al-qat'), which is a mental construct, so the multiplicity is imaginary.

Can a single act have multiple attributions while remaining one? What would change if you saw all your deeds as one motion toward Allah?

The source says multiplicity in attribution does not break unity. Consider your daily actions: some are for family, some for work, some for Allah. Do you feel they pull you apart? How the Type of Motion is Determined by Its Subject and Termini
Defines the rule for specific unity/diversity of motion based on subject and termini.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand how the specific type (uniqueness) of a motion is determined by the subject in which it occurs, its starting point, and its ending point, and you will be able to identify the three scenarios that cause motions to differ in type.

Core Idea
The type of motion depends on three factors: the subject, the start, and the end.

This passage from al-Mawaqif clarifies a foundational principle: motions are not all the same; their specific identity depends on what they are in and where they go. Al-Ījī uses examples from quality and place to show how differences in the subject (ما فيه) or the termini (ما منه وإليه) lead to different types of motion. Understanding this will help you grasp how reality distinguishes between seemingly similar changes.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage and translation
Read the Arabic passage once without analyzing it, then read the full translation. Let the author's own rhythm come through.
Notice the technical terms: ما فيه, ما منه, ما إليه.
2
Identify the three factors
The passage mentions three factors that determine motion's type: the subject (ما فيه), the starting point (ما منه), and the ending point (ما إليه). List them.
3
Understand the three scenarios
There are three cases: (1) only the subject differs, (2) both subject and termini differ, (3) only the termini differ. The passage gives examples for each.
Scenario 1: quality and place examples. Scenario 2: blackening vs heating. Scenario 3: ascending/descending and heating/cooling.
4
Study the examples carefully
For quality, compare the two color progressions. For place, compare straight and circular motion. See why each pair represents different types of motion.
The key is that the subject (the body) is the same in some examples, but the termini differ.
5
Reflect on the principle
Ask yourself: Why does it matter that motion type depends on these factors? This is part of a larger debate about the nature of change and continuity.
This connects to the learning objective.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
اتحاد كانت الحركة واحدة بالنوع وإذا تنوعت كانت الحركة متنوعة (إذ لو اختلف ما فيه ) الحركة بحسب النوع ( كان كل من الحركات الواقعة في تلك الأنواع المختلفة ( نوعان من الحركة) وإن اتحد ما منه وما إليه أما في الكيف فمثل أن يأخذ الجسم في الحركة تارة من البياض إلى الصفرة إلى الحمرة إلى القتمة إلى السواد، وأخرى من البياض إلى الفستقية إلى الخضرة إلى النيلية إلى السواد فإنّ ما فيه الحركة هاهنا مختلف بالنوع وكذا الحركة مع اتحاد المبدأ والمنتهى بالنوع، وأما في الأين فمثل أن يتحرك الجسم من مبدأ إلى منتهى معينين تارة على الاستقامة وأخرى على الاستدارة، فإنّ المستدير والمستقيم مختلفان بالماهية لا بالعوارض فكذلك الحركتان الواقعتان عليهما وإذا كانت الحركة مختلفة بالنوع لاختلاف ما فيه مع ما منه وما إليه فاختلافها بالنوع، لاختلاف ما فيه منضماً إلى اختلافهما كان أولى ( كالتسود والتسخن فإنهما مختلفان بالماهية لاختلاف الأمور الثلاثة فيهما ) وكذلك ما منه وما إليه ) فإنهما إذا اختلفا بالنوع اختلف ماهية الحركة ( وإن اتحد ما فيه كالصاعدة والهابطة ) في الحركة الأينية ( وكالتسخن والتبرد) في الحركة الكيفية، فإن الحركتين في كل واحدة من هذين المثالين مختلفتان بالماهية لاختلاف المبدأ والمنتهى فيهما بالنوع مع اتحاد ما فيه
Unity: Motion is one in type, and if it varies, motion is varied. For if that in which motion occurs differs in type, then each of the motions occurring in those different types are two types of motion, even if the 'from which' and 'to which' are unified. As for quality, for example, the body takes in motion sometimes from whiteness to yellowness to redness to greyness to blackness, and another time from whiteness to pistachio-green to greenness to indigo to blackness; then that in which motion occurs here differs in type, and likewise the motion with the unity of the beginning and end in type. As for place, for example, the body moves from a specific beginning to a specific end sometimes in a straight line and sometimes in a circular path; then the circular and straight differ in essence, not by accidents, so likewise the two motions occurring upon them. And if motion is different in type due to the difference of that in which it occurs with the 'from which' and 'to which', then its difference in type due to the difference of that in which it occurs combined with their difference is more obvious, like blackening and heating, for they differ in essence because of the difference of the three things in them. Likewise, the 'from which' and 'to which' — if they differ in type, the essence of motion differs, even if that in which it occurs is unified, like ascending and descending in place motion, and like heating and cooling in quality motion; for the two motions in each of these two examples differ in essence due to the difference of the beginning and end in them in type while that in which it occurs is unified.
Source pages p. 242

Commentary
This passage defines the principle of specific unity and diversity of motion. It begins by stating that if that in which motion occurs (ما فيه) differs in type, then the motions differ in type, even if the termini (ما منه وما إليه) are the same. It then gives examples in the category of quality (الكيف), such as a body changing from white to yellow to red to grey to black, and from white to pistachio to green to indigo to black: here what it is in differs by type, so the motions differ. In the category of place (الأين), it gives the example of a body moving from a starting point to an endpoint sometimes in a straight line and sometimes in a circle: straight and circular differ essentially, so the motions differ. The passage then adds that if both the subject (ما فيه) and the termini differ, the difference is even more evident, as in blackening and heating (التسود والتسخن), where all three factors differ. Finally, it considers cases where the subject is the same but the termini differ, like ascending and descending (الصاعدة والهابطة) in place, or heating and cooling (التسخن والتبرد) in quality: here the motions differ essentially due to the difference in starting and ending points, even though the subject is the same.

Correction: The passage shows that both the subject (ما فيه) and the termini (ما منه وإليه) matter; a change in any of these factors can result in a different type of motion.

Checkpoint

B) They are different types.
C) They are identical.
D) They are not comparable.

Key Takeaway
The type of motion is determined by the combination of its subject and its starting and ending points; a change in any of these factors can result in a different type of motion.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
You are constantly in motion—not just physically, but morally and spiritually. This lesson shows that the type of motion is determined by both your current state and where you are headed. Right now, what is the 'subject' of your soul's motion? What are the 'termini'—what are you moving from and towards? Does your motion lead you closer to Allah or away from Him?
?
Think of a recent decision you made that changed your direction in life. According to this lesson, even if your starting point was the same as before, a different endpoint makes the entire motion a different type. Are you aware that small changes in intention or destination can transform the very nature of your actions? How will you guard your intentions so that your motion is always towards good?

Answer to objection about accidental difference in starting points
Refutes the claim that up and down differ only accidentally and not essentially.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand why the opposition between being a starting point and an ending point is sufficient for motion to differ essentially, even if the termini themselves are not essentially different.

Core Idea
The opposition of being a beginning vs an end is a real contradiction that makes motions essentially different.

In this passage, a key objection is raised: if up and down differ only accidentally, then ascending and descending motions would not differ essentially. The answer reveals a subtle but crucial point: the roles of 'beginning' and 'end' are themselves opposites in the opposition of contradiction. This teaches us that functional opposition within motion is enough to ground essential difference.

Guided Path
1
Understand the objection
The objector claims that since up and down differ only accidentally (like hot and cold differ essentially), ascending and descending motions would not differ in essence. Recognize the assumption: only essential difference in termini yields essential difference in motion.
The objection uses an analogy from the second example where heat and cold are essentially different.
2
Identify the answer
The answer concedes that up and down are not essentially different, but introduces a new distinction: they differ in being a beginning vs an end (mabda'iyya and muntahaiyya). These two roles are opposites.
This shift from the termini themselves to their functional roles is critical.
3
Grasp 'opposition of contradiction'
The terms 'being a beginning' and 'being an end' are truly contradictory (one excludes the other). This opposition is sufficient to make the motions differ in essence, even if the points themselves are not essentially different.
Opposition of contradiction is a strong relation in kalam.
4
Connect to broader context
This argument shows that relational properties (like mabda'iyya) have real ontological weight in defining motion. It also references the Eastern Investigations for support.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
فإن قيل : تنوع المبدأ والمنتهى في المثال الثاني ظاهر فإن السخونة مخالفة بالماهية للبرودة بخلافه في المثال الأول، لأن الاختلاف بين المبدأين إنما هو باعتبار إن عرض لأحدهما الفوقية وللآخر التحتية وذلك لا يوجب اختلافاً في الماهية. قلنا : إنهما وإن لم يختلفا بالماهية لكنهما اختلفا بالمبدئية والمنتهائية وهما متقابلان تقابل التضاد وهذا القدر كاف في اختلاف الحركة بالماهية كذا في المباحث المشرقية
For it is said: The variety of the beginning and the end in the second example is apparent, for heat is essentially different from cold, unlike in the first example, because the difference between the two beginnings is only due to accidental occurrence of 'upness' to one and 'downness' to the other, and that does not cause difference in essence. We say: The two, even if they do not differ in essence, nevertheless differ in being a beginning and an end, and they are opposites in opposition of contradiction, and this amount is sufficient for the difference of motion in essence, as in the Eastern Investigations.
Source pages p. 242

Commentary
This passage provides an objection and its answer. The objection points out that in the second example of place motion (ascending and descending), the difference between the starting point (e.g., up) and the ending point (e.g., down) is merely accidental (due to 'upness' and 'downness') and not essential, unlike the case of hot and cold which are essentially different. Therefore, it might be thought that the motion should not differ essentially. The answer replies that although up and down are not different in essence, they differ in being a starting point and an ending point (المبدئية والمنتهائية). These two roles are opposites in the opposition of contradiction (تقابل التضاد), and this opposition is sufficient to make the motions differ in essence. The passage then references the 'Mabahith Mashriqiyya' as supporting this view.

Key Terms
المبدأ والمنتهى
المبدئية والمنتهائية
تقابل التضاد

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: One might believe that only essential difference in the termini themselves can cause motion to differ essentially.
Correction: The passage shows that opposition in the functions of being a beginning vs an end (even if the termini are accidentally different) is sufficient for essential difference in motion.

Checkpoint
What is the basis for the answer to the objection that the difference between up and down is accidental?
A) They are essentially different.
B) They are opposites as starting and ending points.
C) They are the same.
D) They are not relevant.

Key Takeaway
The roles of being a beginning and an end are opposites in the opposition of contradiction, and this opposition is sufficient to make motions differ essentially.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
When you look at the 'up' and 'down' in your own life—the positions, roles, or relationships you consider merely accidental—have you ever missed the deeper, contradictory opposition that actually defines them? What would it mean for you to see the 'beginning' and 'end' in them, and how would that change your actions?
?
Consider a conflict or disagreement you've dismissed as 'just a matter of perspective' (like up vs down). Could it be that the real issue is a contradiction between being a beginning and being an end? Are you willing to re-examine that situation today, not as accidental, but as a real opposition that demands a choice?
?
If the roles of 'beginning' and 'end' are true opposites even when the things themselves seem alike, how does that challenge your tendency to equivocate or blur lines in your own convictions? Will you commit to naming the contradictions in your life rather than smoothing them over?
