Refutation that Slowness is Due to Interposed Rests
Al-Jurjani refutes the claim that slowness is due to interposed rests, using a horse and celestial sphere example.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand al-Jurjani's argument that slowness cannot be solely caused by interposed rests, and why this argument supports the refutation of atomism.

Core Idea
If slowness were only rests, a fast horse would be motionless—contradicting observation.

This passage from al-Jurjani's commentary tackles a specific causal claim: that slowness in motion arises from tiny rests between movements. He shows that this claim leads to an absurd consequence when comparing a galloping horse with the motion of the celestial sphere. By following his logical steps, you will see how he uses both reason and sensory evidence to refute the idea, and how this relates to broader theological and philosophical debates.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage carefully
Identify the claim being refuted (slowness is due to interposed rests) and note the logical structure.
The passage begins with a conditional statement: if slowness were only from rests, then...
2
Follow the thought experiment
Compare a horse running 50 leagues from dawn to midday with the celestial sphere covering a quarter of its orbit in the same time.
The sphere's motion is vastly greater, yet if slowness were from rests, the horse's motion would be overwhelmed.
3
Understand the logical consequence
If rests cause slowness, the number of horse's rests would equal the sphere's excess motion (a million times), making horse's motions imperceptible.
The conclusion: the horse would appear motionless, which is false.
4
Examine the objection and response
An objector says rest is non-existential (hence imperceptible). Al-Jurjani responds that rest is perceived by concomitance (e.g., we perceive blindness or sitting).
Therefore, the horse's situation would be perceived if it were stationary.
5
Reflect on the broader significance
This argument is part of proofs against atomism, based on the correlation of motions differing in speed.
It shows that a purely atomistic view of motion leads to absurdity.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وليس البطء أي ليس كل بطء لتخلل السكنات بين الحركات وإلا لم يحس بحركة الفرس وإن فرضت سريعة جداً واللازم بطلانه ظاهر بيان الملازمة أن البطء لو لم يكن إلا لتخلل السكنات فيما بين الحركات كان تفاوت السرعة والبطء بحسب تفاوت السكنات المتخللة في القلة والكثرة فإذا عدا فرس أشد عدو كما إذا قدر أنه عدا من أول اليوم إلى منتصفه خمسين فرسخاً كان حركته هذه أبطأ من حركة المحدد بنسبة غير قليلة لأنها قطعت في المدة المذكورة ربع الدور وهو زائد على مسافة حركة الفرس بما لا يحيط الوهم به ويكون حينئذ زيادة سكناته أي سكنات الفرس على حركاته كزيادة حركة المحدد على حركاته لأن عدد سكناته يساوي عدد زيادات حركة المحدد لا محالة وأنه أي زيادة حركة المحدد على حركاته ألف ألف مرة فتكون زيادة سكناته على حركاته أيضاً ألف ألف مرة فلا تظهر تلك الحركات القليلة في تلك السكنات الكثيرة مثل هذه الكثرة الغامرة لتلك القليلة فوجب الحركة أصلاً وهو باطل قطعاً، لأنا نحس بحركاته ولا نحس بشيء من سكناته. واعلم أن دلائل إبطال الجزء المبنية على تلازم الحركتين المتخالفتين بالسرعة والبطء، وهي قوله: (بنسبة غير قابلة) أي بنسبة لا يمكن توصيفه بالقلة لأنه فرع إحاطة الوهم بتلك النسبة. قوله: (لأنا نحس بحركاته ولا نحس بشيء من سكناته) وقد يجاب بأن السكون عندنا عدمي فلا نحس به والحركة وجودية فلذا نحس بها وفيه نظر إذ قد سبق أن السكون محسوس بالتبع وبالجملة قد يدرك بالحس عمى زيد واقطعيته، وليس أبعد منه أن يدرك حينئذ كون الفرس التي يعدو أشد عدواً في الزمان المتطاول في مكان واحد.
And slowness—i.e., not every slowness—is due to the interposition of rests between motions, otherwise the motion of a horse would not be perceived even if it is assumed very fast. The consequent is obviously false. The explanation of the connection: if slowness were only due to the interposition of rests between motions, then the difference in speed and slowness would be according to the difference in the intervening rests in fewness and multiplicity. So if a horse runs at full speed—as if it is estimated that it runs from the beginning of the day to its midpoint fifty leagues—its motion would be slower than the motion of the [celestial] sphere (al-muḥaddad) by no small ratio, because it has traversed in the mentioned period a quarter of the orbit, which is more than the distance of the horse's motion by what imagination cannot encompass. And then the increase of its rests—i.e., the rests of the horse—over its motions would be like the increase of the motion of the sphere over its motions, because the number of its rests necessarily equals the number of the increases of the sphere's motion. And that increase of the sphere's motion over its motions is a million times, so the increase of its rests over its motions would also be a million times. So those few motions would not appear among those many rests, given such overwhelming multiplicity over those few, so the motion would vanish entirely, which is absolutely false, because we perceive its motions and do not perceive any of its rests. And know that the proofs for the refutation of the atom based on the correlation of two motions differing in speed and slowness—and it is his saying: 'in a non-reciprocal ratio' i.e., a ratio that cannot be described as small because it is a branch of the imagination encompassing that ratio. His saying: 'for we perceive its motions and do not perceive any of its rests' – and it may be answered that rest (sukūn) for us is non-existential, so we do not perceive it, while motion is existential, so we perceive it. But there is consideration in this, since it has preceded that rest is perceived by way of concomitance. In sum, blindness of Zayd and his sitting may be perceived by sense, and it is not more far-fetched than that one would then perceive the horse that runs at full speed over a long period being in one place.
Source pages p. 261

Commentary
This passage refutes the claim that slowness (buṭ’) is solely caused by interposed rests (sakanāt) between motions. The argument is: if that were the case, then a fast horse running fifty leagues from dawn to midday would be slower than the celestial sphere (al-muḥaddad) by a huge ratio, because the sphere covers a quarter of its orbit in that time, which is vastly greater. The number of the horse's rests would equal the number of the sphere's excess distance (its motion over the horse's motion) which is enormous (a million times). Thus the horse's motions would be overwhelmed by the rests and become imperceptible, but we do perceive the horse's motion and not its rests. This disproves the claim. The passage then notes that this argument is part of proofs against atomism based on the correlation of two motions differing in speed. An objection is raised: rest is non-existential (ʿadamī), so it is not perceived, while motion is existential, so it is perceived. The response counters that rest is perceived by concomitance (e.g., we perceive Zayd's blindness or sitting). Therefore, the horse's situation would be perceived if it were stationary, contradicting observation. The passage thus reinforces the refutation of atomism and the correct understanding of motion and rest.

Key Terms
بطء
سكنات
حركة
فرس
محدد
عدم
وجود
جزء

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Slowness is always caused by interposed rests.
Correction: If that were so, the horse's motion would be imperceptible compared to the celestial sphere, but we observe motion, so slowness has other causes.

Checkpoint
What is the main conclusion of the argument about the horse and the celestial sphere?
A) Slowness is caused by many interposed rests
B) If slowness were only due to rests, a fast horse would appear motionless, which is false
C) The horse is faster than the celestial sphere
D) The celestial sphere has no motion

Key Takeaway
Al-Jurjani demonstrates that slowness cannot be solely due to interposed rests by showing that such a theory would make observable motion impossible.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
Have you ever held onto a belief about yourself or your faith that, if you followed it consistently, would lead to a contradiction with what you clearly know to be true? What would it cost you to abandon that belief and submit to reality?
?
Al-Jurjani uses undeniable observation (the horse's motion) to dismantle a flawed theory. What undeniable realities about your own spiritual state—such as your need for Allah, your sin, or your mortality—are you tempted to obscure with intellectual or emotional excuses? What would it mean to accept them fully today?
?
The argument shows that a false premise can make an entire worldview collapse. Is there any premise in your understanding of life that, if examined, would make your daily actions absurd or contradictory? Are you willing to test it against truth, even if it is uncomfortable?

The Shadow Proof: Speed and Slowness Without Intervening Rests
Iji presents the shadow proof to demonstrate continuous motion with varying speed.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand how Iji uses the motion of a shadow to prove that speed and slowness can occur without any intervening moments of rest.

Core Idea
Continuous motion can be slower without any resting points.

Iji begins with a simple observation: as the sun rises, the shadow of a stick decreases. By analyzing the relationship between the sun's motion and the shadow's motion, he builds a case that speed and slowness do not require discrete rests. This passage lays out the empirical grounding for a key Ash'ari position on motion.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage carefully
Read the Arabic passage once without analysing. Then read the translation. Notice the step-by-step logical progression from observation to conclusion.
2
Identify the scenario
Note the setup: a stick planted in the ground, sun rising, shadow decreasing. This is a familiar observable fact.
The shadow is long in the morning and shortens as the sun rises.
3
Follow the logical steps
The author considers two possibilities: if the shadow stops while the sun moves, that would imply a break, which is not observed. Then he shows that the shadow's motion is necessarily smaller than the sun's motion, hence slower without rest.
Key move: the shadow's motion is smaller in magnitude for each corresponding part.
4
Grasp the conclusion
The conclusion is that speed and slowness can exist without intervening rests (سكنات). This refutes the idea that slowness requires discrete stopping points.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
أنا إذا غرزنا خشبة في الأرض فإذا كانت الشمس في أفقها الشرقي وقع الظل في الجانب الغربي طويلا ولا يزال يتناقص الظل بحسب ازدياد ارتفاع الشمس إلى أن تبلغ الشمس غاية ارتفاعها وكلما ارتفع أي إذا ارتفع الشمس مقداراً إن وقف الظل ولم ينتقص أصلاً جاز ذلك في الثاني والثالث فيجوز حينئذ أن يتم الشمس الدورة والظل بحاله، وهو باطل وإن تحرك الظل جزاً كلما تحركت الشمس جزاً يمكن أن يكون هذان الجزآن متساويين في المقدار، ولا أن يكون جزء الظل أكبر بل وجب أن يكون أصغر، وحينئذ كان بإزاء كل حركة للشمس نحو الارتفاع حركة للظل نحو الانتقاص أقل من الحركة الارتفاعية في المقدار فتكون حركة الظل أبطأ بلا تخلل سكون فثبت أن السرعة والبطء بلا تخلل سكنات ويمكن المضايقة في قولهم لو جاز أن تتحرك الشمس جزاً والظل بحاله لجاز في الكل، وإذا كان كذلك جاز أن يتم الدورة والظل بحاله فإن ذلك جائز عندنا لأن جميع الموجودات مستندة إليه تعالى ابتداء بلا وجوب ولا إيجاب والعادة هي القاضية بعدمها من غير استحالة فيها عندنا وهي تستند إلى الفاعل المختار فيجوز أن يوجد حركة الشمس إلى إتمام الدورة ولا يوجد معها حركة الظل أصلاً، إلا أنّ عادته تعالى جرت بخلاف ذلك فما حكمتم باستحالته ليس بمحال، بل هو معدوم بقضاء العادة
If we plant a stick in the ground, then when the sun is in its eastern horizon, the shadow falls on the western side, long, and it continues to decrease as the sun rises until the sun reaches its highest altitude. And whenever the sun rises by a measure, if the shadow stops and does not decrease at all, that would be permissible in the second and third, then it would be permissible for the sun to complete the rotation while the shadow remains as it is, which is false. And if the shadow moves by a part whenever the sun moves by a part, it is possible that these two parts are equal in magnitude, and it is not possible for the part of the shadow to be larger, but rather it must be smaller. Then, for every motion of the sun towards rising, there is a motion of the shadow towards decreasing, less in magnitude than the rising motion. Thus the motion of the shadow is slower without any intervening rest. So it is established that speed and slowness occur without intervening rests. And it is possible to object to their statement: if it were permissible for the sun to move by a part while the shadow remains as is, then it would be permissible in all, and if so, it would be permissible for the sun to complete the rotation while the shadow remains as is. That is permissible for us, because all existents are directly dependent on Him, may He be exalted, without obligation or necessitation, and custom alone decrees their absence, without impossibility in it for us. And it is based on the free agent, so He may cause the sun's motion to complete the rotation while not causing any motion of the shadow at all, except that His custom, may He be exalted, has run contrary to that. So what you have judged to be impossible is not impossible, but rather is non-existent by the decree of custom.
Source pages p. 262

Commentary
This passage presents a proof from observable motion (the shadow of a stick) to establish that speed and slowness can occur without intervening moments of rest (تخلل سكنات). It begins by describing a familiar scenario: as the sun rises, the shadow of a stick decreases continuously. If the shadow were to stop at any point while the sun continues to rise, that would imply a break in the motion, but observation shows the shadow moves with each solar movement, and its motion is necessarily smaller in magnitude, hence slower without any intervening stillness. This refutes the idea that slowness necessarily involves discrete rests. The text then anticipates an objection: if one accepts that the sun could move while the shadow stays still for a part, then it could do so for the whole, leading to the absurdity of a full solar rotation with no shadow change. The author rejects this objection by appealing to Ash'ari occasionalism: all events are directly caused by God without intermediate natural necessities. The only reason the shadow normally tracks the sun is God's custom (عادة), not logical necessity. Therefore, it is not impossible for God to move the sun while keeping the shadow still, but He does not do so as a matter of established custom. The proof thus preserves the possibility of continuous motion without rests by grounding causality in divine will.

Key Terms
سرعة
بطء
تخلل
سكنات
ظل

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Slowness in motion must involve moments of rest or stoppage.
Correction: The shadow proof shows that a motion can be slower than another without any intervening rest; the slowness is due to smaller magnitude per unit time, not due to pauses.

Checkpoint
According to Iji's shadow proof, what would be false if the shadow stopped decreasing while the sun continued rising?
A) The shadow would later catch up.
B) The sun could complete its rotation while the shadow stayed the same.
C) The shadow would move faster.
D) The sun would stop moving.

Key Takeaway
Motion can be slower without any intervening rests; this is proven by the continuous decrease of a shadow compared to the sun's rise.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
When in your daily life have you assumed that a 'slow' spiritual growth means you are at a standstill? How does the shadow's continuous motion challenge you to see even the smallest progress as active movement from Allah?
?
The proof shows that the sun's motion and the shadow's motion are constantly and unceasingly linked. Is your connection to Allah like that—continuous and always responding, even when the change is slow? What would it mean to live with that awareness every moment?
?
Iji uses a simple observation to refute a false assumption. What observable signs in your own life have you ignored that would correct a false belief about Allah's action in the world? Will you commit to examining one such sign today?

Defending Ash'ari Occasionalism: The Objection and Response
Iji defends the Ash'ari position by refuting the objection that continuous motion implies absurdity.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand how Iji defends Ash'ari occasionalism against the objection that if the shadow could stay still while the sun moves, then the whole rotation could happen without shadow change, by appealing to divine custom.

Core Idea
God's custom, not logical necessity, governs the regularity of natural events.

After establishing that speed and slowness can occur without rests, Iji anticipates an objection: if we accept that the shadow could remain still for a single part of motion, why not for the entire rotation? He then provides the Ash'ari response: all events depend directly on God's will, and the regular correlation we observe is merely His custom, not a logical necessity. This passage clarifies the theological framework underpinning the proof.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage
Read the Arabic and translation. Notice the structure: an objection, then the Ash'ari response.
2
Identify the objection
The objection is that if one permits the shadow to be still for one part of the sun's motion, then it could be still for all parts, leading to an absurd consequence.
This objection targets the possibility of continuous motion without rest.
3
Understand the Ash'ari response
Iji replies that this consequence is not impossible because all existents are directly dependent on God without any intermediate necessity. The absence of shadow motion is not impossible, just contrary to God's custom.
Key term: عادة (custom) – the regular pattern God has established.
4
Distinguish between impossibility and non-occurrence
The objector claims impossibility, but Iji says it is merely non-existent due to custom. This is a crucial Ash'ari principle: God can do anything that is logically possible.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
أنا إذا غرزنا خشبة في الأرض فإذا كانت الشمس في أفقها الشرقي وقع الظل في الجانب الغربي طويلا ولا يزال يتناقص الظل بحسب ازدياد ارتفاع الشمس إلى أن تبلغ الشمس غاية ارتفاعها وكلما ارتفع أي إذا ارتفع الشمس مقداراً إن وقف الظل ولم ينتقص أصلاً جاز ذلك في الثاني والثالث فيجوز حينئذ أن يتم الشمس الدورة والظل بحاله، وهو باطل وإن تحرك الظل جزاً كلما تحركت الشمس جزاً يمكن أن يكون هذان الجزآن متساويين في المقدار، ولا أن يكون جزء الظل أكبر بل وجب أن يكون أصغر، وحينئذ كان بإزاء كل حركة للشمس نحو الارتفاع حركة للظل نحو الانتقاص أقل من الحركة الارتفاعية في المقدار فتكون حركة الظل أبطأ بلا تخلل سكون فثبت أن السرعة والبطء بلا تخلل سكنات ويمكن المضايقة في قولهم لو جاز أن تتحرك الشمس جزاً والظل بحاله لجاز في الكل، وإذا كان كذلك جاز أن يتم الدورة والظل بحاله فإن ذلك جائز عندنا لأن جميع الموجودات مستندة إليه تعالى ابتداء بلا وجوب ولا إيجاب والعادة هي القاضية بعدمها من غير استحالة فيها عندنا وهي تستند إلى الفاعل المختار فيجوز أن يوجد حركة الشمس إلى إتمام الدورة ولا يوجد معها حركة الظل أصلاً، إلا أنّ عادته تعالى جرت بخلاف ذلك فما حكمتم باستحالته ليس بمحال، بل هو معدوم بقضاء العادة
If we plant a stick in the ground, then when the sun is in its eastern horizon, the shadow falls on the western side, long, and it continues to decrease as the sun rises until the sun reaches its highest altitude. And whenever the sun rises by a measure, if the shadow stops and does not decrease at all, that would be permissible in the second and third, then it would be permissible for the sun to complete the rotation while the shadow remains as it is, which is false. And if the shadow moves by a part whenever the sun moves by a part, it is possible that these two parts are equal in magnitude, and it is not possible for the part of the shadow to be larger, but rather it must be smaller. Then, for every motion of the sun towards rising, there is a motion of the shadow towards decreasing, less in magnitude than the rising motion. Thus the motion of the shadow is slower without any intervening rest. So it is established that speed and slowness occur without intervening rests. And it is possible to object to their statement: if it were permissible for the sun to move by a part while the shadow remains as is, then it would be permissible in all, and if so, it would be permissible for the sun to complete the rotation while the shadow remains as is. That is permissible for us, because all existents are directly dependent on Him, may He be exalted, without obligation or necessitation, and custom alone decrees their absence, without impossibility in it for us. And it is based on the free agent, so He may cause the sun's motion to complete the rotation while not causing any motion of the shadow at all, except that His custom, may He be exalted, has run contrary to that. So what you have judged to be impossible is not impossible, but rather is non-existent by the decree of custom.

Commentary
This passage presents a proof from observable motion (the shadow of a stick) to establish that speed and slowness can occur without intervening moments of rest (تخلل سكنات). It begins by describing a familiar scenario: as the sun rises, the shadow of a stick decreases continuously. The Ash'ari Answer to the Continuous Cause Objection
Iji answers the objection that a continuous cause must produce continuous motion.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand why the Ash'ari school rejects the argument that a continuous cause necessitates continuous motion, and how the free agent (الفاعل المختار) allows for interruption.

Core Idea
A continuous cause does not necessitate continuous motion if the cause is a free agent who can choose to stop.

In this passage, al-Iji addresses an objection raised by philosophers who argued that since the cause of motion is continuously present throughout the path, the motion itself must be continuous without stops. The Ash'ari response, as presented here, challenges the assumption that the cause is continuous, pointing instead to the free will of God. Let's explore the reasoning.

Guided Path
1
Read the objection carefully
Read the first part of the passage where the philosophers' argument is stated: a continuous cause (natural or forced) must produce continuous motion, so rests cannot occur.
Notice they use the example of a stone moving through air.
2
Identify the Ash'ari response
Find where the author says 'the answer is...' and note that motion is based on the free agent, not on a continuous cause.
The free agent can move or stop at will.
3
Analyze the implications
Consider how this response undermines the objection: even if the medium is uniform, the cause (God's will) can be intermittent.
This affirms divine voluntarism.
4
Connect to broader kalam debates
Reflect on how this argument supports the Ash'ari doctrine of occasionalism and refutes the philosophers' necessary causal chains.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
ومنه يعلم جواب قولهم : علة الحركة مستمرة من أول المسافة إلى آخرها فكذا الحركة يعني أنهم استدلوا على بطلان تخلل السكنات في الحركة بأن علة حركة الحجر مثلا قسرية كانت أو طبيعية مستمرة الوجود من أول المسافة إلى آخرها والهواء قابل للانخراق بلا تفاوت فوجب أن تستمر تلك الحركة من غير أن يتخللها توقف وسكون في بعض الأحياز مع كونها أبطأ من الحركة الفلكية بلا شبهة فثبت البطء بلا تخلل السكنات والجواب إن تلك الحركة عندنا مستندة إلى الفاعل المختار لا إلى القاسر أو الطبيعة، فجاز أن يحرك الحجر في حيز ويسكنه في آخر مع تساويهما في قبول الحركة والسكون تنبيه : الاختلاف بالسرعة والبطء ليس اختلافاً بالنوع فإن الحركة الواحدة سريعة بالنسبة إلى حركة وبطيئة بالنسبة إلى أخرى مع أن ماهيتهما واحدة لا اختلاف فيها ولأنهما أي السرعة والبطء قابلان للاشتداد والتنقص فإن المسافة الواحدة يمكن قطعها بحركات مختلفة في مراتب السرعة والبطء، فلا يكونان فصلين للحركات لأن الفصول لا تقبل الاشتداد والتنقص
And from it is known the answer to their statement: 'The cause of motion is continuous from the beginning of the distance to its end, so likewise the motion.' That is, they argued for the falsity of intervening rests in motion by saying that the cause of the motion of a stone, for example, whether forced or natural, is continuously present from the beginning of the distance to its end, and the air is uniformly susceptible to being pierced, so that motion must continue without any pause or rest intervening in some positions, even though it is slower than celestial motion without doubt—thus slowness is established without intervening rests. The answer: that motion, for us, is based on the free agent, not on the forced [cause] or nature, so it is permissible for Him to move the stone in one position and bring it to rest in another, even though they are equal in their capacity to receive motion and rest. A note: Difference in speed and slowness is not a difference in species, because a single motion is fast relative to one motion and slow relative to another, while their essence is one with no difference in it; and because speed and slowness are subject to increase and decrease, for a single distance can be traversed by different motions in degrees of speed and slowness, so they are not differential for motions, since differential do not admit increase and decrease.
Source pages p. 263

Commentary
This passage contains two distinct parts. First, it presents and answers an objection: philosophers claimed that since the cause of motion (whether natural or forced) is continuously present throughout the entire path, the motion itself must be continuous without any intervening stops. They used this to argue that slowness can occur without rests, but the objection actually sought to prove that rests are impossible. The answer is that, according to the Ash'ari school, motion is not caused by a continuous natural cause but by the free agent (الفاعل المختار), who can move or stop the body at will at any point, regardless of the uniformity of the medium. Thus, the objector's premise (the cause is continuous) is not accepted because the true cause is God's will, which is not bound to continuity. Second, the passage contains a note (تنبيه) clarifying that speed and slowness are not specific differences (فصول) that distinguish species of motion. The proof is twofold: (1) the same motion can be called fast relative to one motion and slow relative to another, while its essence remains unchanged; (2) speed and slowness admit degrees of intensity, whereas specific differences do not. Therefore, the variation in speed and slowness is accidental, not essential.

Key Terms
علة
حركة
فاعل مختار
فاعل قاسر
طبيعة

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Ash'aris deny causality entirely.
Correction: No, they affirm that the true cause is God's will; the objection assumes a natural continuous cause, but Ash'aris reject that type of causality.

Checkpoint
Why does the Ash'ari author reject the argument that a continuous cause necessitates continuous motion without rests?
A) Because the cause is actually discontinuous.
B) Because motion is caused by the free agent (God) who can choose to stop it at any point.
C) Because the medium is not uniformly susceptible.
D) Because slowness requires rests by definition.

Key Takeaway
Motion can be interrupted because its cause is God's will, not a continuous nature.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
Do you truly believe that every movement in your life—even the smallest—is directly caused by Allah's will? If so, how does that change your reaction to delays or interruptions you experience?
?
When you face a situation that seems to slow down or stop, do you attribute that to natural causes or to the direct intervention of the Free Agent? How can you train yourself to see God's hand in every pause?
?
The philosophers assumed uniformity leads to continuity—do you sometimes assume that your circumstances should remain constant because the 'causes' are unchanged? What does this lesson teach you about the freedom of Allah to interrupt your plans?

Speed and Slowness Are Not Specific Differences of Motion
Iji notes that speed and slowness are not specific differences of motion.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand why speed and slowness are considered accidental attributes of motion rather than essential differences that define species of motion.

Core Idea
Speed and slowness are relative and admit degrees, so they cannot be specific differences.

The second part of this passage contains an important note (تنبيه) about the nature of speed and slowness. Many might assume that fast versus slow motion constitutes a difference in kind, but al-Iji explains why this is not the case. The reasoning has implications for how we understand categories of motion in Islamic theology.

Guided Path
1
Read the note carefully
Read the passage starting with 'تنبيه' and understand the claim: difference in speed/slowness is not a specific difference.
The author provides two proofs.
2
Examine the first proof
Notice that one and the same motion can be called fast relative to one and slow relative to another, while its essence remains the same.
This shows relativity.
3
Examine the second proof
Speed and slowness admit degrees of intensity (increase and decrease), which specific differences do not.
For example, the same distance can be traversed with varying speeds.
4
Reflect on the conclusion
Therefore, speed and slowness are accidental attributes, not essential differentiae that determine species of motion.
This aligns with the view that motion has a single essence.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
ومنه يعلم جواب قولهم : علة الحركة مستمرة من أول المسافة إلى آخرها فكذا الحركة يعني أنهم استدلوا على بطلان تخلل السكنات في الحركة بأن علة حركة الحجر مثلا قسرية كانت أو طبيعية مستمرة الوجود من أول المسافة إلى آخرها والهواء قابل للانخراق بلا تفاوت فوجب أن تستمر تلك الحركة من غير أن يتخللها توقف وسكون في بعض الأحياز مع كونها أبطأ من الحركة الفلكية بلا شبهة فثبت البطء بلا تخلل السكنات والجواب إن تلك الحركة عندنا مستندة إلى الفاعل المختار لا إلى القاسر أو الطبيعة، فجاز أن يحرك الحجر في حيز ويسكنه في آخر مع تساويهما في قبول الحركة والسكون تنبيه : الاختلاف بالسرعة والبطء ليس اختلافاً بالنوع فإن الحركة الواحدة سريعة بالنسبة إلى حركة وبطيئة بالنسبة إلى أخرى مع أن ماهيتهما واحدة لا اختلاف فيها ولأنهما أي السرعة والبطء قابلان للاشتداد والتنقص فإن المسافة الواحدة يمكن قطعها بحركات مختلفة في مراتب السرعة والبطء، فلا يكونان فصلين للحركات لأن الفصول لا تقبل الاشتداد والتنقص
And from it is known the answer to their statement: 'The cause of motion is continuous from the beginning of the distance to its end, so likewise the motion.' That is, they argued for the falsity of intervening rests in motion by saying that the cause of the motion of a stone, for example, whether forced or natural, is continuously present from the beginning of the distance to its end, and the air is uniformly susceptible to being pierced, so that motion must continue without any pause or rest intervening in some positions, even though it is slower than celestial motion without doubt—thus slowness is established without intervening rests. The answer: that motion, for us, is based on the free agent, not on the forced [cause] or nature, so it is permissible for Him to move the stone in one position and bring it to rest in another, even though they are equal in their capacity to receive motion and rest. A note: Difference in speed and slowness is not a difference in species, because a single motion is fast relative to one motion and slow relative to another, while their essence is one with no difference in it; and because speed and slowness are subject to increase and decrease, for a single distance can be traversed by different motions in degrees of speed and slowness, so they are not differential for motions, since differential do not admit increase and decrease.
Source pages p. 263

Commentary
This passage contains two distinct parts. First, it presents and answers an objection: philosophers claimed that since the cause of motion (whether natural or forced) is continuously present throughout the entire path, the motion itself must be continuous without any intervening stops. They used this to argue that slowness can occur without rests, but the objection actually sought to prove that rests are impossible. The answer is that, according to the Ash'ari school, motion is not caused by a continuous natural cause but by the free agent (الفاعل المختار), who can move or stop the body at will at any point, regardless of the uniformity of the medium. Thus, the objector's premise (the cause is continuous) is not accepted because the true cause is God's will, which is not bound to continuity. Second, the passage contains a note (تنبيه) clarifying that speed and slowness are not specific differences (فصول) that distinguish species of motion. The proof is twofold: (1) the same motion can be called fast relative to one motion and slow relative to another, while its essence remains unchanged; (2) speed and slowness admit degrees of intensity, whereas specific differences do not. Therefore, the variation in speed and slowness is accidental, not essential.

بطء
فصل
اشتداد
تنقص
ماهية

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Fast and slow motions belong to different species of motion.
Correction: No, the essence of motion is the same; speed and slowness are accidental attributes.

Checkpoint

A) A single motion can be fast relative to one and slow relative to another.
B) Speed and slowness admit increase and decrease.
C) The cause of speed and slowness is the free agent.
D) Specific differences do not admit degrees.

Key Takeaway
Speed and slowness are relative and admit degrees, so they are accidental, not essential, attributes of motion.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?

What specific practice can you start today to anchor your self-worth in the unchanging essence of faith rather than fluctuating performance?

The lesson states that specific differences do not admit increase or decrease. Philosophers' Classification of the Cause of Slowness
Iji presents the philosophers' view on the causes of slowness in natural, forced, and voluntary motions.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the philosophers' explanation for why motions are slow, distinguishing between natural motions (slowed by the medium) and forced/voluntary motions (slowed by the resistance of nature).

Core Idea
Slowness is caused by resistance: the medium for natural motions, nature itself for forced/voluntary motions.

The philosophers provide a systematic account of why motions occur at different speeds. They identify two distinct causes depending on the type of motion—natural or non-natural. This lesson unpacks their classification, using the example of a stone falling through air versus water.

Guided Path
1
Read the philosophers' statement
Read the Arabic and English passage to grasp the main claim and its structure.
Pay attention to the contrast between natural and other motions.
2
Identify the two types of motion
Note that slowness in natural motions is due to the resistance of the medium (مانعة المخروق), while in forced/voluntary motions it is due to the resistance of nature (ممانعة الطبيعة).
The philosophers treat these as distinct causes.
3
Examine the example
Consider the example of a stone descending in water versus air: the denser medium (water) offers more resistance, causing slower motion.
This illustrates how medium density affects speed in natural motion.
4
Note the commentator's remark
The passage ends with a note that the rest is a repetition from earlier material, included to acclimate the reader and remove imaginary objections.
This shows awareness of the reader's potential discomfort with the idea.
5
Reflect on the significance
This classification shows the philosophers' causal approach to explaining observable phenomena, distinguishing between natural and non-natural motions based on their causes.
The Ash'ari author reports this without necessarily endorsing the underlying causal necessity.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
قال الحكماء : علة البطء إما في الحركات الطبيعية فمانعة المخروق الذي في المسافة فكلما كان قوامه أغلظ كان أشد ممانعة للطبيعة وأقوى في اقتضاء بطء الحركة كالماء مع الهواء فنزول الحجر إلى الأرض في الماء أبطأ من نزوله إليها في الهواء. وإما في الحركات القسرية والإرادية فممانعة الطبيعة إما بدون حركتين مع الأخرى وباقي كلام المتن والشرح إعادة لما سبق لا حاجة إليه في إتمام الجواب، إلا أنه تركه لتألف النفس به ويزول عنه الاستبعاد الوهمي الناشئ من جريان العادة
The philosophers said: The cause of slowness: either in natural motions, then it is the resistance of the substance being pierced in the distance, so the denser its consistency, the stronger its resistance to nature and the more it necessitates slowness of motion, like water [compared] to air; thus the descent of a stone to the ground in water is slower than its descent to it in air. Or in forced and voluntary motions, then it is the resistance of nature, either without two motions with the other, and the rest of the text of the matn and the commentary is a repetition of what has preceded, no need for it in completing the answer, except that he left it to familiarise the soul thereby and to remove from it the imaginary improbability arising from the course of custom.

Commentary
This passage introduces the twelfth section of the observatory, presenting the philosophers' account of the cause of slowness. It classifies motions into two types. For natural motions (الحركات الطبيعية), slowness is caused by the resistance of the medium (مانعة المخروق) — the denser the medium, the slower the motion, as exemplified by a stone descending slower in water than in air. For forced and voluntary motions (الحركات القسرية والإرادية), the cause is the resistance of nature (ممانعة الطبيعة). The text then notes that the remainder of the discussion is a repetition of earlier material, included only to make the reader comfortable and remove the imaginary improbability arising from habitual custom. The core idea is that slowness has identifiable physical causes in the medium and in the resisting nature, distinguishing natural from non-natural motions.

Philosophers on Causes of Slowness in Motion
Philosophers classify causes of slowness in motion

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand how the philosophers distinguish the causes of slowness in natural motions versus compulsive and voluntary motions.

Core Idea
Slowness is not always due to the medium; it can also be due to the body's own nature.

The philosophers (الحكماء) offer a precise analysis of why motion is sometimes slow. They divide motions into natural and non-natural (compulsive/voluntary) and assign a different cause of slowness to each. The passage below presents their reasoning, using examples like a stone falling through water versus a heavy body resisting a push. Understanding this classification helps us see how the physical world operates according to consistent principles.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage and its translation
First, read the Arabic passage and the English translation side by side. Notice that the philosophers begin by stating the cause of slowness in general, then specify two cases.
The Arabic uses concise technical terms; the translation unpacks their meaning.
2
Identify the two categories of motion
The passage explicitly distinguishes natural motions (الحركات الطبيعية) from compulsive and voluntary motions (القسرية والإرادية). Underline these terms and note that each has a different cause of slowness.
Natural motions are those caused by the body's own nature (e.g., falling), while compulsive/voluntary motions are imposed externally.
3
Analyze the cause for natural motions
For natural motions, slowness is due to the resistance of the medium (المخروق). The thicker the medium, the greater the resistance and the slower the motion. The example given is water versus air.
This is intuitive: a stone falls slower in water than in air.
4
Analyze the cause for compulsive/voluntary motions
For these motions, slowness is due to the resistance of the body's own nature (ممانعة الطبيعة). The larger and more massive the body, the greater its inherent resistance to being moved, even if the medium is the same.
This explains why a huge rock is harder to push than a small pebble, even in the same fluid.
5
Reflect on the distinction
Consider why the philosophers separate these causes. They are making a logical point: slowness can arise either from external factors (the medium) or from internal factors (the body's nature). This classification is foundational for understanding motion in natural philosophy.
This lesson prepares you for later discussions on causality and the nature of bodies.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
قال الحكماء : علة البطء إما في الحركات الطبيعية فمانعة المخروق الذي في المسافة فكلما كان قوامه أغلظ كان أشد ممانعة للطبيعة وأقوى في اقتضاء بطء الحركة كالماء مع الهواء. وإما في الحركات القسرية والإرادية فممانعة الطبيعة وحدها وذلك أنه كلما كان الجسم أكبر مقداراً وكان الطبيعة السارية فيه أكبر وأعظم كان ذلك الجسم بطبيعته أشد ممانعة للقاسر والمحرك بالإرادة وأقوى في اقتضاء البطء وإن اتحد المخروق
The philosophers said: The cause of slowness is either in natural motions: the resistance of the pierced substance which is in the distance, so whenever its consistency is thicker, it is more resistant to nature and stronger in requiring slowness of motion, like water with air. And in compulsive and voluntary motions: the resistance of nature alone, and that is because whenever the body is greater in magnitude and the nature pervading in it is greater and mightier, that body by its nature is more resistant to the compeller and the voluntary mover and stronger in requiring slowness, even if the pierced substance is uniform.
Source pages p. 263

Commentary
This passage defines the cause of slowness according to the philosophers (الحكماء). It begins by classifying motions into two types: natural motions (الحركات الطبيعية), where slowness is caused by the resistance of the medium (المخروق) through which the body moves. The thicker the medium, the greater the resistance and the slower the motion, as in the example of a stone falling in water versus air. The second type is compulsive motions (القسرية) and voluntary motions (الإرادية), where slowness is caused by the resistance of the body's own nature (الطبيعة) against the external mover. The larger and more massive the body, the greater its inherent resistance to being moved, and thus the slower its motion, even if the medium (المخروق) is the same.

Key Terms

الإرادية
المخروق

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Slowness is always caused by the resistance of the medium (e.g., air or water).
Correction: The philosophers distinguish: for natural motions, yes; but for compulsive/voluntary motions, slowness is caused by the body's own nature resisting the mover, independent of the medium.

Checkpoint

Key Takeaway
The philosophers attribute slowness to two distinct causes: the medium's resistance in natural motions, and the body's own nature's resistance in compulsive and voluntary motions.

Equilibration of Resistances: Nature and Medium Combined
Explains how nature and medium resistances can balance, causing equal speed for different bodies

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand how the resistances of nature and medium can compensate for each other, causing two bodies of different sizes to move at the same speed under the same compeller.

Core Idea
A larger body's greater nature resistance can be offset by a less resistant medium, leading to equal speeds.

This passage delves into the combined effect of two types of resistance: the body's own nature and the medium it moves through. The examples of an arrow shot in air versus water illustrate how these resistances can counteract each other, producing equal speeds despite differences in size. This concept is crucial for understanding why slowness is not solely determined by size.

Guided Path
1
Introduce the two resistances
Identify the two types of resistance: mumani'at al-tabi'a (nature resistance) and mumani'at al-makhruq (medium resistance).
These are the key factors in the combined hindrance.
2
Examine the examples
Read the examples of an arrow shot in water vs. air, and a person walking in both media. Notice how the compeller force is the same in each case.
3
Understand the compensation mechanism
Realize that the larger body has more nature resistance, so it needs a less resistant medium (air) to balance. The smaller body has less nature resistance, so it can tolerate a more resistant medium (water).
The ratios of nature resistances and medium resistances must match for compensation.
4
Apply the concept
Consider how this shows that speed depends on net hindrance, not just size. The compeller’s force is constant, but the combined resistance determines motion.
This refines the earlier idea that slowness is due to resistance alone.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
أو ممانعة الطبيعة مع ممانعة المخروق كالسهم المرمي بقوة واحدة تارة في الماء وتارة في الهواء وكالشخص السائر فيهما بإرادته وربما عاوق أحدهما أكثر والآخر أقل فتعادلا يعني أن معاوقة طبيعة الجسم الأكبر أكثر من معاوقة طبيعة الأصغر، فإذا فرض أن معاوقة مخروق الأصغر أكثر من معاوقة مخروق الأكبر على تلك النسبة، انجبر التفاوت الذي بحسب الطبيعة وتعادل الجسمان في المعاوقة المركبة وتساويا في الحركة، مثل أن يحرك قاسر واحد الجسم الكبير في الهواء، والصغير في الماء الذي تزيد معاوقته على معاوقه الهواء بمقدار الزيادة التي في طبيعة الأكبر.
Or the resistance of nature together with the resistance of the pierced substance, like an arrow shot with one force sometimes in water and sometimes in air, and like a person walking in both with his will. And perhaps one of them hinders more and the other less, so they equilibrate. That means that the hindrance of the nature of the larger body is more than the hindrance of the nature of the smaller. So if it is supposed that the hindrance of the pierced substance of the smaller is more than the hindrance of the pierced substance of the larger in that proportion, the disparity according to nature is compensated, and the two bodies become equal in the combined hindrance and become equal in motion. For example, that one compeller moves the large body in air and the small body in water, whose hindrance exceeds the hindrance of air by the amount of the increase in the nature of the larger.
Source pages p. 264

Commentary
This passage expands on the cause of slowness in compulsive and voluntary motions by considering both nature resistance (ممانعة الطبيعة) and medium resistance (ممانعة المخروق) together. It gives examples: an arrow shot with the same force first in water then in air, and a person walking in both media with his will. The key point is that the two types of resistance can compensate for each other. The larger body has greater nature resistance but may move in a less resistant medium (air), while the smaller body has less nature resistance but may move in a more resistant medium (water). If the differences balance out, the combined hindrance (المعاوقة المركبة) becomes equal, and the two bodies move at the same speed. This shows that slowness depends on the net effect of both resistances.

Key Terms
ممانعة الطبيعة
ممانعة المخروق
المعاوقة المركبة
التعادل

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: A larger body always moves slower than a smaller body under the same force.
Correction: This passage shows that if the larger body is in a less resistant medium (e.g., air) and the smaller body in a more resistant medium (e.g., water), the nature and medium resistances can balance, making them move at the same speed.

Checkpoint

B) When the large body is in a more resistant medium and the small body in a less resistant medium
C) When the large body is in a less resistant medium and the small body in a more resistant medium
D) When the large body has a weaker nature

Key Takeaway
The combination of nature and medium resistances can equalize, allowing bodies of different sizes to move at the same speed.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
Consider the 'medium' of your life—your environment, habits, and influences. Is it compensating for your 'natural' spiritual weaknesses, giving you a false sense of progress? Or does your environment actually reveal your true state? What would happen if the medium were removed?

Philosophers on a State of Rest Between Ascent and Descent
Philosophers argue that a state of rest separates two opposite rectilinear motions.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the philosophers' claim that a state of rest necessarily occurs between two opposite rectilinear motions, such as ascent and descent, and why this claim is made.

Core Idea
Between opposite rectilinear motions, a state of rest is necessary.

This lesson introduces a key claim from the philosophers regarding motion: that between every two rectilinear motions moving in opposite directions, there must be an intervening state of rest. The example of a stone thrown upward and then falling back down illustrates this idea. We will explore the reasoning behind this claim.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage and identify the claim
Read the Arabic passage once without analysing. Then read the translation. Notice that the passage presents a specific claim about motion and rest.
The claim is made by Aristotle and al-Jubba'i.
2
Understand the example given
The example of a stone ascending forcefully then descending is used to illustrate the claim. Reflect on what 'forcefully' (qasran) implies about the motion.
3
Reflect on why this claim matters
Ask yourself: If there is no rest between ascent and descent, what would that imply? This claim is part of a larger debate about the nature of motion and causality.
This connects to the learning objective.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
ذهب بعض الحكماء كأرسطو وأتباعه والجبائي من المعتزلة إلى أن بين كل حركتين مستقيمتين كصاعدة وهابطة سكوناً فالحجر إذا صعد قسراً ثم رجع، فلا بد من سكون بينهما
Some philosophers, like Aristotle and his followers, and al-Jubba'i from the Mu'tazila, held that between every two rectilinear motions, such as ascending and descending, there is a state of rest. So when a stone ascends forcefully then returns, there must be a state of rest between them.
Source pages p. 264

Commentary
This passage introduces the thirteenth discourse (المقصد الثالث عشر), which presents the view of some philosophers, notably Aristotle and his followers, along with al-Jubba'i of the Mu'tazila. They assert that between any two rectilinear motions—for example, an ascending motion and a descending motion—there is an intervening state of rest (سكون). The concrete example is a stone that is thrown upward (ascending by force) and then falls back down (descending). According to this view, there must be a moment of rest at the peak between the two motions. This is a claim about the nature of motion and change, implying that a body cannot directly transition from one direction of motion to the opposite without an intermediate state of rest.

Key Terms
سكون
حركة مستقيمة
صاعدة
هابطة
قسراً

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: A projectile's motion from ascent to descent is continuous with no pause.
Correction: The philosophers claim that a state of rest is necessary between the two opposite motions.

Checkpoint

A) Aristotle and al-Jubba'i held that between ascent and descent there is a rest
B) The example of a stone ascending forcefully then returning is used
C) The rest occurs only if the motions are on a curved path
D) The rest is necessary between the two rectilinear motions

Key Takeaway
The philosophers assert that a state of rest necessarily intervenes between any two rectilinear motions moving in opposite directions, as illustrated by a stone's ascent and descent.

The Two Causes of Slowness in Motion
Explains the philosophers' cause of slowness and balancing of resistances.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the philosophers' explanation of slowness as a result of two resistances—the body's own nature and the medium—and how they can balance to produce equal motion.

Core Idea
Slowness comes from two resistances: nature and medium, which can balance out.

Philosophers observed that not all bodies move at the same speed even under the same force. They traced slowness to two sources of resistance: the body's inherent nature and the medium it travels through. This passage from al-Ījī presents their principle and shows how these resistances can offset each other.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage carefully
Begin by reading the full Arabic passage once, then its English translation. Notice the logical flow from principle to example to balancing scenario.
Pay attention to the key terms: طبيعة (nature) and مخروق (medium).
2
Identify the two resistances
The passage first describes how a body's size and nature cause resistance. Then it introduces the medium's resistance. List both factors and how they contribute to slowness.
The stone example illustrates only natural resistance; the arrow example illustrates only medium resistance.
3
Understand the balancing principle
The passage explains that the two resistances can balance each other. Imagine a large stone in air and a small stone in water—their speeds can become equal if the medium's extra resistance compensates for the larger body's natural resistance.
This balancing shows that slowness is not solely determined by size or nature alone.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
المرصد الرابع - المقصد الثاني عشر : قول الحكماء في علة البطء وحدها (و) ذلك أنه ( كلما كان الجسم أكبر) مقداراً (و) كان (الطبيعة) السارية فيه (أكبر) وأعظم ( كان ) ذلك الجسم بطبيعته ( أشد ممانعة ) للقاسر والمحرك بالإرادة وأقوى في اقتضاء البطء ) وإن اتحد المخروق ) والقاسر والمحرك الإرادي ومن ثمة كان حركة الحجر الكبير أبطأ من حركة الصغير في مسافة واحدة من قاسر واحد (أو) ممانعة الطبيعة ( مع ممانعة المخروق ( كالسهم المرمي بقوة واحدة تارة في الماء وتارة في الهواء وكالشخص السائر فيهما بإرداته ) وربما عاوق أحدهما أكثر والآخر أقل فتعادلا ) يعني أن معاوقة طبيعة الجسم الأكبر أكثر من معاوقة الأصغر، فإذا فرض أن معاوقة مخروق الأصغر أكثر من معاوقة مخروق الأكبر على تلك النسبة، انجبر التفاوت الذي بحسب الطبيعة وتعادل الجسمان في المعاوقة المركبة وتساويا في الحركة، مثل أن يحرك قاسر واحد الجسم الكبير في الهواء، والصغير في الماء الذي تزيد معاوقته على معاوقه الهواء بمقدار الزيادة التي في طبيعة الأكبر.
قوله : ( كالسهم المرمي إلخ مثال ممانعة المخروق فقط وليس مثالاً لممانعة الطبيعة والمخروق معاً، كما وهم واعترض بأن ليس فيه ممانعة الطبيعة لاتحاد المتحرك والمحرك في المثالين فإن مثل الممانعتين يحصل من جميع المثالين.
The fourth observation – the twelfth purpose: The philosophers' statement on the cause of slowness alone. (And) that is because (the greater the body) in size (and) (the greater) and more immense (the nature) flowing in it (is), (then) that body by its nature (is) (more resistant) to the compeller and the voluntary mover and stronger in requiring slowness) (even if the pierced medium) and the compeller and the voluntary mover are the same. Hence the motion of a large stone is slower than the motion of a small stone over one distance by one compeller. (Or) the resistance of nature (together with the resistance of the pierced medium) (like an arrow shot with one force sometimes in water and sometimes in air, and like a person walking in both by his will) (and perhaps one of them opposes more and the other less, so they balance). That is, the resistance of the nature of the larger body is greater than the resistance of the smaller. So if it is supposed that the resistance of the medium of the smaller is greater than the resistance of the medium of the larger by that proportion, the disparity due to nature is compensated, and the two bodies become equal in the combined resistance and equal in motion, such as when one compeller moves the large body in air and the small in water whose resistance exceeds the resistance of air by the amount of the increase in the nature of the larger. His statement: '(Like an arrow shot...)' is an example of the resistance of the medium only, not an example of the resistance of both nature and medium together, as some misunderstood and objected that there is no resistance of nature in it because the mover and moved are the same in both examples; indeed the like of the two resistances obtains from all the examples.
Source pages p. 264

Commentary
This passage presents the philosophers' explanation of the cause of slowness in motion. It begins by stating a principle: the larger a body's size and the greater its inherent nature (a principle of resistance), the stronger its resistance to a compeller or voluntary mover, and thus the greater its tendency to slowness, even if the medium and the mover are the same. It gives the example of a large stone moving slower than a small stone under the same compeller. Then it introduces a second factor: the resistance of the medium (e.g., water vs. air). The passage explains that these two resistances (nature and medium) can balance: the larger body's greater natural resistance can be offset by a more resistant medium for the smaller body, resulting in equal combined resistance and equal speed. Finally, a marginal note clarifies that the example of the arrow only illustrates medium resistance, not nature plus medium, correcting a misunderstanding.

ممانعة
طبيعة
مخروق
قاسر
محرك بالإرادة
البطء

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Some thought the arrow example demonstrates both the resistance of nature and the medium together.
Correction: The passage clarifies that the arrow example only shows medium resistance; the nature of the arrow remains constant, so it does not illustrate the combined effect.

Checkpoint
According to the philosophers in this passage, what two factors cause slowness in motion?
A) The size of the body and the force of the mover
B) The resistance of the body's nature and the resistance of the medium
C) The speed of the mover and the density of the medium
D) The weight of the body and the distance traveled

Key Takeaway
The philosophers attribute slowness to two resistances—nature and medium—which can balance to produce equal motion.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
What internal 'resistances'—like laziness, pride, or attachment to ease—are slowing your spiritual journey? Are you attributing that slowness entirely to your environment (medium) while ignoring your own nature? How will you adjust both starting today?
?
The philosophers taught that two resistances can cancel out to produce equal motion. In your life, where have you accepted a slow pace as inevitable, when you could instead change either your inner resolve or your external circumstances? What is one concrete step you will take this week to break that stalemate?
?
If slowness in seeking Allah can come from both your own nature and your surroundings, which one have you been neglecting to address? Will you commit to honestly examining that neglected area, and then act on it before the day ends?

The Claim of a Rest Between Straight Motions
Presents the affirmative view and its proof for a rest.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the argument that a rest necessarily occurs between two opposed straight-line motions.

Core Idea
Every straight-line motion must end, requiring a rest before change.

This lesson presents the view of Aristotle, his followers, and al-Jubba'i that a moment of rest separates any two straight-line motions in opposite directions, such as ascent and descent. The example of a stone thrown upward and falling back illustrates the intuition. The proof is based on the finitude of dimensions and the impossibility of infinite straight-line motion.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage and translation
Read the Arabic passage once, then the translation, noting the structure: claim, example, proof.
Pay attention to the logical connectors: 'because', 'so', 'either...or'.
2
Identify the core proof
The proof shows every straight motion must end because dimensions are finite. Three possibilities: stop, return on the same line, or turn to another line. In each case, a rest is needed.
The third option (turn) implicitly changes direction, still requires a rest.
3
Consider the opponents
Note that Plato and most Mu'tazilites denied this. Ask yourself: what might their counterargument be?
This sets up the next lesson on instantaneous arrival.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
المقصد الثالث عشر : قول الحكماء حول الصعود والهبوط ( ذهب بعض الحكماء كأرسطو وأتباعه ) والجبائي من المعتزلة إلى أن بين كل حركتين مستقيمتين كصاعدة وهابطة سكوناً ) فالحجر إذا صعد قسراً ثم رجع، فلا بد أن يسكن فيما بينهما (و) محصول ما ذكروه ( أنَّ كل حركة مستقيمة تنتهي ) البتة (إلى) سكون وذلك ) لأنها لا تذهب ) على الاستقامة ) إلى غير النهاية ) فإن الأبعاد متناهية، فإما أن تنقطع وهو ظاهر أو ترجع على سمتها أو تنعطف على سمت آخر وعلى التقديرين لا بد من سكون بين هاتين المستقيمتين فتكون الأولى منقطعة ( ومنعه غيرهم ) كأفلاطون من الحكماء وأكثر المتكلمين من المعتزلة ( وأما المثبتون فلكل من الفريقين في إثباته طريق فقال الحكماء : الوصول إلى المنتهى آني ) إذ لو كان زمانياً ففي النصف الأول من ذلك الزمان إن حصل الوصول فذلك النصف هو زمان الوصول لا كله وهو خلاف المفروض، وإن لم يحصل كان حاصلاً في الثاني ويعود المحذور والأظهر أن يقال : الحد الذي هو منتهى المسافة الممتدة لا النصف
The thirteenth purpose: The philosophers' statement on ascent and descent. (Some philosophers, like Aristotle and his followers) and al-Jubba'i from the Mu'tazila held that between every two straight-line motions, like ascending and descending, there is a rest. So if a stone ascends forcibly then returns, it must rest between them. (And) the gist of what they mentioned (is that every straight-line motion necessarily ends) (in) a rest, and that is (because it does not go) in a straight line (to infinity), for dimensions are finite. Either it stops, which is apparent, or it returns on its course or turns to another course; in both cases, a rest must occur between these two straight lines, so the first is terminated. (And others denied it), like Plato among the philosophers and most of the theologians from the Mu'tazila. (As for the affirmers, each of the two groups has a way of proving it. The philosophers said: arrival at the endpoint is instantaneous; for if it were temporal, then in the first half of that time, if arrival occurs, that half is the time of arrival, not the whole time, contrary to the assumption; and if it does not occur, then it occurs in the second half, and the same problem recurs. And the more apparent is to say: the limit which is the endpoint of the extended distance, not the half.)
Source pages p. 264

Commentary
This passage presents the thirteenth purpose, which discusses whether a moment of rest exists between two opposed straight-line motions, such as ascent and descent. It first states the view of some philosophers (Aristotle and his followers) and al-Jubba'i that a rest must occur between such motions, using the example of a stone forced upward then falling back. The proof is that every straight-line motion must end: it either stops, returns along its path, or turns to another direction, and in all cases a rest is needed. Opponents include Plato and most Mu'tazilite theologians. The passage then gives the philosophers' proof for the necessity of rest: arrival at the endpoint must be instantaneous. If it were temporal, a regress arises. Finally, a preferred formulation is given: the limit is the endpoint of the distance, not a half.

Key Terms
حركة مستقيمة
سكون
صعود
هبوط

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: One might think that change of direction can happen without any pause.
Correction: The passage argues that a rest must occur because straight motion is finite and must end before any new motion begins.

Checkpoint
According to the passage, what is the main reason a rest must occur between two straight-line motions?
A) The stone gets tired
B) Dimensions are finite
C) The body cannot change direction without a pause
D) The philosophers said so

Key Takeaway
The claim that a rest exists between straight motions is grounded in the finitude of dimensions and the need for an endpoint.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
The argument that every straight motion must end rests on the finitude of dimensions. Reflect on your own life: do you live as if your time, energy, and resources are finite? How does that awareness change your daily priorities?
?
The stone is forced upward and then falls – but it must rest at the peak before descending. In your spiritual journey, when you experience a 'peak' moment (success, joy) or a 'fall' (failure, grief), do you allow yourself a moment of rest to acknowledge Allah's hand? Or do you rush from one state to the next without pausing?
?
Some philosophers denied that a rest is necessary between motions. Consider an area of your life where you avoid stillness – constant work, distraction, or activity. What would happen if you deliberately inserted a moment of rest, trusting that it is a divinely ordained necessity for meaningful change?

Instantaneous Arrival at the Endpoint
Presents the philosophers' proof for instantaneous arrival and the preferred definition.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the philosophers' argument that arrival at the endpoint of motion must be instantaneous, and the preferred formulation regarding the limit.

Core Idea
If arrival were temporal, a regress occurs; thus it is instantaneous.

Following the claim that a rest exists, the philosophres provide a rigorous proof: the moment of reaching the endpoint cannot be a temporal duration. This lesson unpacks their argument and the preferred way to phrase the limit as the endpoint of distance, not a half of it.

Guided Path
1
Read the argument
Read the philosophers' proof that arrival at the endpoint is instantaneous. Note the conditional reasoning.
The argument uses a reductio ad absurdum: if temporal, then either half is contradictory.
2
Work through the reductio
Assume arrival is temporal. Then in the first half of that time, if arrival occurs, that half is the time of arrival, contradicting the whole. If not, then it occurs in the second half, and the half is now the time of arrival, again contradicting.
This shows no half can be the moment of arrival; therefore arrival must be instantaneous.
3
Understand the preferred formulation
The passage then refines: the limit is the endpoint of the extended distance, not a half. This avoids confusion about the temporal extent.
The endpoint is a point, not a segment; hence arrival at it is instantaneous.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
المقصد الثالث عشر : قول الحكماء حول الصعود والهبوط ( ذهب بعض الحكماء كأرسطو وأتباعه ) والجبائي من المعتزلة إلى أن بين كل حركتين مستقيمتين كصاعدة وهابطة سكوناً ) فالحجر إذا صعد قسراً ثم رجع، فلا بد أن يسكن فيما بينهما (و) محصول ما ذكروه ( أنَّ كل حركة مستقيمة تنتهي ) البتة (إلى) سكون وذلك ) لأنها لا تذهب ) على الاستقامة ) إلى غير النهاية ) فإن الأبعاد متناهية، فإما أن تنقطع وهو ظاهر أو ترجع على سمتها أو تنعطف على سمت آخر وعلى التقديرين لا بد من سكون بين هاتين المستقيمتين فتكون الأولى منقطعة ( ومنعه غيرهم ) كأفلاطون من الحكماء وأكثر المتكلمين من المعتزلة ( وأما المثبتون فلكل من الفريقين في إثباته طريق فقال الحكماء : الوصول إلى المنتهى آني ) إذ لو كان زمانياً ففي النصف الأول من ذلك الزمان إن حصل الوصول فذلك النصف هو زمان الوصول لا كله وهو خلاف المفروض، وإن لم يحصل كان حاصلاً في الثاني ويعود المحذور والأظهر أن يقال : الحد الذي هو منتهى المسافة الممتدة لا النصف
The thirteenth purpose: The philosophers' statement on ascent and descent. (Some philosophers, like Aristotle and his followers) and al-Jubba'i from the Mu'tazila held that between every two straight-line motions, like ascending and descending, there is a rest. So if a stone ascends forcibly then returns, it must rest between them. (And) the gist of what they mentioned (is that every straight-line motion necessarily ends) (in) a rest, and that is (because it does not go) in a straight line (to infinity), for dimensions are finite. Either it stops, which is apparent, or it returns on its course or turns to another course; in both cases, a rest must occur between these two straight lines, so the first is terminated. (And others denied it), like Plato among the philosophers and most of the theologians from the Mu'tazila. (As for the affirmers, each of the two groups has a way of proving it. And the more apparent is to say: the limit which is the endpoint of the extended distance, not the half.)

Commentary
This passage presents the thirteenth purpose, which discusses whether a moment of rest exists between two opposed straight-line motions, such as ascent and descent. It first states the view of some philosophers (Aristotle and his followers) and al-Jubba'i that a rest must occur between such motions, using the example of a stone forced upward then falling back. Misconception & Correction
Misconception: One might think that arrival at a point takes a finite time, however small.
Correction: The proof shows that any temporal duration leads to a contradiction because the endpoint is a point, not a segment.

?

Philosophers' Argument: Arrival at a Limit is Instantaneous
Philosophers' argument that arrival is instantaneous due to limit indivisibility and cause-effect simultaneity.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand why the philosophers argue that arrival at a limit must be instantaneous and how the cause of inclination (mīl) relates to that instant.

Core Idea
Arrival at a boundary is instantaneous because a limit is indivisible.

The philosophers (al-ḥukamā') present a logical argument that the arrival at the endpoint of motion cannot be a temporal process. They base this on the indivisibility of limits and the requirement that the cause exists simultaneously with its effect. This lesson unpacks the first part of their reasoning.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage carefully
Read the Arabic passage once to familiarise yourself with the argument. Then read the translation to grasp the logical flow.
Pay attention to the terms 'mīl' (inclination) and 'ān' (instant).
2
Identify the claim and its justification
Find where the philosophers state that arrival is instantaneous and the reason given: because the limit is indivisible.
The text says: 'فالوصول إليه آني إذ لو كان زمانياً لكان ذلك الحد منقسماً'
3
Understand the role of the cause (mīl)
The argument holds that the cause of arrival (the inclination) must exist at the instant of arrival. This is a causal principle: the cause must coexist with its effect.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
المرصد الرابع - المقصد الثاني عشر : قول الحكماء في علة البطء. هو يكون منقسماً في ذلك الامتداد وإلا لم يكن بتمامه حداً، فالوصول إليه آني إذ لو كان زمانياً لكان ذلك الحد منقسماً لتعلق الوصول به شيئاً فشيئاً، ثم إن للوصول علة الميل فوجب أن تكون هذه العلة موجودة في آن الوصول لأن العلة الموجدة يجب وجودها حال وجود المعلول وهذا هو المراد بقوله : ( فكذلك الميل الموجب له ) أي أيضاً موجود في ذلك الآن مع حدوثه في آن ابتداء الحركة واستمراره إلى انتهائها ( والرجوع ) عن المنتهى أيضاً ( آني ( كالوصول ( فكذلك الميل الموجب له آني ) أي حادث في آن ) وأن الوصول غير أن الرجوع لامتناع اجتماعهما، فلو لم يكن بينهما زمان لزم تتالي الآنات وتركب الزمان منها ( وأنه باطل ) إذ يلزم حينئذ تركب الحركة من أجزاء لا تتجزأ، فيلزم تركب المسافة أيضاً منها ( فذلك الزمان لا حركة فيه ) لا إلى المنتهى، ولا عنه فهو (سكون أي زمان سكون ) والجواب أن الوصول في آن هو قوله : ( والرجوع إلخ ) لأنه عبارة عن رفع الوصول ورفع الأين وفيه ما فيه .
The Fourth Observatory - The Twelfth Objective: The Philosophers' Statement on the Cause of Slowness. It is divisible in that extension, otherwise it would not be a limit in its entirety. Thus arrival at it is instantaneous, for if it were temporal, that limit would be divisible due to the attachment of arrival to it bit by bit. Then arrival has a cause of inclination (mīl), so it is necessary that this cause exist in the instant of arrival, because the efficient cause must exist at the time of the effect. This is what is meant by his saying: 'So likewise the inclination that necessitates it' – that is, it also exists in that instant, with its occurrence in the instant of the beginning of motion and its continuation until its end. And the return from the endpoint is also instantaneous like the arrival, so likewise the inclination that necessitates it is instantaneous – that is, occurring in an instant. And that arrival is different from return due to the impossibility of their coexistence. So if there were no time between them, the succession of instants (tātālī al-ānāt) would necessarily follow, and the composition of time from them – and that is false – for then the composition of motion from indivisible parts would necessarily follow, and consequently the composition of distance from them. So that time has no motion in it, neither towards the endpoint nor away from it; it is rest (sukūn), i.e., a time of rest. The answer: that arrival is in an instant is his saying: 'And the return, etc.' because it is an expression of removing arrival and removing the 'where' (al-ayn), and there is something in it.
Source pages p. 266

Commentary
This passage presents the philosophers' (ḥukamā') argument regarding the cause of slowness in motion, followed by a response. The argument begins by asserting that the arrival at a limit (such as the endpoint of motion) must be instantaneous, not temporal. If it were temporal, the limit would be divisible, which is not possible. The limit, as a boundary, is indivisible. Then it states that the cause of the inclination (mīl) that brings about the motion must exist at the instant of arrival, because a cause must coexist with its effect. That inclination is also instantaneous, arising at the start of motion and continuing to its end. Similarly, the return from the endpoint is instantaneous. Since arrival and return cannot coexist, there must be a time between them; otherwise, consecutive instants would compose time, leading to the falsehood that motion and distance are composed of indivisible parts (atomism). Therefore, that intermediate time contains no motion – it is a time of rest. The response provided (al-jawāb) clarifies that the arrival is indeed instantaneous, as it is a boundary (ṭaraf) of motion, and analogously the return is also instantaneous. The answer notes that this explanation has its own difficulties ('fīhi mā fīhi').

Key Terms
ميل (mīl)
آني (instantaneous)
آن (instant)
حد (limit)

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Some might think that reaching a point always takes time, but the philosophers argue that reaching a limit (boundary) is atemporal because limits are not extended.
Correction: The passage shows that the limit itself is indivisible, so arrival cannot be temporal; it must occur in a single instant.

Checkpoint
Why do the philosophers say that arrival at a limit must be instantaneous?
A) Because motion is continuous
B) Because the limit is indivisible
C) Because the cause must be temporal
D) Because the return is also instantaneous

Key Takeaway
The philosophers establish that arrival at a boundary is instantaneous because limits are indivisible and the cause of motion must coexist with its effect in the same instant.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
If every arrival at a limit is instantaneous, what does that imply about the moments of decision or repentance in your life? Can you recall a time when you reached a moral limit and the change was indivisible, not gradual?
?
The philosophers argue that the cause (mīl) must exist at the instant of arrival. What 'inclinations' are present in you at the moment you cross a boundary into sin or righteousness? Are you aware of the cause that exists in that instant, and do you take responsibility for it?
?
The limit is indivisible; there is no 'almost arriving' temporally. How does this truth about physical limits challenge you spiritually? Are you living as if arrival at the limit of death or judgment is instantaneous, or do you assume you have time to prepare? What needs to change today?
