Philosophers' Argument: Return and the Response
Philosophers' argument that return is instantaneous and the response noting difficulties.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the philosophers' argument that return is also instantaneous and the response that acknowledges the difficulties.

Core Idea
Return is instantaneous like arrival, but the response highlights the problems with this view.

Having established that arrival is instantaneous, the philosophers extend the same reasoning to the return from the endpoint. They argue that if there is no time between arrival and return, time would be composed of successive instants, leading to atomism. The response, however, points out the difficulties in this interpretation.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage carefully
Read the Arabic passage and its translation. Note the parallel structure with the arrival argument.
2
Understand the argument for instantaneous return
The philosophers claim that the return is also instantaneous, and its cause (mīl) is likewise instantaneous.
3
Examine the implication of no time between arrival and return
If no time separates them, successive instants would compose time, leading to the falsehood of motion being composed of indivisible parts (atomism).
The text says: 'فلو لم يكن بينهما زمان لزم تتالي الآنات'
4
Understand the response
The response clarifies that arrival being instantaneous means it is a boundary, and similarly return. But the phrase 'fīhi mā fīhi' indicates the response is not without its own problems.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
المرصد الرابع - المقصد الثاني عشر : قول الحكماء في علة البطء. هو يكون منقسماً في ذلك الامتداد وإلا لم يكن بتمامه حداً، فالوصول إليه آني إذ لو كان زمانياً لكان ذلك الحد منقسماً لتعلق الوصول به شيئاً فشيئاً، ثم إن للوصول علة الميل فوجب أن تكون هذه العلة موجودة في آن الوصول لأن العلة الموجدة يجب وجودها حال وجود المعلول وهذا هو المراد بقوله : ( فكذلك الميل الموجب له ) أي أيضاً موجود في ذلك الآن مع حدوثه في آن ابتداء الحركة واستمراره إلى انتهائها ( والرجوع ) عن المنتهى أيضاً ( آني ( كالوصول ( فكذلك الميل الموجب له آني ) أي حادث في آن ) وأن الوصول غير أن الرجوع لامتناع اجتماعهما، فلو لم يكن بينهما زمان لزم تتالي الآنات وتركب الزمان منها ( وأنه باطل ) إذ يلزم حينئذ تركب الحركة من أجزاء لا تتجزأ، فيلزم تركب المسافة أيضاً منها ( فذلك الزمان لا حركة فيه ) لا إلى المنتهى، ولا عنه فهو (سكون أي زمان سكون ) والجواب أن الوصول في آن هو قوله : ( والرجوع إلخ ) لأنه عبارة عن رفع الوصول ورفع الأين وفيه ما فيه .
The Fourth Observatory - The Twelfth Objective: The Philosophers' Statement on the Cause of Slowness. It is divisible in that extension, otherwise it would not be a limit in its entirety. Thus arrival at it is instantaneous, for if it were temporal, that limit would be divisible due to the attachment of arrival to it bit by bit. Then arrival has a cause of inclination (mīl), so it is necessary that this cause exist in the instant of arrival, because the efficient cause must exist at the time of the effect. This is what is meant by his saying: 'So likewise the inclination that necessitates it' – that is, it also exists in that instant, with its occurrence in the instant of the beginning of motion and its continuation until its end. And the return from the endpoint is also instantaneous like the arrival, so likewise the inclination that necessitates it is instantaneous – that is, occurring in an instant. And that arrival is different from return due to the impossibility of their coexistence. So if there were no time between them, the succession of instants (tātālī al-ānāt) would necessarily follow, and the composition of time from them – and that is false – for then the composition of motion from indivisible parts would necessarily follow, and consequently the composition of distance from them. So that time has no motion in it, neither towards the endpoint nor away from it; it is rest (sukūn), i.e., a time of rest. The answer: that arrival is in an instant is his saying: 'And the return, etc.' because it is an expression of removing arrival and removing the 'where' (al-ayn), and there is something in it.
Source pages p. 266

Commentary
This passage presents the philosophers' (ḥukamā') argument regarding the cause of slowness in motion, followed by a response. The argument begins by asserting that the arrival at a limit (such as the endpoint of motion) must be instantaneous, not temporal. If it were temporal, the limit would be divisible, which is not possible. The limit, as a boundary, is indivisible. Then it states that the cause of the inclination (mīl) that brings about the motion must exist at the instant of arrival, because a cause must coexist with its effect. That inclination is also instantaneous, arising at the start of motion and continuing to its end. Similarly, the return from the endpoint is instantaneous. Since arrival and return cannot coexist, there must be a time between them; otherwise, consecutive instants would compose time, leading to the falsehood that motion and distance are composed of indivisible parts (atomism). Therefore, that intermediate time contains no motion – it is a time of rest. The response provided (al-jawāb) clarifies that the arrival is indeed instantaneous, as it is a boundary (ṭaraf) of motion, and analogously the return is also instantaneous. The answer notes that this explanation has its own difficulties ('fīhi mā fīhi').

Key Terms
تتالي الآنات (succession of instants)
سكون (rest)
آن (instant)

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to the philosophers, what would happen if there were no time between arrival and return?
A) Motion would be continuous
B) Succession of instants would compose time
C) The limit would be divisible
D) The cause would not exist

Key Takeaway
The philosophers argue that return is instantaneous, but the gap between arrival and return must be filled by rest; the response points to the difficulty of this conception.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
When you achieve a major goal, do you allow yourself a genuine moment of rest before moving on to the next thing, or do you demand an instantaneous return to activity? What fear drives that urgency?
?
The philosophers' argument reveals that even between two seemingly instantaneous events there is a necessary interval of rest. Where in your spiritual life have you tried to skip the rest between arrival and return—perhaps between repentance and the next sin, or between worship and worldly engagement? What would change if you honoured that rest?
?
The response admits difficulties ('fīhi mā fīhi'). When you encounter a difficulty in your own understanding of God's action or causality, do you quickly dismiss it with a surface answer, or do you sit in the unsettledness and let it deepen your inquiry? Name one belief about how God acts that you have not examined deeply.

Consecutive Instants: Mental vs. External Necessity
Al-Katibi's response and the limit/part distinction.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the objection of consecutive instants and the distinction between mental and external necessity in kalam arguments.

Core Idea
Consecutive instants are problematic only externally, not in the mind.

This lesson examines Al-Katibi's response to the objection that motion over an instant would require a succession of instants. Al-Katibi argues that the necessity of instants is only mental, not external. However, the commentator rebuts that even mental succession leads to the problem of indivisible parts. We will also explore the crucial distinction between an instant as a limit (ṭaraf) and an instant as a part (juz').

Guided Path
1
Read the objection and Al-Katibi's response
Identify the objection that consecutive instants would follow, and see how Al-Katibi distinguishes between mental and external necessity.
Pay attention to the phrase 'لزوم الآنات في الخارج ممنوع'.
2
Follow the commentator's rebuttal
Understand why the commentator argues that even mental succession leads to a problem of indivisible parts, using the example of a body moving in two mental instants.
3
Analyze the limit vs. part distinction
Learn how the instant as a limit avoids the problem of composition, analogous to a point in a line.
This distinction is critical for the subsequent arguments.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
قوله : (لزم تتالي الآنات) أجاب عنه الكاتبي بما حاصله أن لزوم الآنات في الخارج ممنوع وإنما يلزم فيه أن لو كان الآن موجوداً في الخارج وهو ممنوع ولزومه في الذهن مسلم لكن استحالته ممنوعة إنما المستحيل تتالى الآنات في الخارج ورده الشارح بأنه إذا تتالى آنات في الذهن فلنفرض أن جسماً قد تحرك فيهما على مسافة فيلزم انقسام الحركة إلى جزأين لا ينقسمان أصلاً، وكذا انقسام المسافة إليهما، فإما أن يكون الجزآن في المسافة بالفعل فيلزم الجزء بالفعل وإما بالقوة فيلزم الجزء بالقوة، فكما أن ترك الأمر الممتد من الأجزاء الممتنعة الانقسام في الخارج ممنوع فكذا تركيبه منها في الذهن لا يقال : إذا لم يكن الآن موجوداً في الخارج لا يكون مجموع الآنين موجوداً فيه، فلا يصح وقوع الحركة فيه فلا يتم ما ذكرتم لأنا تقول : إذا فرض تتالي الآنين يكون ذلك المجموع زماناً، والزمان سواء كان موجوداً أو موهوماً يجوز وقوع الحركة فيه قطعاً. قوله: (فيلزم تركب المسافة أيضاً منها وأما إذا تحقق الآن ولم يتال فلا يلزم هذا المحذور لأن الآن طرف للزمان وهو عرض قائم به غير حال فيه حلول السريان والمنطبق على المسافة هو المحل، فلا يلزم من انطباقه عليها محذور وهذا كما أن ثبوت النقطة لا يستلزم الجزء وكون الخط متألفاً عن النقط يستلزمه . قوله : (فهو (سكون قالوا: وهذا السكون ليس من مقتضيات الطبيعة، فإنها تقتضي الحركة إلى الحالة الملائمة لها وهذا السكون لا يلائمها لأنه في الحيز القريب بل الميل القسري لا طرف حركة) متوجهة نحو المنتهى ) والرجوع في آن هو طرف حركة) منصرفة عنه ( فلم لا يجوز أن يكون ) آن واحد ( حداً مشتركاً بينهما ) أي بين الحركتين بل بين زمانيهما، فإن الطرف الواحد يجوز أن يكون مشتركا بين شيئين كالنقطة الواحدة المشتركة بين خطين بخلاف الجزء ولذلك قال : ( وأما الآن بمعنى جزء زماني ينقسم ذلك الجزء ( فأنتم لا تقولون (به حتى يمتنع اشتراكه بين زمان الحركتين ( قولكم أن الرجوع غير أن الوصول قلنا : نعم بينهما تغاير (لكن) لا بالذات بل باعتبار كونه منتهى لزمان الحركة الموصلة ومبدأ لزمان حركة الرجوع واعلم أن الحجة المشهورة للمثبتين من الحكماء هي أن المتحرك إلى المنتهى إنما يصل إليه في آن، وإذا تحرك عنه بعد كونه واصلاً إليه، فلا محالة يصير مفارقاً ومبايناً له في آن أيضاً ، ولا يمكن اتحاد الآنين وإلا كان واصلاً إلى المنتهى ومبايناً له معاً، فوجب تغايرهما بالذات واستحال تتاليهما بلا تخلل زمان بينهما لاستلزامه القول بالجزء، وذلك الزمان زمان سكون إذ لا حركة هناك لا إلى ذلك الحد ولا عنه، وأبطلها ابن سينا بأن المفارقة والمباينة هي حركة الرجوع فهناك :آنان آن يقع فيه ابتداء الرجوع والمباينة، وآن يصدق فيه على المتحرك أنه مفارق مباين لذلك الحد الذي هو المنتهى، فإن عنوا بأن المباينة طرف زمان المباينة نختار أن ذلك الآن هو بعينه آن الوصول بأن يكون حداً مشتركاً بین زمان الحركتين، فإن طرف الحركة يجوز أن يكون شيئاً ليس فيه حركة أصلاً، وإن عنوا به آناً يصدق فيه على المتحرك أنه راجع مباین نختار أنه مغاير لآن الوصول، وإن بين الآنين زماناً لكنه ليس زمان السكون بل زمان الحركة، وهو بعض حركة الرجوع فإن كل آن يفرض في زمان وقع فيه حركة قوله : ( وأبطلها إلخ) ونقض بأن هذه الحجة بعينها جارية في الحدود المفروضة في المسافة التي تقطعها حركة واحدة حركة الرجوع أو سببها . قوله : ( يكون بينه وبين إلخ ) بناء على أن الحركة ليس له أول حدوث، إذ لا يوجد إلا زمان وإلا بعد زمان إذ هي مقتضية لأين لم يكن الجسم قبله فيه ولا يكون بعده فيه، فيقتضي تقدماً وتأخراً زمانياً . كما أفاد قوة التحريك إلى الحد المعين كذلك أفاد قوة التسكين في ذلك الحد ثم الطبيعة بشرط السكون في ذلك الحد تحدث بعد ذلك ميلاً ومدافعة إلى جهة ليسفل، فيحدث الحركة إليه قال المولى العلامة شمس الملة والدين محمد بن مبارك شاه البخاري الأشبه أن هذا السكون قسري والقاسر امتناع تتالي الآنات إذ الضرورة الطبيعية مثل ضرورة الخلاء وغيرها كثيراً ما تقتضي أموراً استبعدها العقل.
His saying: (The succession of instants would necessarily follow). Al-Katibi answered it with the gist that the necessity of instants in the external world is denied; rather it would be necessary only if the instant existed externally, which is denied. And its necessity in the mind is admitted, but its impossibility is denied; only the succession of instants in the external world is impossible. The commentator rebutted that if instants succeed one another in the mind, let us suppose that a body moved in them over a distance; then motion would be divided into two indivisible parts, and likewise the distance into them. Then either the two parts exist actually in the distance, necessitating an actual part, or potentially, necessitating a potential part. So just as the composition of an extended thing from parts indivisible externally is denied, so is its composition from them in the mind. It cannot be said: if the instant does not exist externally, the sum of two instants does not exist in it, so motion cannot occur in it, so what you mentioned is not established. For we say: if the succession of two instants is assumed, that sum is a time; and time, whether existent or imaginary, absolutely admits the occurrence of motion in it. His saying: (Therefore the composition of the distance from them would follow). But if the instant is actual and not successive, this danger does not follow, because the instant is a limit of time, and it is an accident subsisting in it, not in the manner of permeation. What is applied to the distance is the locus, so no danger follows from its application. This is like the fact that the fixing of a point does not necessitate a part, but the composition of a line from points would necessitate it. His saying: (So it is rest). They said: This rest is not a requirement of nature, for nature requires motion to the state suitable for it, and this rest is not suitable for it because it is in the near place; rather it is compulsory inclination (mīl qasrī). (It is a limit of motion) directed towards the endpoint. And the return in an instant is a limit of motion turning away from it. So why is it not allowed that one instant be a common limit between them – i.e., between the two motions, rather between their times? For a single limit may be shared between two things, like the single point shared between two lines, unlike the part. Therefore he said: (As for the instant in the sense of a temporal part, that part is divisible; you do not assert it) so that its sharing between the times of the two motions is prevented. Your saying that the return is other than the arrival; we say: yes, there is difference between them, but not in essence, rather in consideration of it being the end of the time of the conveying motion and the beginning of the time of the return motion. Know that the famous proof of the affirmative philosophers is that the mover towards the endpoint arrives at it in an instant, and when it moves away from it after having arrived, it necessarily becomes separate and distinct from it in an instant as well. The two instants cannot be identical, otherwise it would be both arriving and distinct simultaneously. So they must differ in essence, and their succession without an intervening time is impossible because it would necessitate the [atomistic] part. That time is a time of rest, since there is no motion there, neither towards that limit nor away from it. Ibn Sina refuted this by [saying] that separation and distinctness are the return motion; there are two instants: an instant in which the beginning of return and separation occurs, and an instant in which the mover is truly described as separate and distinct from that limit which is the endpoint. If they mean by distinctness the limit of the time of distinctness, we choose that that instant is exactly the instant of arrival, being a common limit between the times of the two motions; for the limit of motion may be something in which there is no motion at all. And if they mean by it an instant in which the mover is truly described as returning and distinct, we choose that it is different from the instant of arrival, and there is time between the two instants, but it is not time of rest; rather it is time of motion, and it is part of the return motion, for every instant assumed in time occurs during motion. His saying: (And he refuted it, etc.) And it was refuted by [saying] that this very argument applies to the limits assumed in the distance that a single motion traverses, whether the motion is of return or its cause. His saying: (There is between it and, etc.) is based on the fact that motion has no first occurrence, for it only exists in time or after time, since it requires a 'where' that the body was not in before and will not be in after, so it implies temporal priority and posteriority. Just as the power of moving towards a specific limit provides the power of stopping at that limit, then nature, conditioned by rest at that limit, afterward generates inclination and impetus towards the lower direction, so motion towards it occurs. The lord, the most learned, Shams al-Milla wa al-Din Muhammad ibn Mubarak Shah al-Bukhari, said: it is more plausible that this rest is compulsory, and the compeller is the impossibility of successive instants, for natural necessity, like the necessity of a vacuum and others, often requires things that reason deems unlikely.
Source pages p. 266

Commentary
This passage continues the discussion on the philosophers' argument by addressing objections and presenting Ibn Sina's refutation. It begins with an objection regarding the necessary succession of instants (tātālī al-ānāt). Al-Katibi responds that the necessity of instants exists only in the mind, not externally; the commentator rebuts that even mental succession leads to problems of parts. Then it discusses that if the instant is a limit, not a part, the problem of composition from parts is avoided, analogously to a point in a line. The passage then presents the famous proof (al-ḥujja al-mashhūra) of the philosophers: the mover arrives in an instant, moves away in a different instant, and the two cannot be identical; hence there must be a time of rest between. Ibn Sina refutes this by distinguishing the instant as boundary (ṭaraf) and the instant as description (ṣidq). He argues that the instant of arrival can serve as a common limit for both motions, avoiding the need for rest. The passage also includes further critiques about the nature of rest and a concluding remark from Shams al-Din al-Bukhari that rest is compulsory due to the impossibility of successive instants.

Key Terms
تتالي الآنات
آن
حد
جزء
خارج
ذهن

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Consecutive instants are impossible both mentally and externally.
Correction: Al-Katibi argues they are only impossible externally; mental succession is admitted, though the commentator questions that.

Checkpoint
According to the passage, what problem does considering the instant as a limit (ṭaraf) avoid?
A) It avoids the need for time altogether.
B) It avoids the composition of distance from indivisible parts.
C) It avoids the existence of the instant in the mind.
D) It avoids the succession of instants.

Key Takeaway
Consecutive instants are problematic only when considered as parts, not as limits in the mind.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
Have you ever been certain of something in your mind—like a conclusion about Allah's attributes or the nature of creation—only to later realize it was based on an assumption that did not hold in external reality? How will you train yourself to test your mental constructs against the Qur'an and Sunnah before treating them as certain truth?
?
The commentator warns that even a mental assumption (like successive instants) can lead to logical contradictions if followed through. What assumption in your own aqeedah might you be holding onto without examining its implications? Are you willing to let go of it if it leads to error?
?
This lesson distinguishes between time as something that can be imaginary yet still contain motion. If time can be simply a mental construct, how does that affect your view of your daily routines and deadlines? Are you treating time as an independent reality rather than a creation of Allah under His control? What would change if you lived each moment knowing that time itself is subject to Allah's will?

Ibn Sina's Refutation: The Instant as a Common Limit
Ibn Sina's refutation of the rest argument.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand Ibn Sina's refutation of the philosophers' argument for a time of rest, using the concept of the instant as a common limit.

Core Idea
The instant of arrival can be a common limit for both motions, eliminating the need for rest.

This lesson presents the famous proof of the philosophers that there must be a time of rest between arrival and return, and Ibn Sina's powerful refutation. Ibn Sina argues that the instant of arrival, when considered as a boundary (limit), can be shared between the two motions, so no rest interval is required. We will also explore the distinction between an instant as a boundary and as a part, and the further critique about the nature of rest.

Guided Path
1
Review the philosophers' proof
Read the proof that the mover arrives in an instant, leaves in another, and since they cannot be identical, there must be a time of rest between.
This proof is presented as 'al-ḥujja al-mashhūra'.
2
Examine Ibn Sina's refutation
Notice how Ibn Sina distinguishes between the instant as a boundary (limit) of time and the instant as a description. He argues that the instant of arrival can serve as a common limit for both motions.
Key phrase: 'حداً مشتركاً بين زمان الحركتين'.
3
Understand the shared limit concept
Like a point shared between two lines, a single instant can be the end of one motion and the beginning of the other.
4
Consider the nature of rest
The passage ends with a discussion that this rest may be compulsory, due to the impossibility of consecutive instants.
Shams al-Din al-Bukhari's opinion.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
قوله : (لزم تتالي الآنات) أجاب عنه الكاتبي بما حاصله أن لزوم الآنات في الخارج ممنوع وإنما يلزم فيه أن لو كان الآن موجوداً في الخارج وهو ممنوع ولزومه في الذهن مسلم لكن استحالته ممنوعة إنما المستحيل تتالى الآنات في الخارج ورده الشارح بأنه إذا تتالى آنات في الذهن فلنفرض أن جسماً قد تحرك فيهما على مسافة فيلزم انقسام الحركة إلى جزأين لا ينقسمان أصلاً، وكذا انقسام المسافة إليهما، فإما أن يكون الجزآن في المسافة بالفعل فيلزم الجزء بالفعل وإما بالقوة فيلزم الجزء بالقوة، فكما أن ترك الأمر الممتد من الأجزاء الممتنعة الانقسام في الخارج ممنوع فكذا تركيبه منها في الذهن لا يقال : إذا لم يكن الآن موجوداً في الخارج لا يكون مجموع الآنين موجوداً فيه، فلا يصح وقوع الحركة فيه فلا يتم ما ذكرتم لأنا تقول : إذا فرض تتالي الآنين يكون ذلك المجموع زماناً، والزمان سواء كان موجوداً أو موهوماً يجوز وقوع الحركة فيه قطعاً. قوله: (فيلزم تركب المسافة أيضاً منها وأما إذا تحقق الآن ولم يتال فلا يلزم هذا المحذور لأن الآن طرف للزمان وهو عرض قائم به غير حال فيه حلول السريان والمنطبق على المسافة هو المحل، فلا يلزم من انطباقه عليها محذور وهذا كما أن ثبوت النقطة لا يستلزم الجزء وكون الخط متألفاً عن النقط يستلزمه . قوله : (فهو (سكون قالوا: وهذا السكون ليس من مقتضيات الطبيعة، فإنها تقتضي الحركة إلى الحالة الملائمة لها وهذا السكون لا يلائمها لأنه في الحيز القريب بل الميل القسري لا طرف حركة) متوجهة نحو المنتهى ) والرجوع في آن هو طرف حركة) منصرفة عنه ( فلم لا يجوز أن يكون ) آن واحد ( حداً مشتركاً بينهما ) أي بين الحركتين بل بين زمانيهما، فإن الطرف الواحد يجوز أن يكون مشتركا بين شيئين كالنقطة الواحدة المشتركة بين خطين بخلاف الجزء ولذلك قال : ( وأما الآن بمعنى جزء زماني ينقسم ذلك الجزء ( فأنتم لا تقولون (به حتى يمتنع اشتراكه بين زمان الحركتين ( قولكم أن الرجوع غير أن الوصول قلنا : نعم بينهما تغاير (لكن) لا بالذات بل باعتبار كونه منتهى لزمان الحركة الموصلة ومبدأ لزمان حركة الرجوع واعلم أن الحجة المشهورة للمثبتين من الحكماء هي أن المتحرك إلى المنتهى إنما يصل إليه في آن، وإذا تحرك عنه بعد كونه واصلاً إليه، فلا محالة يصير مفارقاً ومبايناً له في آن أيضاً ، ولا يمكن اتحاد الآنين وإلا كان واصلاً إلى المنتهى ومبايناً له معاً، فوجب تغايرهما بالذات واستحال تتاليهما بلا تخلل زمان بينهما لاستلزامه القول بالجزء، وذلك الزمان زمان سكون إذ لا حركة هناك لا إلى ذلك الحد ولا عنه، وأبطلها ابن سينا بأن المفارقة والمباينة هي حركة الرجوع فهناك :آنان آن يقع فيه ابتداء الرجوع والمباينة، وآن يصدق فيه على المتحرك أنه مفارق مباين لذلك الحد الذي هو المنتهى، فإن عنوا بأن المباينة طرف زمان المباينة نختار أن ذلك الآن هو بعينه آن الوصول بأن يكون حداً مشتركاً بین زمان الحركتين، فإن طرف الحركة يجوز أن يكون شيئاً ليس فيه حركة أصلاً، وإن عنوا به آناً يصدق فيه على المتحرك أنه راجع مباین نختار أنه مغاير لآن الوصول، وإن بين الآنين زماناً لكنه ليس زمان السكون بل زمان الحركة، وهو بعض حركة الرجوع فإن كل آن يفرض في زمان وقع فيه حركة قوله : ( وأبطلها إلخ) ونقض بأن هذه الحجة بعينها جارية في الحدود المفروضة في المسافة التي تقطعها حركة واحدة حركة الرجوع أو سببها . قوله : ( يكون بينه وبين إلخ ) بناء على أن الحركة ليس له أول حدوث، إذ لا يوجد إلا زمان وإلا بعد زمان إذ هي مقتضية لأين لم يكن الجسم قبله فيه ولا يكون بعده فيه، فيقتضي تقدماً وتأخراً زمانياً . كما أفاد قوة التحريك إلى الحد المعين كذلك أفاد قوة التسكين في ذلك الحد ثم الطبيعة بشرط السكون في ذلك الحد تحدث بعد ذلك ميلاً ومدافعة إلى جهة ليسفل، فيحدث الحركة إليه قال المولى العلامة شمس الملة والدين محمد بن مبارك شاه البخاري الأشبه أن هذا السكون قسري والقاسر امتناع تتالي الآنات إذ الضرورة الطبيعية مثل ضرورة الخلاء وغيرها كثيراً ما تقتضي أموراً استبعدها العقل.
His saying: (The succession of instants would necessarily follow). Al-Katibi answered it with the gist that the necessity of instants in the external world is denied; rather it would be necessary only if the instant existed externally, which is denied. And its necessity in the mind is admitted, but its impossibility is denied; only the succession of instants in the external world is impossible. The commentator rebutted that if instants succeed one another in the mind, let us suppose that a body moved in them over a distance; then motion would be divided into two indivisible parts, and likewise the distance into them. Then either the two parts exist actually in the distance, necessitating an actual part, or potentially, necessitating a potential part. So just as the composition of an extended thing from parts indivisible externally is denied, so is its composition from them in the mind. It cannot be said: if the instant does not exist externally, the sum of two instants does not exist in it, so motion cannot occur in it, so what you mentioned is not established. For we say: if the succession of two instants is assumed, that sum is a time; and time, whether existent or imaginary, absolutely admits the occurrence of motion in it. His saying: (Therefore the composition of the distance from them would follow). But if the instant is actual and not successive, this danger does not follow, because the instant is a limit of time, and it is an accident subsisting in it, not in the manner of permeation. What is applied to the distance is the locus, so no danger follows from its application. This is like the fact that the fixing of a point does not necessitate a part, but the composition of a line from points would necessitate it. His saying: (So it is rest). They said: This rest is not a requirement of nature, for nature requires motion to the state suitable for it, and this rest is not suitable for it because it is in the near place; rather it is compulsory inclination (mīl qasrī). (It is a limit of motion) directed towards the endpoint. And the return in an instant is a limit of motion turning away from it. So why is it not allowed that one instant be a common limit between them – i.e., between the two motions, rather between their times? For a single limit may be shared between two things, like the single point shared between two lines, unlike the part. Therefore he said: (As for the instant in the sense of a temporal part, that part is divisible; you do not assert it) so that its sharing between the times of the two motions is prevented. Your saying that the return is other than the arrival; we say: yes, there is difference between them, but not in essence, rather in consideration of it being the end of the time of the conveying motion and the beginning of the time of the return motion. Know that the famous proof of the affirmative philosophers is that the mover towards the endpoint arrives at it in an instant, and when it moves away from it after having arrived, it necessarily becomes separate and distinct from it in an instant as well. The two instants cannot be identical, otherwise it would be both arriving and distinct simultaneously. So they must differ in essence, and their succession without an intervening time is impossible because it would necessitate the [atomistic] part. That time is a time of rest, since there is no motion there, neither towards that limit nor away from it. Ibn Sina refuted this by [saying] that separation and distinctness are the return motion; there are two instants: an instant in which the beginning of return and separation occurs, and an instant in which the mover is truly described as separate and distinct from that limit which is the endpoint. If they mean by distinctness the limit of the time of distinctness, we choose that that instant is exactly the instant of arrival, being a common limit between the times of the two motions; for the limit of motion may be something in which there is no motion at all. And if they mean by it an instant in which the mover is truly described as returning and distinct, we choose that it is different from the instant of arrival, and there is time between the two instants, but it is not time of rest; rather it is time of motion, and it is part of the return motion, for every instant assumed in time occurs during motion. His saying: (And he refuted it, etc.) And it was refuted by [saying] that this very argument applies to the limits assumed in the distance that a single motion traverses, whether the motion is of return or its cause. His saying: (There is between it and, etc.) is based on the fact that motion has no first occurrence, for it only exists in time or after time, since it requires a 'where' that the body was not in before and will not be in after, so it implies temporal priority and posteriority. Just as the power of moving towards a specific limit provides the power of stopping at that limit, then nature, conditioned by rest at that limit, afterward generates inclination and impetus towards the lower direction, so motion towards it occurs. The lord, the most learned, Shams al-Milla wa al-Din Muhammad ibn Mubarak Shah al-Bukhari, said: it is more plausible that this rest is compulsory, and the compeller is the impossibility of successive instants, for natural necessity, like the necessity of a vacuum and others, often requires things that reason deems unlikely.
Source pages p. 266

Commentary
This passage continues the discussion on the philosophers' argument by addressing objections and presenting Ibn Sina's refutation. It begins with an objection regarding the necessary succession of instants (tātālī al-ānāt). Al-Katibi responds that the necessity of instants exists only in the mind, not externally; the commentator rebuts that even mental succession leads to problems of parts. Then it discusses that if the instant is a limit, not a part, the problem of composition from parts is avoided, analogously to a point in a line. The passage then presents the famous proof (al-ḥujja al-mashhūra) of the philosophers: the mover arrives in an instant, moves away in a different instant, and the two cannot be identical; hence there must be a time of rest between. Ibn Sina refutes this by distinguishing the instant as boundary (ṭaraf) and the instant as description (ṣidq). He argues that the instant of arrival can serve as a common limit for both motions, avoiding the need for rest. The passage also includes further critiques about the nature of rest and a concluding remark from Shams al-Din al-Bukhari that rest is compulsory due to the impossibility of successive instants.

حد مشترك

حركة
سكون
ميل قسري
ابن سينا

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: There must always be a time of rest between arrival and return.
Correction: Ibn Sina shows that if the instant is a boundary, it can be shared, so rest is not necessary.

Checkpoint
What is Ibn Sina's main point in refuting the philosophers' argument for a time of rest?
A) Motion cannot occur in an instant.
B) The instant of arrival can be a common limit between both motions.
C) Rest is impossible because nature always moves.
D) Consecutive instants are impossible.

Key Takeaway
Ibn Sina refutes the necessity of rest by showing that the instant of arrival, as a limit, can be common to both motions.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
Have you ever assumed that a significant change in your life (like a new habit or a fresh start) requires a period of rest or pause between the old and the new? After this lesson, do you still think that pause is necessary, or could the transition itself be continuous, with one moment serving as both an ending and a beginning?
?
Ibn Sina argued that an instant can be a common limit shared by two motions. In your own spiritual journey, is there a single moment that could mark both the end of a sin and the beginning of repentance, without a gap of indifference? What would it mean for you to act on that continuity today?
?
The affirmative philosophers insisted that rest must intervene because instants cannot be successive without time. Think of a relationship or responsibility you've recently ended and another you've begun. Are you waiting for a 'time of rest' before fully committing to the new? What if that rest is an illusion, and you are actually already in motion? How would that change what you do tomorrow?

The Philosophers' Argument on the Instant of Arrival and Ibn Sina's Refutation
Philosophers argue for a distinct instant of arrival; Ibn Sina refutes.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the philosophers' argument that a mover must rest between arrival and departure, and Ibn Sina's refutation that the instant of arrival and beginning of return are the same instant as a common boundary.

Core Idea
The instant of arrival is a common boundary, not a distinct instant of rest.

The philosophers argue that a mover arriving at a limit must do so in an instant, and then depart in another instant, requiring a time of rest between. Ibn Sina refutes this by showing that the departure begins at the same instant considered as the end of one motion and the start of another, eliminating the need for a rest.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage and translation
Read the Arabic passage carefully, then its literal translation. Notice the structure: an initial objection, then the philosophers' proof, then Ibn Sina's refutation.
The passage uses parentheses to mark logical distinctions.
2
Identify the three main parts
Identify the objection (why not one instant as common boundary?), the philosophers' proof (arrival instant, departure instant, impossibility of union/immediate succession, conclusion of rest), and Ibn Sina's refutation (distinction of two senses of separation).
3
Analyze the philosophers' proof step by step
The mover arrives at the limit in an instant. When it moves from the limit, it becomes separate in another instant. These two instants cannot be the same (else simultaneous arrival and departure) nor can they be immediately successive (would entail temporal atom). Therefore there must be an intervening time of rest.
The philosophers rely on the impossibility of a temporal atom (جزء لا يتجزأ).
4
Examine Ibn Sina's refutation
Ibn Sina distinguishes two meanings of 'instant of separation': (1) the instant of beginning the return motion, which is the same as the instant of arrival as a common boundary between two times; (2) the instant when the mover is truly described as separated, which is later and occurs within the return motion. Thus the time between is not rest but part of motion.
This shows that the philosophers' conclusion of rest is based on a confusion about the instant of departure.
5
Summarize the key insight
The instant of arrival can serve as a common boundary, so no separate instant of rest is needed. Motion continues without a pause.
This refutation supports the continuity of motion and avoids the need for atomic time.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
طرف حركة) متوجهة نحو المنتهى ) والرجوع في آن هو طرف حركة) منصرفة عنه ( فلم لا يجوز أن يكون ) آن واحد ( حداً مشتركاً بينهما ) أي بين الحركتين بل بين زمانيهما، فإن الطرف الواحد يجوز أن يكون مشتركا بين شيئين كالنقطة الواحدة المشتركة بين خطين بخلاف الجزء ولذلك قال : ( وأما الآن بمعنى جزء زماني ينقسم ذلك الجزء ( فأنتم لا تقولون (به حتى يمتنع اشتراكه بين زمان الحركتين ( قولكم أن الرجوع غير أن الوصول قلنا : نعم بينهما تغاير (لكن) لا بالذات بل باعتبار كونه منتهى لزمان الحركة الموصلة ومبدأ لزمان حركة الرجوع واعلم أن الحجة المشهورة للمثبتين من الحكماء هي أن المتحرك إلى المنتهى إنما يصل إليه في آن، وإذا تحرك عنه بعد كونه واصلاً إليه، فلا محالة يصير مفارقاً ومبايناً له في آن أيضاً ، ولا يمكن اتحاد الآنين وإلا كان واصلاً إلى المنتهى ومبايناً له معاً، فوجب تغايرهما بالذات واستحال تتاليهما بلا تخلل زمان بينهما لاستلزامه القول بالجزء، وذلك الزمان زمان سكون إذ لا حركة هناك لا إلى ذلك الحد ولا عنه، وأبطلها ابن سينا بأن المفارقة والمباينة هي حركة الرجوع فهناك :آنان آن يقع فيه ابتداء الرجوع والمباينة، وآن يصدق فيه على المتحرك أنه مفارق مباين لذلك الحد الذي هو المنتهى، فإن عنوا بأن المباينة طرف زمان المباينة نختار أن ذلك الآن هو بعينه آن الوصول بأن يكون حداً مشتركاً بین زمان الحركتين، فإن طرف الحركة يجوز أن يكون شيئاً ليس فيه حركة أصلاً، وإن عنوا به آناً يصدق فيه على المتحرك أنه راجع مباین نختار أنه مغاير لآن الوصول، وإن بين الآنين زماناً لكنه ليس زمان السكون بل زمان الحركة، وهو بعض حركة الرجوع فإن كل آن يفرض في زمان وقع فيه حركة
(The end of motion directed toward the limit) and the return in an instant is the end of a motion departing from it. So why is it not allowed that one instant be a common boundary between them, i.e., between the two motions, but rather between their times? For a single boundary may be common between two things, like a single point common between two lines, unlike a part. Therefore he said: 'As for the instant in the sense of a temporal part that divides that part, you do not affirm it, so that its being common between the times of the two motions is impossible.' (Your saying that the return is other than the arrival. We say: Yes, there is a difference between them, but not in essence, rather by virtue of its being the end of the time of the arriving motion and the beginning of the time of the return motion.) And know that the famous argument of the affirmers among the philosophers is that the mover to the limit arrives at it only in an instant, and when it moves from it after being arrived at it, it inevitably becomes departing and separate from it in an instant as well. The union of the two instants is impossible, otherwise it would be arriving at the limit and separate from it simultaneously. So their difference in essence is necessary, and their succession without an intervening time is impossible because it would entail the affirmation of the part. That time is a time of rest, since there is no motion there, neither toward that limit nor from it. Ibn Sina refuted it by saying that the departure and separation are the motion of return. There are two instants: an instant in which the beginning of return and separation occurs, and an instant in which it is true of the mover that it is departing and separate from that limit which is the end. If they mean by separation the end of the time of separation, we choose that that instant is precisely the instant of arrival, being a common boundary between the times of the two motions; for the end of motion may be something in which there is no motion at all. And if they mean by it an instant in which it is true of the mover that it is returning and separate, we choose that it is different from the instant of arrival, and between the two instants there is time, but it is not time of rest; rather it is time of motion, and it is some part of the motion of return, for every instant assumed in time occurs in a motion.
Source pages p. 267

Commentary
This passage presents the central argument of the philosophers regarding the cause of slowness (ʿillat al-buṭ') and Ibn Sina's refutation. The author first states an objection: why cannot a single instant be a common boundary between two motions—the motion toward the limit and the return motion? This is possible like a point shared between two lines. The philosophers then present their famous proof: a mover arrives at the limit in an instant; when it departs, it becomes separate in another instant. These two instants cannot be identical (otherwise it would be both arriving and departing at once) nor can they follow each other without an intervening time (which would imply a temporal atom). Therefore there must be a time of rest between them. Ibn Sina refutes this by distinguishing two senses of 'instant of separation': the instant of beginning the return motion (which is the instant of arrival as a common boundary) and the instant when the mover is truly described as separated (which is later, within the return motion). Thus the intervening time is not rest but part of the return motion itself.

Key Terms
آن (instant)
حركة متوجهة (directed motion)
حد مشترك (common boundary)
تخلل زمان (intervening time)
سكون (rest)
مفارقة ومباينة (departure and separation)

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: The instant of arrival and the instant of departure must be two separate instants with a rest in between.
Correction: Ibn Sina shows that the instant of arrival can be the same as the instant of beginning of return, considered as a common boundary between two times, so no rest is needed.

Checkpoint
According to Ibn Sina's refutation in this passage, what is the relationship between the instant of arrival and the instant of beginning of return?
A) They are two distinct instants with no time between them.
B) They are the same instant considered as a common boundary between two times.
C) They are separated by a time of rest.
D) They are the same instant and there is no motion between them.

Key Takeaway
Ibn Sina refutes the necessity of a rest by showing that the instant of arrival can serve as a common boundary between the time of arrival and the time of return, making the time between part of motion, not rest.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
The philosophers assumed a rest period between arrival and departure. Where in your own life do you assume 'rest' when actually you are already in motion toward something else? What would change if you recognized that momentum?
?
Ibn Sina shows that a single instant can serve as a boundary between two states without contradiction. How often do you hold onto a fixed point in your spiritual or intellectual journey, thinking you are stuck, when actually that point is a shared boundary for growth? What would it take to see it that way?
?
This passage forces you to reexamine an assumption about time and motion. What is one belief about your own spiritual progress that you hold as necessary, but that might actually be an illusion? Will you test that belief today?

Rebuttal of Ibn Sina's Refutation
Initial rebuttal of Ibn Sina's refutation.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand how the same argument Ibn Sina used against the philosophers can be turned back against his own assumptions about motion limits.

Core Idea
One argument can dismantle both sides if it is truly universal.

The passage opens by directly challenging Ibn Sina’s refutation. It points out that the very same reasoning he used can be applied to the boundaries assumed within a single motion of return. This counter-argument exposes an inconsistency in his position.

Guided Path
1
Read the rebuttal passage
Read the Arabic passage and its translation. Notice how the author identifies the self-applicability of Ibn Sina's argument.
The argument about limits in a single motion is mirroring Ibn Sina's own structure.
2
Compare the two applications
Understand why the same reasoning applies both to the original refutation and to the limits in a single motion. This is a classic diallelus.
It shows that the proof is not specific to one side.
3
Reflect on the role of motion origination
Note the statement that motion has no first moment – this is crucial for the rebuttal.

⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
قوله : ( وأبطلها إلخ) ونقض بأن هذه الحجة بعينها جارية في الحدود المفروضة في المسافة التي تقطعها حركة واحدة حركة الرجوع أو سببها . قوله : ( يكون بينه وبين إلخ ) بناء على أن الحركة ليس له أول حدوث، إذ لا يوجد إلا . زمان وإلا بعد زمان إذ هي مقتضية لأين لم يكن الجسم قبله فيه ولا يكون بعده فيه، فيقتضي تقدماً وتأخراً زمانياً . كما أفاد قوة التحريك إلى الحد المعين كذلك أفاد قوة التسكين في ذلك الحد ثم الطبيعة بشرط السكون في ذلك الحد تحدث بعد ذلك ميلاً ومدافعة إلى جهة ليسفل، فيحدث الحركة إليه قال المولى العلامة شمس الملة والدين محمد بن مبارك شاه البخاري الأشبه أن هذا السكون قسري والقاسر امتناع تتالي الآنات إذ الضرورة الطبيعية مثل ضرورة الخلاء وغيرها كثيراً ما تقتضي أموراً استبعدها العقل. 
المرصد الرابع - المقصد الثاني عشر : قول الحكماء في علة البطء 
الرجوع يكون بينه وبين آن ابتداء الرجوع بعض حركة الرجوع، ثم إنه أقام الحجة على وجوب تخلل السكون بأن اعتبر الميل الموصل والميل الموجب لحركة المفارقة وحكم بأن اجتماعهما في آن واحد محال، إذ يستحيل أن يجتمع في جسم الإيصال إلى حد والتنحية عنه فوجب أن يكون كل منهما في آن مغاير؛ لأن الآخر بينهما زمان سكون كما مر والمصنف قرر الحجة التي أوردها ابن سينا وأجاب عنها بما هو جواب الحجة المشهورة فالصواب أن يجاب بمنع استحالة اجتماع الميلين، أو بتجويز عن لا قوله: (فوجب أن يكون كل منهما في (آن فيه بحث لأن الموجب لحركة المفارقة لا يمكن أن يكون آنياً وإلا لزم وقوع الحركة في الآن وإن أراد بإيجابه لها أنها تحصل بعده فلا تخلف فلا نسلم أن اجتماعه مع الميل الموصل يستلزم احتمالات للإيصال والتنجية في زمان هو طرفه، فهذا الدليل غير تام كالحجة المشهورة وأخذ الميل في الاستدلال فيما يفيد لدفع الشبهة ما لم يثبت آنية الميل وامتناع اجتماع الميلين في آن واحد والتحقيق أن العلة الموصولة إلى الحد، وليس ميل موجودة حال الإيصال فإن كان يوجد موصلاً زماناً فقد صح السكون وإن كان يوجد إلا آناً، فإذا تحرك فلا بد للحركة الثانية من علة موجودة وهو الميل، إذ لا يكفي انتفاء الميل الأول وهو ظاهر فذلك الميل الثاني لأن الشيء لا يكون في طبيعة ما يوجب الحصول وما يوجب اللاحصول معاً، فيكون طباعه تقتضي أن تكون فيه اقتضاء فيه بالفعل، وأن لا يكون اقتضاء بالفعل فإذن آن آخر الميل الأول غير أول الميل الثاني، فهما سكون، هذا خلاصة ما في الشفاء تعدية من النظرية، ولا يخفى أنه لا حاجة في هذا البيان إلى إثبات آنية الوصول وهو الميل أنه لا توجد إلا في آن وأن اجتماع الميلين محال، وأن المعنى المراد أعني العلة الموجدة لا يمكن أن تكون معداً وأنه لا يمكن أن يكون زمانياً، بمعنى أن يكون وجوده متعلقاً بالزمان. وإن كان زمانياً بمعنى أن يوجد في الآن فاندفع جميع الأجوبة فخذ ما أعطينا وكن من الشاكرين.
His saying: 'And he refuted it, etc.' And it was refuted by saying that this very argument applies to the limits assumed in the distance traversed by a single motion, the motion of return or its cause. His saying: 'There is between it and, etc.' based on the fact that motion has no first moment of origination, for it exists only in time and not after time, since it necessitates a location in which the body was not before it and will not be after it, thus implying temporal priority and posteriority. Just as the power of moving toward a specific limit is effective, so also the power of settling at that limit is effective. Then nature, conditionally upon rest at that limit, subsequently produces an inclination and a thrust toward a lower direction, thus generating motion toward it. The eminent master Shams al-Milla wa al-Din Muhammad ibn Mubarak Shah al-Bukhari said: The more likely is that this rest is forced, and the compeller is the impossibility of successive instants, for natural necessity, such as the necessity of a vacuum and others, often requires things that reason deems improbable. 
The Fourth Observation - The Twelfth Objective: The saying of the philosophers on the cause of slowness. 
The return is between it and the instant of beginning of return some part of the return motion. Then he advanced the proof of the necessity of intervening rest by considering the conveying inclination and the inclining that necessitates the motion of separation, and ruled that their coexistence in one instant is impossible, for it is impossible for a body to combine arrival at a limit and removal from it. So each must be in a different instant, because between them there is a time of rest as previously. The author formulated the argument that Ibn Sina presented and answered it with what is the answer to the famous argument. So the correct response is to deny the impossibility of the coexistence of the two inclinations, or by permitting... His saying: 'So each must be in an instant' contains discussion, because the cause of the motion of separation cannot be instantaneous; otherwise it would entail the occurrence of motion in the instant. And if he means by its necessitation that it occurs after it, then there is no delay, so we do not grant that its coexistence with the conveying inclination entails possibilities for arrival and removal in a time that is its limit. So this proof is incomplete like the famous argument. Taking inclination in the inference is useful for repelling the doubt only if the instantaneity of inclination and the impossibility of two inclinations coexisting in one instant are established. The verification is that the cause that conveys to the limit is not an inclination existing at the moment of conveyance. If it exists conveying temporally, then rest is established; if it exists only instantaneously, then when it moves, the second motion necessarily has a cause, which is inclination, for the non-existence of the first inclination is not sufficient, and that is clear. So the second inclination, because a thing cannot by nature necessitate both obtaining and not obtaining simultaneously. So its nature requires that it have an actual disposition and not have an actual disposition. Therefore, the last instant of the first inclination is not the first instant of the second inclination, so they are rest. This is the summary from the Shifa, transferred from theoretical matters. And it is clear that in this exposition there is no need to prove the instantaneity of arrival, i.e., that inclination exists only in an instant, and that the coexistence of two inclinations is impossible, and that the intended meaning—the efficient cause—cannot be a preparer and cannot be temporal, in the sense that its existence is tied to time. And if it is temporal in the sense that it exists in the instant, then all the responses are rebutted. Take what we have given you and be among the thankful.
Source pages p. 267

Commentary
This passage is a critical discussion of Ibn Sina's refutation and an attempt to prove the necessity of a time of rest (sukūn) between two opposed motions. The author first presents a rebuttal: the same argument that Ibn Sina used against the philosophers can be applied to the limits assumed within a single motion of return. Then a proof based on inclination (mayl) is introduced: the inclination that conveys to the limit and the inclination that causes separation cannot coexist in one instant, so each must be in a different instant, implying a time of rest between them. The author then examines the details: if the cause of separation is not instantaneous, the argument fails; but if it is instantaneous, then the second motion requires a new inclination. The eventual verification from the Shifa shows that there is no need to prove instantaneity or impossibility of two inclinations; the key is that the efficient cause cannot be temporal. The passage ends with a summary and a rhetorical flourish.

Key Terms
تخلل السكون (intervening rest)
آنية الميل (instantaneity of inclination)

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
What is the main point of the rebuttal in this passage?
A) Ibn Sina's argument is invalid because it contradicts the philosophers.
B) The same argument applies to the limits within a single motion of return.
C) Rest between motions is impossible.
D) Motion has a first moment of origination.

Key Takeaway
Ibn Sina's refutation can be turned against itself by applying it to the boundaries within a single motion.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
You just saw how Ibn Sina's own argument was turned against him. Is there a belief or argument you defend that, if consistently applied, would also undermine your own position? What would it take for you to examine your own reasoning with the same rigor you apply to others? Write down one specific area where you will do this today.
?
The passage mentions 'forced rest' due to the impossibility of successive instants. In your spiritual life, do you have areas of 'rest'—complacency, stagnation—that you accept as natural but are actually forced by your own neglect or fear? What is one 'rest' that you need to break today, and what movement toward Allah will you initiate?
?
The author used universal logic to refute his opponent. When you disagree with someone, do you seek principles that apply equally to both sides, or do you only see their flaws? This week, commit to arguing against your own view as strongly as you argue for it. What is one issue where you will practice this? How will you hold yourself accountable?

Causality and Created Things
advanced
7 min
Proof of Required Rest via Inclinations
Proof of necessary rest using the concept of inclination.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the proof that a rest must intervene between two opposed motions, based on the impossibility of two inclinations coexisting in one instant.

Core Idea
Conveying and separating inclinations cannot share the same instant, so rest is necessary.

This lesson presents the core argument for the necessity of a period of rest (sukūn) between two opposite motions. The proof relies on the nature of inclination (mayl): the inclination that conveys to a limit and the inclination that necessitates separation from it cannot exist together in the same instant. Each requires its own moment, with rest in between.

Guided Path
1
Identify the two inclinations
Read the passage to find the two types of inclination: 'al-mayl al-mūṣil' (conveying) and 'al-mayl al-mūjib' (necessitating separation).
These are central to the argument.
2
Understand the impossibility of coexistence
The passage asserts that both inclinations cannot coexist in one instant because that would combine arrival and removal – a logical contradiction.
3
Trace the conclusion of rest
Since each inclination must be in a different instant, and motion requires continuity, there must be a period of rest between them.
This is the key claim to prove.
4
Review the criticism
The passage also includes a critique: the argument is incomplete unless the instantaneity of inclination and impossibility of coexistence are established.
The verification lesson will resolve this.

Source Text

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Some think the proof of rest is self-sufficient without establishing instantaneity or impossibility of coexistence.

Checkpoint
What condition does the passage say is necessary for the proof of rest to be valid?
A) The two inclinations must be of equal strength.
B) The instantaneity of inclination and impossibility of two inclinations coexisting must be established.
C) The motion must be rectilinear.
D) The body must be in a vacuum.

Key Takeaway
The proof of rest via inclinations is not self-sufficient; it depends on prior metaphysical commitments.

?

?

?

Verification: Efficient Cause and Temporal Restraints
Verification from the Shifa that the efficient cause is instantaneous.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the verification from Avicenna's Shifa that the efficient cause of motion cannot be temporal, thereby rebutting objections against the necessity of rest.

Core Idea
The efficient cause is instantaneous, not temporal – this dissolves all objections.

This lesson concludes the discussion with a verification from Avicenna's Shifa. The key insight is that the efficient cause ('illa mūjida) of motion cannot be temporal; it must be instantaneous. Once this is recognized, the proof of the need for rest becomes solid without requiring additional assumptions about inclinations.

Guided Path
1
Read the verification passage
Read the Arabic and translation. Identify the claim that the efficient cause is not temporal.
This is the core move that solves the earlier difficulties.
2
Understand the case analysis
The passage considers two scenarios: if the conveying cause exists temporally, rest is established; if only instantaneously, the second motion still requires a new cause (inclination).
3
See the conclusion
Since the efficient cause cannot be temporal (in the sense of enduring), all objections are rebutted. The rest is necessary.
This is the final resolution.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
قوله : ( وأبطلها إلخ) ونقض بأن هذه الحجة بعينها جارية في الحدود المفروضة في المسافة التي تقطعها حركة واحدة حركة الرجوع أو سببها . قوله : ( يكون بينه وبين إلخ ) بناء على أن الحركة ليس له أول حدوث، إذ لا يوجد إلا . زمان وإلا بعد زمان إذ هي مقتضية لأين لم يكن الجسم قبله فيه ولا يكون بعده فيه، فيقتضي تقدماً وتأخراً زمانياً . كما أفاد قوة التحريك إلى الحد المعين كذلك أفاد قوة التسكين في ذلك الحد ثم الطبيعة بشرط السكون في ذلك الحد تحدث بعد ذلك ميلاً ومدافعة إلى جهة ليسفل، فيحدث الحركة إليه قال المولى العلامة شمس الملة والدين محمد بن مبارك شاه البخاري الأشبه أن هذا السكون قسري والقاسر امتناع تتالي الآنات إذ الضرورة الطبيعية مثل ضرورة الخلاء وغيرها كثيراً ما تقتضي أموراً استبعدها العقل. 
المرصد الرابع - المقصد الثاني عشر : قول الحكماء في علة البطء 
الرجوع يكون بينه وبين آن ابتداء الرجوع بعض حركة الرجوع، ثم إنه أقام الحجة على وجوب تخلل السكون بأن اعتبر الميل الموصل والميل الموجب لحركة المفارقة وحكم بأن اجتماعهما في آن واحد محال، إذ يستحيل أن يجتمع في جسم الإيصال إلى حد والتنحية عنه فوجب أن يكون كل منهما في آن مغاير؛ لأن الآخر بينهما زمان سكون كما مر والمصنف قرر الحجة التي أوردها ابن سينا وأجاب عنها بما هو جواب الحجة المشهورة فالصواب أن يجاب بمنع استحالة اجتماع الميلين، أو بتجويز عن لا قوله: (فوجب أن يكون كل منهما في (آن فيه بحث لأن الموجب لحركة المفارقة لا يمكن أن يكون آنياً وإلا لزم وقوع الحركة في الآن وإن أراد بإيجابه لها أنها تحصل بعده فلا تخلف فلا نسلم أن اجتماعه مع الميل الموصل يستلزم احتمالات للإيصال والتنجية في زمان هو طرفه، فهذا الدليل غير تام كالحجة المشهورة وأخذ الميل في الاستدلال فيما يفيد لدفع الشبهة ما لم يثبت آنية الميل وامتناع اجتماع الميلين في آن واحد والتحقيق أن العلة الموصولة إلى الحد، وليس ميل موجودة حال الإيصال فإن كان يوجد موصلاً زماناً فقد صح السكون وإن كان يوجد إلا آناً، فإذا تحرك فلا بد للحركة الثانية من علة موجودة وهو الميل، إذ لا يكفي انتفاء الميل الأول وهو ظاهر فذلك الميل الثاني لأن الشيء لا يكون في طبيعة ما يوجب الحصول وما يوجب اللاحصول معاً، فيكون طباعه تقتضي أن تكون فيه اقتضاء فيه بالفعل، وأن لا يكون اقتضاء بالفعل فإذن آن آخر الميل الأول غير أول الميل الثاني، فهما سكون، هذا خلاصة ما في الشفاء تعدية من النظرية، ولا يخفى أنه لا حاجة في هذا البيان إلى إثبات آنية الوصول وهو الميل أنه لا توجد إلا في آن وأن اجتماع الميلين محال، وأن المعنى المراد أعني العلة الموجدة لا يمكن أن تكون معداً وأنه لا يمكن أن يكون زمانياً، بمعنى أن يكون وجوده متعلقاً بالزمان. وإن كان زمانياً بمعنى أن يوجد في الآن فاندفع جميع الأجوبة فخذ ما أعطينا وكن من الشاكرين.
His saying: 'And he refuted it, etc.' And it was refuted by saying that this very argument applies to the limits assumed in the distance traversed by a single motion, the motion of return or its cause. His saying: 'There is between it and, etc.' based on the fact that motion has no first moment of origination, for it exists only in time and not after time, since it necessitates a location in which the body was not before it and will not be after it, thus implying temporal priority and posteriority. Just as the power of moving toward a specific limit is effective, so also the power of settling at that limit is effective. Then nature, conditionally upon rest at that limit, subsequently produces an inclination and a thrust toward a lower direction, thus generating motion toward it. The eminent master Shams al-Milla wa al-Din Muhammad ibn Mubarak Shah al-Bukhari said: The more likely is that this rest is forced, and the compeller is the impossibility of successive instants, for natural necessity, such as the necessity of a vacuum and others, often requires things that reason deems improbable. 
The Fourth Observation - The Twelfth Objective: The saying of the philosophers on the cause of slowness. 
The return is between it and the instant of beginning of return some part of the return motion. Then he advanced the proof of the necessity of intervening rest by considering the conveying inclination and the inclining that necessitates the motion of separation, and ruled that their coexistence in one instant is impossible, for it is impossible for a body to combine arrival at a limit and removal from it. So each must be in a different instant, because between them there is a time of rest as previously. The author formulated the argument that Ibn Sina presented and answered it with what is the answer to the famous argument. So the correct response is to deny the impossibility of the coexistence of the two inclinations, or by permitting... His saying: 'So each must be in an instant' contains discussion, because the cause of the motion of separation cannot be instantaneous; otherwise it would entail the occurrence of motion in the instant. And if he means by its necessitation that it occurs after it, then there is no delay, so we do not grant that its coexistence with the conveying inclination entails possibilities for arrival and removal in a time that is its limit. So this proof is incomplete like the famous argument. Taking inclination in the inference is useful for repelling the doubt only if the instantaneity of inclination and the impossibility of two inclinations coexisting in one instant are established. The verification is that the cause that conveys to the limit is not an inclination existing at the moment of conveyance. If it exists conveying temporally, then rest is established; if it exists only instantaneously, then when it moves, the second motion necessarily has a cause, which is inclination, for the non-existence of the first inclination is not sufficient, and that is clear. So the second inclination, because a thing cannot by nature necessitate both obtaining and not obtaining simultaneously. So its nature requires that it have an actual disposition and not have an actual disposition. Therefore, the last instant of the first inclination is not the first instant of the second inclination, so they are rest. This is the summary from the Shifa, transferred from theoretical matters. And it is clear that in this exposition there is no need to prove the instantaneity of arrival, i.e., that inclination exists only in an instant, and that the coexistence of two inclinations is impossible, and that the intended meaning—the efficient cause—cannot be a preparer and cannot be temporal, in the sense that its existence is tied to time. And if it is temporal in the sense that it exists in the instant, then all the responses are rebutted. Take what we have given you and be among the thankful.
Source pages p. 267

Commentary
This passage is a critical discussion of Ibn Sina's refutation and an attempt to prove the necessity of a time of rest (sukūn) between two opposed motions. The author first presents a rebuttal: the same argument that Ibn Sina used against the philosophers can be applied to the limits assumed within a single motion of return. Then a proof based on inclination (mayl) is introduced: the inclination that conveys to the limit and the inclination that causes separation cannot coexist in one instant, so each must be in a different instant, implying a time of rest between them. The author then examines the details: if the cause of separation is not instantaneous, the argument fails; but if it is instantaneous, then the second motion requires a new inclination. The eventual verification from the Shifa shows that there is no need to prove instantaneity or impossibility of two inclinations; the key is that the efficient cause cannot be temporal. The passage ends with a summary and a rhetorical flourish.

Key Terms
علة موجدة (efficient cause)
آن (instant)
زمان (time)

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Some think that the proof of rest depends on establishing the instantaneity of inclination and impossibility of two inclinations coexisting.
Correction: The verification shows that these are unnecessary; the key is the non-temporality of the efficient cause.

Checkpoint
According to the verification from the Shifa, what is the essential point that rebuts all objections?
A) The conveying inclination is always temporal.
B) The efficient cause cannot be temporal (tied to time).
C) Two inclinations can coexist in one instant.
D) Motion has no cause.

Key Takeaway
The efficient cause's instantaneity resolves the need for rest without relying on unproven assumptions about inclinations.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
You have just read that the efficient cause is instantaneous, not stretched across time. Where in your life have you assumed that your actions have a gradual, temporal effect, when in reality the decisive moment is an instant? Will you start looking for those instants and being present in them, or will you continue to live as if time alone brings change?
?
Avicenna argues that the efficient cause cannot be a preparer—it either produces its effect in the instant or it isn't the real cause. Are you living as if your small, repeated efforts are 'preparers' for some future change, or do you believe that Allah’s creative power is needed in every single instant? How does this reshape your daily worship and reliance on Him?
?
The passage ends with: 'Take what we have given you and be among the thankful.' Thankfulness here is not just for knowledge, but for a truth that settles the mind and frees it from confusion. Are you truly thankful for the clarity Allah grants you through this reasoning, or do you still hold onto doubts that this truth should have removed?

Objection to Rest Interpolation in All Motions
Objection to rest interpolation in all motions

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the objection that the rest argument would imply rests in every rectilinear and even circular celestial motion, contradicting the nature of celestial bodies.

Core Idea
The rest argument overreaches: it would force rests in the heavens.

This passage presents a powerful objection to the argument that rest must interpolate between motions. If the argument were valid, it would not only apply to rectilinear motion but also to celestial rotations—yet we know the heavens have no rest. Let's examine the objection and its implications.

Guided Path
1
Read the objection
Read the Arabic passage carefully, then the translation. Notice that the objection targets the universality of the rest argument.
2
Identify the two types of motion
The passage mentions rectilinear motion and circular celestial motion. Understand why both would be affected.
Celestial motion is eternal and has no rest.
3
Ask: what is the core problem?
The objection shows that if rest occurs at every point, then circular motion would have infinite rests, contradicting the nature of celestial motion.
This challenges the original proof.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
قوله : ( وأبطلها ابن سينا إلخ) قيل : ويرد عليه أيضاً أنه يلزم على هذا تخلل السكنات في كل حركة مستقيمة سيما إذا كانت على أجسام منضودة، بل يلزم تخلل السكنات في الحركات المستديرة الفلكية باعتبار الوصول إلى الحدود التي في المسافة والزوال عنها مع أنه لا سكون في الفلكيات . قوله: (ثم إنه أقام الحجة إلخ) وقد يجاب عنها بأن الميل الذي هو علة الحركة، كما أنه علة الوصول إلى حد كذلك هو علة الزوال عن ذلك الحد بشرطين فليس هناك ميلان متغايران وأنت خبير بأن هذا الجواب لا يجدي كثير نفع لتوجه الاستدلال حينئذ بالنظر إلى أني حدوث الشرطين نعم يرد منع آنية حدوث الشرط كما يرد منع آنية حدوث الميل فتأمل. قوله : ( فالصواب أن يجاب بمنع استحالة الميلين بل هو واقع كما في الحجر المرمي إلى فوق فان فيه ميلاً طبيعياً إلى تحت وميلا قسرياً إلى فوق وأيضاً الدليل المذكور على تقدير تمامه لا يتمشى في حركة الكم والكيف فإن الحركة التي فيهما غنى عن ذلك الميل كذا ذكره الأبهري في شرحه .
His saying: 'And Ibn Sina refuted it, etc.' It is said: It also objects to him that this entails the interpolation of rests in every rectilinear motion, especially if it is on contiguous bodies; rather, it entails the interpolation of rests in circular celestial motions by virtue of arriving at the limits in the distance and departing from them, even though there is no rest in celestial things. His saying: 'Then he advanced the proof, etc.' And it may be answered that the inclination which is the cause of motion, just as it is the cause of arriving at a limit, so too it is the cause of departing from that limit under two conditions; thus there are not two distinct inclinations. And you are well aware that this answer is of little benefit, because the inference then becomes directed at the how of the occurrence of the two conditions. Indeed, one can deny the instantaneity of the occurrence of the condition just as one can deny the instantaneity of the occurrence of the inclination, so reflect. His saying: 'So the correct response is to deny the impossibility of the two inclinations, rather it actually occurs, as in the stone thrown upward, for there is in it a natural inclination downward and a forced inclination upward. Also, the mentioned proof, even if complete, does not apply to motion in quantity and quality, for the motion in them is independent of that inclination. This is what al-Abhari mentioned in his commentary.
Source pages p. 268

Commentary
This passage presents additional objections and responses to the proof of rest between motions. The first objection: if the argument for rest is valid, it would also imply rests in every rectilinear motion and even in celestial circular motions, but celestial bodies have no rest. The second objection: it is possible that a single inclination causes both arrival and departure under different conditions, so there are not two inclinations. However, the author notes this answer is weak because it only shifts the issue to the instantaneity of the conditions. The final response: the correct answer is to deny the impossibility of two inclinations coexisting, as seen in a stone thrown upward (natural inclination down, forced inclination up). Moreover, the proof does not apply to motion in quantity or quality, which do not require such inclination. This is from al-Abhari.

Key Terms
حركة مستقيمة
حركة مستديرة فلكية
سكون

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: The rest argument only applies to rectangular motions and does not affect celestial motions.
Correction: The objection shows it would apply to all motions, including celestial, leading to an absurdity.

Checkpoint
According to the objection, what kind of motion would also require rests if the rest argument were valid?
A) Only rectilinear motion
B) Circular celestial motion
C) Motion in quantity
D) Motion in quality

Key Takeaway
The rest argument, if extended, leads to the absurd conclusion that even eternal celestial motions would contain rests, contradicting their nature.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
In what areas of your spiritual life have you assumed continuous motion—like constant prayer or good deeds—but upon honest inspection, you find 'rests' of negligence? What specific stops will you remove today to align your soul with the perpetual motion of the heavens?
?
The objection shows that an argument, if pressed too far, leads to an absurdity. Where in your own reasoning about your faith have you stubbornly held a conclusion that, if fully extended, would contradict something you know to be true from revelation? Are you willing to abandon that flawed reasoning?
?
Celestial spheres move without rest, a sign of Allah's perfect order. But your heart often rests in heedlessness. What is the one 'limit' in your life where you repeatedly stop—a sin, a distraction, a comfort—that prevents your soul from moving continuously towards Allah? What will you do to pass that point without pause?

Coexistence of Inclinations and the Correct Response
Responses to the rest argument and demonstration of two inclinations

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the correct response to the rest argument: that two opposite inclinations can coexist in one body, and that the proof does not apply to certain types of motion.

Core Idea
Two inclinations can coexist, and the rest argument fails for motions that lack inclination.

After considering a weak response, al-Abhari provides the correct answer: it is not impossible for two inclinations to exist in the same body. He gives the example of a stone thrown upward, which simultaneously has a natural inclination downward and a forced inclination upward. Additionally, the proof does not apply to motions in quantity and quality, which are independent of such inclinations.

Guided Path
1
Read the weak answer
First, read the attempted answer that uses a single inclination with two conditions. Note why it is considered weak.
The author says it is of little benefit because it shifts the problem to the instantaneity of conditions.
2
Read the correct answer
Then read the correct response: deny the impossibility of two inclinations, and consider the stone example.
This is a concrete case where two opposite inclinations actually occur.
3
Understand the limitation
Finally, note that the proof does not apply to motion in quantity and quality, as those motions do not need inclination.
This limits the scope of the original argument.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books

His saying: 'And Ibn Sina refuted it, etc.' It is said: It also objects to him that this entails the interpolation of rests in every rectilinear motion, especially if it is on contiguous bodies; rather, it entails the interpolation of rests in circular celestial motions by virtue of arriving at the limits in the distance and departing from them, even though there is no rest in celestial things. This is what al-Abhari mentioned in his commentary.

The first objection: if the argument for rest is valid, it would also imply rests in every rectilinear motion and even in celestial circular motions, but celestial bodies have no rest. ?

?
The rest argument fails for motions in quantity and quality – it only applies to local motion. Are there arguments or excuses you have been using to justify your shortcomings that only apply to a limited context, while you wrongly generalize them to all aspects of your deen? Identify one such argument and commit to examining its validity in light of the full scope of Islamic teachings.
?

In your spiritual life, have you ever tried to 'answer' a conviction or a call to change by only moving the problem elsewhere rather than truly addressing it? What would it mean for you to give the 'correct response' – a full acceptance of the truth and a decisive action – starting today with one specific issue?
