The Philosophers' Argument for Intervening Rest
Presents and critiques the philosophers' proof for intervening rest between two motions.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the philosophers' proof for a necessary rest between opposite motions and the kalam rebuttal that questions the impossibility of simultaneous inclinations.

Core Idea
Opposing inclinations can coexist; rest is not proven necessary.

Sanusi presents a famous argument from Ibn Sina that a body moving toward a limit and then away must have a moment of rest between. The argument seems logical, but the kalam tradition offers a robust critique. This lesson walks you through both the argument and the standard rebuttal.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage carefully
Read the Arabic and translation. Notice the structure: argument, then author's presentation, then rebuttal.
This is a dense philosophical passage.
2
Identify the key premises
Find the two inclinations and why they are said to be incompatible in one instant.
The author's rebuttal denies this incompatibility.
3
Analyze the rebuttal
Two responses are offered: deny impossibility of coexistence, or note that the inclining motion for separation is not instantaneous. Understand why these undermine the argument.
4
Compare with the famous argument
The passage says this argument is incomplete like the famous argument. Reflect on why the kalam tradition finds such proofs unconvincing.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
ثم إنه أقام الحجة على وجوب تخلل السكون بأن اعتبر الميل الموصل والميل الموجب لحركة المفارقة وحكم بأن اجتماعهما في آن واحد محال، إذ يستحيل أن يجتمع في جسم الإيصال إلى حد والتنحية عنه فوجب أن يكون كل منهما في آن مغاير؛ لأن الآخر بينهما زمان سكون كما مر والمصنف قرر الحجة التي أوردها ابن سينا وأجاب عنها بما هو جواب الحجة المشهورة فالصواب أن يجاب بمنع استحالة اجتماع الميلين، أو بتجويز عن لا قوله: (فوجب أن يكون كل منهما في (آن فيه بحث لأن الموجب لحركة المفارقة لا يمكن أن يكون آنياً وإلا لزم وقوع الحركة في الآن وإن أراد بإيجابه لها أنها تحصل بعده فلا تخلف فلا نسلم أن اجتماعه مع الميل الموصل يستلزم احتمالات للإيصال والتنجية في زمان هو طرفه، فهذا الدليل غير تام كالحجة المشهورة.
Then he presented the argument for the necessity of the interpolation of rest by considering the inclining motion that leads to a limit and the inclining motion that necessitates the motion of separation, and he ruled that their coming together in a single instant is impossible, because it is impossible for a body to simultaneously be brought to a limit and removed from it. So it must be that each of them is in a different instant; because between them there is a time of rest, as has passed. And the author (al-Muṣannif) set forth the argument that Ibn Sīnā presented and answered it with the answer of the famous argument. So the correct thing is to answer by denying the impossibility of the conjunction of two inclinations, or by permitting... he said: (So it must be that each of them is in an instant) therein is discussion, because that which necessitates the motion of separation cannot be instantaneous, otherwise it would necessitate the occurrence of motion in the instant. And if he means by its necessitation that it occurs after it, then there is no succession, so we do not accept that its conjunction with the inclining motion to the limit necessitates possibilities for reaching and removing in a time that is its endpoint. So this proof is incomplete, like the famous argument.
Source pages p. 268

Commentary
This passage presents and critiques the philosophers' argument for the necessity of intervening rest between two opposite motions. The argument posits that a body cannot simultaneously have an inclination towards a limit and an inclination away from it, so these must occur in distinct instants, with a time of rest between them. The author (al-Muṣannif) presents Ibn Sīnā's version and offers a standard answer. The correct rebuttal, the passage says, is to deny the impossibility of two inclinations coexisting, or to allow that the inclination causing separation is not instantaneous. The argument is deemed incomplete.

سكون

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception: Opposing inclinations must occur in separate instants.
Correction: The passage argues that this is not necessarily proven; inclinations may coexist.

Checkpoint
What is the main flaw the author identifies in the philosophers' argument for intervening rest?
A) The impossibility of two inclinations coexisting is not proven.
B) The argument relies on a false premise about motion.
C) Rest cannot occur between instants.
D) The argument is circular.

Key Takeaway
The argument for intervening rest is not definitive; the impossibility of simultaneous inclinations is debatable.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?

?

The Nature of the Causative Inclination: Instant or Temporal?
Investigates the nature of the inclining cause and its relation to motion and rest.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand why the causative inclination conjoined to the limit is not an inclination existing at the moment of arrival, and why the proof does not require proving its instantaneity.

Core Idea
The cause of motion is not the inclination at the moment of arrival.

This passage presents a refined investigation (taḥqīq) into the nature of the causative inclination that brings a body to a limit. The author distills Ibn Sīnā's argument, showing that the cause cannot be the inclination present at the moment of reaching. Before diving into the source text, let's understand the key concepts: what does it mean for a cause to be 'conjoined to the limit'? And why does the author say we don't need to prove instantaneity?

Guided Path
1
Read the passage carefully
Read the Arabic passage once without trying to analyse it. Then read the translation. Let the author's own rhythm come through before you interpret.
Notice the dense philosophical style.
2
Identify the two scenarios
The passage presents two possibilities: the cause as temporal (zamānī) or instantaneous (ānī). Understand how each leads to a different conclusion about rest and motion.
The key is the distinction between existing for a time vs. existing only in an instant.
3
Understand the author's claim about necessity
The author says the explanation does not require proving the instantaneity of the inclination or the impossibility of two inclinations. Ask yourself: why would these be unnecessary?
This is a critical move that simplifies the argument.
4
Reflect on the implication
Consider how this investigation refines our understanding of causality in motion. What kind of cause is the inclining cause?
The author concludes that all objections based on such premises are repelled.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
والتحقيق أن العلة الموصولة إلى الحد، وليس ميل موجودة حال الإيصال فإن كان يوجد موصلاً زماناً فقد صح السكون وإن كان يوجد إلا آناً، فإذا تحرك فلا بد للحركة الثانية من علة موجودة وهو الميل، إذ لا يكفي انتفاء الميل الأول وهو ظاهر فذلك الميل الثاني لأن الشيء لا يكون في طبيعة ما يوجب الحصول وما يوجب اللاحصول معاً، فيكون طباعه تقتضي أن تكون فيه اقتضاء فيه بالفعل، وأن لا يكون اقتضاء بالفعل فإذن آن آخر الميل الأول غير أول الميل الثاني، فهما سكون، هذا خلاصة ما في الشفاء تعدية من النظرية، ولا يخفى أنه لا حاجة في هذا البيان إلى إثبات آنية الوصول وهو الميل أنه لا توجد إلا في آن وأن اجتماع الميلين محال، وأن المعنى المراد أعني العلة الموجدة لا يمكن أن تكون معداً وأنه لا يمكن أن يكون زمانياً، بمعنى أن يكون وجوده متعلقاً بالزمان. وإن كان زمانياً بمعنى أن يوجد في الآن فاندفع جميع الأجوبة فخذ ما أعطينا وكن من الشاكرين.
And the investigation is that the cause conjoined to the limit, and it is not an inclination existing at the moment of reaching. For if it exists as a connector for a time, then rest is established; and if it exists only in an instant, then when it moves, the second motion must have an existing cause, which is the inclination, because the absence of the first inclination is not sufficient, and that is apparent. That second inclination, because a thing cannot have in its nature what necessitates obtaining and what necessitates non-obtaining together, so its nature would require that it have an actual disposition and not have an actual disposition. Thus the last instant of the first inclination is other than the first instant of the second inclination, so they are rest. This is the summary of what is in al-Shifā', a transposition from theory. And it is not hidden that there is no need in this explanation to prove the instantaneity of reaching, and that the inclination does not exist except in an instant, and that the conjunction of two inclinations is impossible, and that the intended meaning, namely the existentiating cause, cannot be a preparer, and that it cannot be temporal in the sense that its existence is related to time. But if it is temporal in the sense that it exists in the instant, then all the answers are repelled. So take what we have given and be among the grateful.
Source pages p. 268

Commentary
This passage provides a detailed investigation (taḥqīq) into the nature of the inclining cause (al-ʿilla al-mawṣūla) that brings a body to a limit. The author explains that the cause is not an inclination present at the moment of reaching. If it were temporal (zamānī), rest would be established between motions; if instantaneous (ānī), then the second motion requires a new inclination. The passage distills Ibn Sīnā's argument from al-Shifā', concluding that the explanation does not require proving the instantaneity of the inclination or the impossibility of two inclinations coexisting. Therefore, all objections based on these premises are repelled. The passage ends with an exhortation to accept this refined explanation.

Correction: The author explicitly states that there is no need to prove the instantaneity of the inclination or the impossibility of two inclinations; the argument stands without these proofs.

Checkpoint

Key Takeaway

Additional Objections to Ibn Sīnā's Argument
Lists additional objections and the correct rebuttal.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand several key objections to Ibn Sīnā's argument and the correct rebuttal that avoids the problem of two inclinations.

Core Idea
Ibn Sīnā's argument fails for circular motion and qualitative change.

This passage builds on earlier refutations by presenting further objections to Ibn Sīnā's argument regarding motion and inclination. It also examines a proposed answer and ultimately provides the correct rebuttal based on the reality of two coexisting inclinations, as illustrated by a stone thrown upward. Additionally, it notes that the argument does not apply to motions in quantity and quality.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage and translation
First, read the Arabic passage and its literal translation to get an overview of the objections and responses.
Notice the use of 'قيل' to introduce objections.
2
Identify the first objection
The first objection is that the argument would require intervening rests in all rectilinear motions, even in celestial circular motions, which is impossible.
This targets the universality of Ibn Sīnā's reasoning.
3
Analyse the proposed answer
One proposed answer suggests a single inclination causes both reaching and departing under two conditions. The passage criticizes this as insufficient because it shifts the issue to the instantaneity of conditions.
4
Understand the correct rebuttal
The correct rebuttal denies the impossibility of two inclinations coexisting, using the example of a stone with natural and forced inclinations. Also, the argument fails for motion in quantity and quality.
This is the final and strongest response.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
قوله : ( وأبطلها ابن سينا إلخ) قيل : ويرد عليه أيضاً أنه يلزم على هذا تخلل السكنات في كل حركة مستقيمة سيما إذا كانت على أجسام منضودة، بل يلزم تخلل السكنات في الحركات المستديرة الفلكية باعتبار الوصول إلى الحدود التي في المسافة والزوال عنها مع أنه لا سكون في الفلكيات . قوله: (ثم إنه أقام الحجة إلخ) وقد يجاب عنها بأن الميل الذي هو علة الحركة، كما أنه علة الوصول إلى حد كذلك هو علة الزوال عن ذلك الحد بشرطين فليس هناك ميلان متغايران وأنت خبير بأن هذا الجواب لا يجدي كثير نفع لتوجه الاستدلال حينئذ بالنظر إلى أني حدوث الشرطين نعم يرد منع آنية حدوث الشرط كما يرد منع آنية حدوث الميل فتأمل. قوله : ( فالصواب أن يجاب بمنع استحالة الميلين بل هو واقع كما في الحجر المرمي إلى فوق فان فيه ميلاً طبيعياً إلى تحت وميلا قسرياً إلى فوق وأيضاً الدليل المذكور على تقدير تمامه لا يتمشى في حركة الكم والكيف فإن الحركة التي فيهما غنى عن ذلك الميل كذا ذكره الأبهري في شرحه.
His saying: (And Ibn Sīnā invalidated it, etc.) It is said: There also applies to it that this necessitates the interpolation of rests in every rectilinear motion, especially if on bodies that are stacked; rather, it necessitates the interpolation of rests in the circular motions of the celestial spheres by virtue of reaching the limits in the distance and departing from them, while there is no rest in celestial things. His saying: (Then he presented the argument, etc.) And it may be answered that the inclination which is the cause of motion, just as it is the cause of reaching a limit, it is also the cause of departing from that limit under two conditions, so there are not two distinct inclinations. And you are aware that this answer does not bring much benefit because the argument then proceeds with respect to the instantaneity of the occurrence of the two conditions. Yes, one can deny the instantaneity of the occurrence of the condition, just as one can deny the instantaneity of the occurrence of the inclination, so reflect. His saying: (The correct thing is to answer by denying the impossibility of two inclinations, rather it is real, as in a stone thrown upward, for it has a natural inclination downward and a forced inclination upward. Also, the mentioned proof, even if complete, does not apply to motion in quantity and quality, for the motion in them is independent of that inclination. Thus al-Abharī mentioned in his commentary.
Source pages p. 268

Commentary
This passage presents several objections to Ibn Sīnā's argument and alternative answers. First, it is argued that if the argument were valid, it would entail intervening rests in all rectilinear motions, and even in celestial circular motions, which is false because there is no rest in the heavens. Second, a possible answer is considered: the same inclination causes both reaching and departing under different conditions, so there are not two distinct inclinations. However, this answer is insufficient because the argument then shifts to the instantaneity of the conditions. The passage concludes that the correct rebuttal is to deny the impossibility of two coexisting inclinations, citing the example of a stone thrown upward having both natural and forced inclinations. Additionally, the argument does not apply to quantitative and qualitative change (al-kamm wa al-kayf), as those motions do not require such an inclination.

فلكيات

حركة الكم والكيف

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Ibn Sīnā's argument applies universally to all motions.
Correction: The argument fails for circular celestial motions and for motions in quantity and quality, as shown by the objections.

Checkpoint

Key Takeaway

Al-Jubba'i's Argument on Inclination and Rest at Equilibrium
Presents al-Jubba'i's argument on stone's motion and its rebuttal.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand al-Jubba'i's reasoning about a stone's motion and the necessity of rest at equilibrium, and why this argument is ultimately rejected.

Core Idea
Equilibrium between inclinations forces rest, but al-Jubba'i's own view on inclination refutes it.

Al-Jubba'i, a prominent Mu'tazili theologian, proposed a sophisticated account of projectile motion involving two inclinations. He argued that at the point of equilibrium between these inclinations, rest must occur. However, this argument faces a serious internal inconsistency, as we will see.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage carefully
Read the Arabic passage and its translation. Pay attention to the two types of inclination and the description of the stone's ascent and descent.
Note the technical terms: الاعتماد المجتلب (acquired inclination) and الاعتماد اللازم (necessary inclination).
2
Understand the argument for rest at equilibrium
Identify why al-Jubba'i claims that at the point of equilibrium, rest must occur. The key phrase is 'otherwise there would be preponderance without a preponderator.'
This is a logical argument against sudden reversal without a middle state.
3
Analyze the counter-argument
The response rejects the argument on two grounds: al-Jubba'i himself does not hold that inclination generates motion or rest, and the argument does not cover voluntary motions.
Notice how the critique uses al-Jubba'i's own doctrine against him.
4
Reflect on the significance
Consider why this debate matters in kalam. It touches on causality, atomism, and the nature of motion.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وقال الجبائي : لا شك أن الاعتماد المجتلب في الحجر يغلب الاعتماد اللازم إذا الحادث أقوى من الباقي فيصعد الاعتماد المجتلب في الحجر، ويضعف بمصاكات الهواء المخروق متدرجاً في الضعف إلى أن يغلب اللازم المجتلب فينزل الحجر ولا شك أن غلبته على المجتلب إنما تكون بعد التعادل بينهما إذ لا ينقلب المغلوب من المغلوبية إلى الغالبية دفعة من غير تخلل تعادل وعند التعادل يجب السكون وإلا لزم الترجيح بلا مرجح إذ لو لم يسكن لكان متحركاً، إما بالاعتماد اللازم أو بالاعتماد المجتلب مع تعادلهما وتساويهما فيكون تحكماً محضاً ، والجواب عنه أن الجبائي ليس قائلاً بتولد الاعتماد للحركة ولا للسكون، فهذا لا يوافق مذهبه كما مر في مباحث الاعتماد في مباحث الاعتماد مع أنه غير شامل للحركات الإرادية الصادرة عن الحيوانات.
And al-Jubba'i said: There is no doubt that the acquired inclination (al-iʿtimād al-mujtalab) in the stone overcomes the necessary inclination (al-iʿtimād al-lāzim) because the newly occurring is stronger than the remaining, so the stone ascends. Then the acquired inclination in the stone weakens due to the friction of the pierced air, gradually decreasing in strength until the necessary overcomes the acquired, and the stone descends. And there is no doubt that its overcoming of the acquired only occurs after equilibrium between them, since the overcome does not change from being overcome to being overcoming all at once without the interpolation of equilibrium. And at equilibrium, rest is necessary, otherwise there would be preponderance without a preponderator, for if it did not rest, it would be moving, either by the necessary inclination or by the acquired inclination, while they are equal, and that would be sheer arbitrariness. And the answer to it is that al-Jubba'i does not hold that inclination generates motion or rest, so this does not agree with his school, as has passed in the discussions on inclination. Moreover, it is not inclusive of voluntary motions emanating from animals.
Source pages p. 269

Commentary
This passage presents al-Jubba'i's (al-Jubbā'ī) view on the motion of a stone thrown upward. He argues that the acquired inclination (al-iʿtimād al-mujtalab) initially overcomes the natural necessary inclination (al-iʿtimād al-lāzim), causing ascent. As the acquired inclination weakens due to air resistance, an equilibrium point is reached, after which the natural inclination prevails and the stone descends. At the point of equilibrium, rest must occur, otherwise there would be preponderance without a preponderator, leading to an arbitrary conclusion. The answer given is that al-Jubba'i does not actually hold that inclination generates motion or rest, so this argument does not align with his own doctrine. Additionally, it does not cover voluntary motions of animals.

Key Terms
الاعتماد المجتلب
الاعتماد اللازم
تعادل
ترجيح بلا مرجح
سكون

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Al-Jubba'i's argument is a valid proof that rest must occur at equilibrium.
Correction: The argument is rejected because al-Jubba'i himself does not believe that inclination generates motion or rest, making his reasoning self-contradictory. Also, it does not apply to voluntary motions.

Checkpoint
Why is al-Jubba'i's argument for rest at equilibrium rejected according to the passage?
A) Because equilibrium never occurs.
B) Because he does not believe that inclination generates motion or rest.
C) Because the stone always moves continuously.
D) Because the argument only applies to voluntary motions.

Key Takeaway
Al-Jubba'i's argument for rest at equilibrium is logically coherent but fails because it contradicts his own doctrine that inclination does not generate motion or rest.

Deniers of Intervening Rest and the Mustard Seed Argument
Philosophers' argument for intervening rest via mustard seed example.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the philosophers' argument for the possibility of intervening rest using the mustard seed and mountain example, and how it refutes the deniers.

Core Idea
Equilibrium between opposing motions necessitates a moment of rest.

The philosophers defend the possibility of rest intervening between two opposite motions. They use a vivid thought experiment: a tiny mustard seed ascending and a mountain descending meet in mid-air. At the moment of contact, the two forces are equal, forcing the seed to pause before descending. This lesson unpacks that argument and the concept of 'second existence' at equilibrium.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage carefully
Read the Arabic passage once without trying to analyse it. Then read the English translation. Let the author's own rhythm come through before you interpret.
Notice the parenthetical comment from the original author.
2
Understand the thought experiment
Visualize the mustard seed ascending and the mountain descending. They meet in mid-air. What must happen next according to the philosophers?
3
Grasp the concept of equilibrium and necessary rest
Identify the key principle: when two opposing inclinations are balanced, rest occurs. This is explained as a 'second existence' in the first place.
This is the core logical move.
4
Consider the objection and its dismissal
The passage anticipates an objection: maybe equilibrium is only an instant, so no time for rest. Find how the author dismisses this objection as invalid.
The dismissal is brief but important.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وأما المنكرون لتخلل السكون بين المستقيمتين فلكل من الفريقين أيضاً في إنكاره طريق فقال الحكماء : إن صح وجود السكون بينهما فإذا فرض أنه صعد الخردلة وهبط الجبل وتلاقيا في الجو بحيث يماس سطحها سطحه فلا شك أنه تنزل الحردلة راجعة وحينئذ وجب وقوف الخردلة لتوسط قوله : ( وعند التعادل بجب السكون وهو كون ثان في مكان أول، لأن اعتماد المجتلب حال غلبته أحدث له كوناً في مكان حصل فيه تعادل الاعتمادين، فحصل في ذلك الزمان كون ثان لعدم الترجيح بلا مرجح فما قيل : لوسلم التعادل فهو حاصل في أن الوصول، فلا يكون زمان سكون بين الحركتين ليس بشيء.
As for the deniers of the interpolation of rest between two rectilinear motions, each of the two groups also has a way in its denial. So the philosophers said: If the existence of rest between them is correct, then if it is assumed that a mustard seed ascends and a mountain descends, and they meet in the air such that its surface touches its surface, there is no doubt that the mustard seed descends returning, and then the stopping of the mustard seed becomes necessary due to intermediation. His saying: (And at equilibrium, rest is necessary, which is a second existence in a first place, because the acquired inclination at the time of its overcoming generated for it an existence in a place where the equilibrium of the two inclinations occurred. So there occurred in that time a second existence due to the absence of preponderance without a preponderator. So what is said: 'If equilibrium is granted, it occurs in the instant of reaching, so there is no time of rest between the two motions' is nothing.)
Source pages p. 269

Commentary
This passage discusses the deniers of intervening rest between two rectilinear motions. The philosophers present a thought experiment: if a tiny mustard seed ascends and a mountain descends and they meet in mid-air, the mustard seed must then descend. At the moment of contact, there is a necessary moment of rest for the mustard seed due to equilibrium. The author (in the parenthetical comment) explains that at equilibrium, rest is a second being (kawn thānin) in a first place. This is because the acquired inclination at its moment of overcoming caused a location where the two inclinations become equal, resulting in a second being due to lack of a preponderator. The objection that equilibrium occurs at the instant of reaching (so no time of rest) is dismissed as invalid.

Key Terms
منكرون
تخلل السكون
الخردلة
الجبل
تعادل
كون ثان

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Equilibrium occurs only at an instant, so no time for rest.
Correction: The passage argues that equilibrium requires a duration of rest because a 'second existence' occurs in the place.

Checkpoint
What happens to the mustard seed when it meets the descending mountain according to the philosophers?
A) It continues upward.
B) It stops descending.
C) It must stop and then descend.
D) It passes through the mountain.

Key Takeaway
The philosophers use the mustard seed example to show that intervening rest is necessary when opposing forces reach equilibrium.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
When you find yourself torn between two equally compelling choices or desires, do you force a decision immediately or allow yourself a pause? What makes you resist the necessary rest that equilibrium demands?
?
The philosophers argued that a moment of rest is inevitable when opposing forces meet. Consider a time when you experienced a 'standstill' in your life—where you felt pulled in opposite directions. Did you see that rest as a failure or as a necessary transition? How might embracing that rest change your outlook?
?
The passage dismisses the objection that equilibrium is only an instant. In your life, when you feel balanced, do you dismiss that state as fleeting or do you recognize it as a moment of weighty decision? What would it mean to honor that equilibrium as a real, restful pause before action?

Causality and Created Things
intermediate

Al-Jubba'i's Argument for Rest at Equilibrium and Its Refutation
Al-Jubba'i's argument for rest at equilibrium and its refutation.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand Al-Jubba'i's argument for necessary rest at equilibrium in projectile motion and the criticism that it contradicts his own principles and fails to explain voluntary motion.

Core Idea
Equilibrium between inclinations would force rest, but Al-Jubba'i's own theory undermines this necessity.

This passage presents a physical argument by the Mu'tazilite theologian Al-Jubba'i about projectile motion and a sharp refutation. Al-Jubba'i claims that when a stone is thrown upward, the acquired and inherent inclinations eventually balance, necessitating a moment of rest. The response, however, points out that this argument does not cohere with Al-Jubba'i's own denial of inclination generating motion or rest, and it fails to explain voluntary motions. Let's examine his reasoning and the critique.

Guided Path
1
Read Al-Jubba'i's argument
Read the Arabic passage focusing on Al-Jubba'i's description of the stone's ascent and descent, and the concept of equilibrium.
Notice the gradual weakening due to air resistance.
2
Identify the key claim
Al-Jubba'i asserts that rest must occur at equilibrium to avoid unjustified preference (tarjīḥ bilā murjīḥ). Understand why motion without a preferrer is impossible in his framework.
3
Examine the refutation
The answer criticizes that Al-Jubba'i does not believe inclination generates motion or rest; this contradicts his own school. Also note the limitation to voluntary motions.
This refutation references earlier discussions on inclination (iʿtimād).
4
Reflect on the implications
Consider how this debate fits into wider kalām discussions on causality and the nature of motion. What does it show about the method of theological argument?

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وقال الجبائي : لا شك أن الاعتماد المجتلب في الحجر يغلب الاعتماد اللازم إذا الحادث أقوى من الباقي فيصعد الاعتماد المجتلب في الحجر، ويضعف بمصاكات الهواء المخروق متدرجاً في الضعف إلى أن يغلب اللازم المجتلب فينزل الحجر ولا شك أن غلبته على المجتلب إنما تكون بعد التعادل بينهما إذ لا ينقلب المغلوب من المغلوبية إلى الغالبية دفعة من غير تخلل تعادل وعند التعادل يجب السكون وإلا لزم الترجيح بلا مرجح إذ لو لم يسكن لكان متحركاً، إما بالاعتماد اللازم أو بالاعتماد المجتلب مع تعادلهما وتساويهما فيكون تحكماً محضاً ، والجواب عنه أن الجبائي ليس قائلاً بتولد الاعتماد للحركة ولا للسكون، فهذا لا يوافق مذهبه كما مر في مباحث الاعتماد مع أنه غير شامل للحركات الإرادية الصادرة عن الحيوانات
Al-Jubba'i said: There is no doubt that the acquired inclination in the stone overcomes the inherent inclination because the new [impression] is stronger than the persistent one, so the acquired inclination in the stone ascends, and weakens through collisions with the split air, gradually decreasing until the inherent [inclination] overcomes the acquired, so the stone descends. And there is no doubt that its overcoming the acquired only occurs after equilibrium between them, because the vanquished does not turn from being vanquished to being victorious at once without an intervening equilibrium. And at equilibrium, rest is necessary, otherwise an unjustified preference would follow, because if it did not rest, it would be moving, either by the inherent inclination or by the acquired inclination while they are equal and balanced, which would be pure arbitrariness. The answer to him is that Al-Jubba'i does not hold that inclination generates motion or rest; this does not agree with his school, as was mentioned in the investigations on inclination, and moreover it does not encompass voluntary motions emanating from animals.
Source pages p. 269

Commentary
This passage presents Al-Jubba'i's account of projectile motion and a subsequent refutation. Al-Jubba'i argues that when a stone is thrown upward, the acquired inclination (al-iʿtimād al-mujtalab) initially overcomes the inherent inclination (al-iʿtimād al-lāzim) because a new impression is stronger. The stone rises as the acquired inclination dominates. This acquired inclination then weakens gradually due to air resistance, until the inherent inclination overcomes it, causing the stone to descend. Al-Jubba'i asserts that the transition from being overcome (by the acquired) to overcoming (by the inherent) cannot happen instantly; there must be an intermediate state of equilibrium (taʿādul) between the two inclinations. At the point of equilibrium, the stone must come to rest (sukūn). If it did not rest, it would be moving either by the inherent or the acquired inclination, but since both are equal, motion would require a preference without a sufficient reason (tarjīḥ bilā murjīḥ), which is impossible. Thus rest is necessary. The response to Al-Jubba'i is that he himself does not believe that inclination generates motion or rest; this argument contradicts his own school's position as previously established in the discussions on inclination. Moreover, his theory does not apply to voluntary motions of animals, which are not caused by such inclinations.

اعتماد لازم
تعادل

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: One might think Al-Jubba'i's argument is internally consistent and logically compelling.
Correction: The response shows that the argument contradicts Al-Jubba'i's own school's position on inclination generating motion or rest, and it fails to account for voluntary motion.

Checkpoint
According to Al-Jubba'i, what necessarily occurs when the acquired and inherent inclinations become equal?
A) The stone continues ascending
B) The stone descends
C) The stone rests
D) The stone moves randomly

Key Takeaway
Al-Jubba'i's argument for rest at equilibrium is self-defeating because it relies on a premise he rejects—that inclination produces motion—and it does not cover voluntary motions.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
In the moments of 'equilibrium' in your life—when opposing desires or pressures seem balanced—do you tend to assume rest is natural? This lesson shows that even rest requires a cause. Who or what do you credit for your moments of stability? Will you consciously acknowledge the Giver of all causes today?
?
Al-Jubba'i's argument contradicted his own principles. Have you ever held a belief that, upon deeper examination, contradicts another conviction you hold? This lesson calls you to seek internal consistency in your faith. Will you examine one area of potential contradiction in your own understanding of God's attributes?
?
The refutation points out that voluntary motion does not follow physical inclinations. Your choices are not mere physics; they are acts of will accountable to God. When you act, do you consider that your will is a gift that directly reflects your submission or rebellion? How will you align your next voluntary action with what you have learned about the limits of natural causes?
